The perceived stupidity of the dodo, a medium-sized flightless bird that was native to [[Mauritius]], is due to naivety and passivity from living in isolation without significant predators.<noinclude><ref>{{Cite web |title=Dodos might have been quite intelligent, new research finds |url=https://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2016/02/160223143148.htm |access-date=2025-01-04 |website=ScienceDaily |language=en}}</ref></noinclude>
* Many believe that the dodo was hunted to extinction by European settlers due to its high culinary value. However, the dodo's meat was stated to be inedible by historical accounts, as one of its early names given by the Dutch was ''Walghvoghel (Tasteless bird).'' The dodo's decline was caused more by predation of their eggs from [[invasive species]] as opposed to direct predation from humans like the [[Passenger pigeon#Relationship with humans|passenger pigeon]] or [[Woolly mammoth#Extinction|woolly mammoth]].''<noinclude><ref>{{Cite book |last=Parish |first=Jolyon C. |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=bp8wK8zCg7wC |title=The Dodo and the Solitaire: A Natural History |date=2013 |publisher=Indiana University Press |isbn=978-0-253-00099-6 |language=en}}</ref></noinclude>''
* Sixty common starlings were released in 1890 into New York's [[Central Park]] by [[Eugene Schieffelin]], but there is no evidence that he was trying to introduce every bird species mentioned in the works of [[William Shakespeare]] into North America. This claim has been traced to an essay in 1948 by naturalist [[Edwin Way Teale]], whose notes appear to indicate that it was speculation.<noinclude><ref name="Fugate2021">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Fugate |first1=Lauren |last2=Miller |first2=John MacNeill |title=Shakespeare's Starlings: Literary History and the Fictions of Invasiveness |journal=Environmental Humanities |date=November 1, 2021 |volume=13 |issue=2 |pages=301–322 |doi=10.1215/22011919-9320167 |s2cid=243468840 |url=https://read.dukeupress.edu/environmental-humanities/article/13/2/301/234995/Shakespeare-s-StarlingsLiterary-History-and-the |access-date=November 26, 2021 |issn=2201-1919|doi-access=free }}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last1=Strycker|first1=Noah|title=The Thing with Feathers|date=2014|publisher=Penguin Group|isbn=978-1-59448-635-7|page=42}}</ref></noinclude>

==== Other vertebrates ====

* Contrary to the allegorical story about the [[boiling frog]], frogs die immediately when cast into boiling water, rather than leaping out; furthermore, frogs will attempt to escape cold water that is slowly heated before reaching their [[critical thermal maximum]].<noinclude><ref name="fastcompany">{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.fastcompany.com/26455/next-time-what-say-we-boil-consultant| title= Next Time, What Say We Boil a Consultant| access-date=February 29, 2016 |date=October 1995|work=Fast Company Issue 01 }}</ref></noinclude>
* The memory span of goldfish is much longer than just a few seconds. It is up to a few months long.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Hipsley|first=Anna|url=http://www.abc.net.au/news/stories/2008/02/19/2166204.htm|title=Goldfish three-second memory myth busted (Australian Broadcasting Corporation)|publisher=ABC |location=Australia |date=February 19, 2008|access-date=August 29, 2009 | archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20110625100621/http://www.abc.net.au/news/stories/2008/02/19/2166204.htm | archive-date = June 25, 2011| url-status=live}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://dsc.discovery.com/fansites/mythbusters/episode/episode-tab-07.html |title=Sinking Titanic: Goldfish Memory |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110225120856/http://dsc.discovery.com/fansites/mythbusters/episode/episode-tab-07.html |archive-date=February 25, 2011 }}. [[MythBusters (2004 season)#Episode 12 – "Sinking Titanic, Goldfish Memory, Trombone Explosion"|2004 season, Episode 12]]. ''[[MythBusters]]''. Discovery.com. February 22, 2004.</ref></noinclude>
* [[Shark#In culture|Sharks can get cancer]]. The misconception that sharks do not get cancer was spread by the 1992 book ''[[Sharks Don't Get Cancer]]'', which was used to sell extracts of shark [[cartilage]] as cancer prevention treatments. Reports of [[carcinoma]]s in sharks exist, and current data does not support any conclusions about the incidence of tumors in sharks.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Ostrander |first1=G. K. |last2=Cheng |first2=KC |last3=Wolf |first3=JC |last4=Wolfe |first4=MJ |title=Shark Cartilage, Cancer and the Growing Threat of Pseudoscience |journal=Cancer Research |volume=64 |issue=23 |pages=8485–91 |date=2004 |pmid=15574750 |doi=10.1158/0008-5472.CAN-04-2260|doi-access=free }}</ref></noinclude>
* Great white sharks do not mistake human divers for seals or other [[pinniped]]s. When attacking pinnipeds, the shark surfaces quickly and attacks violently. In contrast, attacks on humans are slower and less violent: the shark charges at a normal pace, bites, and swims off. Great white sharks have efficient eyesight and [[color vision]]; the bite is not predatory, but rather for identification of an unfamiliar object.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://news.nationalgeographic.com/news/2004/01/0123_040123_tvgreatwhiteshark.html|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20040125025359/http://news.nationalgeographic.com/news/2004/01/0123_040123_tvgreatwhiteshark.html|archive-date=January 25, 2004|title=Great White Shark Attacks: Defanging the Myths|work=nationalgeographic.com |author=Jennifer Hile |date=January 23, 2004}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Snake#Feeding and diet|Snake jaws cannot unhinge]]. The posterior end of the lower jaw bones contains a quadrate bone, allowing jaw extension. The anterior tips of the lower jaw bones are joined by a flexible ligament allowing them to bow outwards, increasing the mouth gape.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://daily.jstor.org/how-snakes-swallow/|first=James|last=MacDonald|date=June 26, 2019|title=How Snakes Swallow|newspaper=JSTOR Daily}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.livescience.com/32096-how-do-snakes-swallow-large-animals.html|first=Corey|last=Binns|date=September 11, 2012|title=How Do Snakes Swallow Large Animals?|website=[[Live Science]] }}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Pacific tree frog]] and the [[Baja California chorus frog]] are some of the only frog species that make a "ribbit" sound. The misconception that all frogs, or at least all those found in North America, make this sound comes from its extensive use in Hollywood films.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Brulliard|first=Karin|url=http://www.beachwatchers.wsu.edu/island/essays/TreeFrogs.htm |title=Why French pigs say groin, Japanese bees say boon and American frogs say ribbit |newspaper=Washington Post |date=October 14, 2016 |access-date=June 25, 2022 |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190427154344/https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/animalia/wp/2016/10/12/why-french-pigs-say-groin-japanese-bees-say-boon-and-american-frogs-say-ribbit/ |archive-date=April 27, 2019 |quote=Only American frogs are said to go "ribbit," and that's believed to be because early Hollywood producers used the ribbiting sound of the Pacific tree frog during night scenes.}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://fpdcc.com/have-you-heard-the-calls-from-cook-countys-12-frog-and-toad-species/|title=Have You Heard the Calls from Cook County's 12 Frog and Toad Species?|website=Forest Preserves of Cook County|date=May 25, 2022|access-date=June 25, 2022}}</ref></noinclude>
* There is no credible evidence that the [[Candiru (fish)|candiru]], a South American parasitic catfish, can swim up a human urethra if one urinates in the water in which it lives. The sole documented case of such an incident, written in 1997, has been heavily criticized upon peer review, and this phenomenon is now largely considered a myth.<noinclude><ref name="Gudger">{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Gudger |first=E.W. |date=January 1930 |title=On the alleged penetration of the human urethra by an Amazonian catfish called candiru with a review of the allied habits of other members of the family pygidiidae |journal=The American Journal of Surgery |type=Print |publisher=Elsevier Inc. |volume=8 |issue=1 |pages=170–188 |doi=10.1016/S0002-9610(30)90912-9 |issn=0002-9610}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Pacu]]s, South American fish related to [[piranha]]s, do not attack or feed on human testicles. This myth originated from a misinterpreted joke in a 2013 report of a pacu being found in [[Øresund]], the strait between Sweden and Denmark, which claimed that the fish ate "nuts".<noinclude><ref name="CNN">{{#invoke:cite web||author=Gray |first=Melissa |date=August 16, 2013 |title=Warning over testicle-biting fish in Denmark? It's all wet |url=http://www.cnn.com/2013/08/10/world/europe/scandanavia-swim-warning |access-date=September 8, 2013 |website=[[CNN]]}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Than |first=Ker |date=August 13, 2013 |title=Fears of 'Testicle-Eating' Fish Overblown |url=https://www.nationalgeographic.com/animals/article/130813-pacu-piranha-testicle-biting-fish-invasive-species |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20211104112851/https://www.nationalgeographic.com/animals/article/130813-pacu-piranha-testicle-biting-fish-invasive-species |archive-date=November 4, 2021 |access-date=June 13, 2022 |website=[[National Geographic]] |language=en}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Piranha]]s do not eat only meat but are omnivorous, and they only swim in schools to defend themselves from predators and not to attack. They very rarely attack humans, only when under stress and feeling threatened, and even then, bites typically only occur on hands and feet.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=April 7, 2020|title=Killer Piranhas: Myth or Fact?|url=https://explorersweb.com/2020/04/07/killer-piranhas-myth-or-fact/|access-date=June 26, 2021|website=Explorersweb}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Chameleon#Change of color|The skin of a chameleon is not adapted solely for camouflage purposes]],<noinclude><ref name="Stuart-Fox 2008">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Stuart-Fox |first1=Devi |last2=Moussalli |first2=Adnan |date=January 2008 |title=Selection for Social Signalling Drives the Evolution of Chameleon Colour Change |journal=PLOS Biology |volume=6 |issue=1 |pages=e25 |doi=10.1371/journal.pbio.0060025 |doi-access=free |issn=1544-9173 |pmc=2214820 |pmid=18232740}}</ref></noinclude> nor can a chameleon change its skin color to match any background.<noinclude><ref name="Clark 2015">{{cite magazine |last1=Clark |first1=Laura |title=We Finally Know How Chameleons Change Their Color |url=https://www.smithsonianmag.com/smart-news/we-finally-know-how-chameleons-change-their-color-180954527/ |access-date=29 July 2024 |magazine=Smithsonian Magazine |date=11 March 2015}}</ref></noinclude> Chameleons usually change color for social signaling, based on their mood, and for heat regulation.<noinclude><ref name="Clark 2015" /><ref>{{cite magazine |last1=Bates |first1=Mary |title=How Do Chameleons Change Colors? |url=https://www.wired.com/2014/04/how-do-chameleons-change-colors/ |magazine=Wired}} [[Wired Magazine]]. April 11, 2014.</ref></noinclude> The use in social signaling may be to display bright colors for only brief periods of time to avoid increased visibility to predators.<noinclude><ref name="Stuart-Fox 2008" /></noinclude>

==== Invertebrates ====
* Not all earthworms become two worms when cut in half. Only a limited number of earthworm species<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||doi=10.1002/jez.1401170102|title=Simultaneous anterior and posterior regeneration and other growth phenomena in Maldanid polychaetes|date=1942|last1=Moment|first1=Gairdner B.|journal=Journal of Experimental Zoology|volume=117|pages=1–13}}</ref></noinclude> are capable of anterior [[Earthworm#Regeneration|regeneration]].
* Houseflies have an average lifespan of 20 to 30 ''days'', not 24 ''hours''.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||title=The Housefly |url=http://www.newton.dep.anl.gov/natbltn/400-499/nb453.htm |newspaper=Forest Preserve District of Cook County (Illinois) |date=April 15, 1972 |access-date=January 16, 2011 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130301174011/http://www.newton.dep.anl.gov/natbltn/400-499/nb453.htm |archive-date=March 1, 2013 }}</ref></noinclude> However, members of [[Dolania|one species]] of mayfly have an adult lifespan of as little as 5 minutes.<noinclude><ref name="Sweeney">{{#invoke:cite journal||author1=Sweeney, Bernard W. |author2=Vannote, Robin L. |year=1982 |title=Population Synchrony in Mayflies: A Predator Satiation Hypothesis |journal=Evolution |volume=36 |issue=4 |pages=810–22 |doi=10.2307/2407894 |jstor=2407894 |pmid=28568232 }}</ref></noinclude>
* The daddy longlegs spider (''[[Pholcidae]]'') is not the most venomous spider in the world. Their fangs are capable of piercing human skin, but the tiny amount of venom they carry causes only a mild burning sensation for a few seconds.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://dsc.discovery.com/fansites/mythbusters/episode/episode-tab-07.html |title=Buried in Concrete: Daddy Long Legs |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110225120856/http://dsc.discovery.com/fansites/mythbusters/episode/episode-tab-07.html |archive-date=February 25, 2011 }}. [[MythBusters (2004 season)#Episode 13 – "Buried in Concrete, Daddy Long-legs, Jet Taxi"|(2004 Season, Episode 13)]]. ''[[MythBusters]]''. Discovery Channel. February 25, 2004.</ref></noinclude> Other species such as [[harvestmen]] and [[Tipuloidea|crane flies]] are also called ''daddy longlegs'', and share the misconception of being highly venomous but unable to pierce the skin of humans.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=UCR Spider Site – Daddy Long Legs Myth |url=http://spiders.ucr.edu/daddylonglegs.html |publisher=[[University of California Riverside]] |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131104173738/http://spiders.ucr.edu/daddylonglegs.html |archive-date=November 4, 2013 |access-date=September 18, 2008 }}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Spider Myths – If it could only bite |url=http://www.washington.edu/burkemuseum/spidermyth/myths/daddyvenom.html |date=2003 |publisher=Burke Museum of Natural History & Culture, University of Washington |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20070714061900/http://www.washington.edu/burkemuseum/spidermyth/myths/daddyvenom.html |archive-date=July 14, 2007 |access-date=July 22, 2008 }}</ref></noinclude>
* People do not swallow large numbers of spiders during sleep. A sleeping person makes noises that warn spiders of danger.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Myth: You unknowingly swallow an average of four live spiders in your sleep each year.|url=https://www.burkemuseum.org/collections-and-research/biology/arachnology-and-entomology/spider-myths/myth-you-swallow-spiders|publisher=Burke Museum|access-date=February 5, 2020|date=2010}}</ref><ref>{{cite magazine |last1=Sneed |first1=Annie |title=Fact or Fiction? People Swallow 8 Spiders a Year While They Sleep |url=http://www.scientificamerican.com/article/fact-or-fiction-people-swallow-8-spiders-a-year-while-they-sleep1/ |magazine=Scientific American |access-date=June 7, 2015 |date=April 15, 2014}}</ref></noinclude> Most people also wake up from sleep when they have a spider on their face.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last1=Burchell |first1=Helen |title=Do people really swallow spiders in their sleep? |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/uk-england-beds-bucks-herts-66319172 |access-date=2023-08-06 |work=[[BBC News]] |date=2023-08-06 |location=[[Hertfordshire]] |language=en-gb}}</ref></noinclude><!--featured here: [[Cultural depictions of spiders#Modern myths and urban legends]]-->
[[File:Mantis Tenodera aridifolia01.jpg|thumb|A female [[Chinese mantis]] simultaneously [[Sexual cannibalism|copulating with and cannibalizing]] her mate; this does not occur every time mantises mate.]]
* Female praying mantises do not always [[Sexual cannibalism|eat the males during mating]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://entomologytoday.org/2013/12/22/do-female-praying-mantises-always-eat-the-males/|title=Do Female Praying Mantises Always Eat the Males?|website=EntomologyToday|date=December 22, 2013}}</ref></noinclude>
[[File:AD2009Aug08 Bombus pratorum.jpg|thumb|Aerodynamic theory does not predict that [[Bumblebees can't fly|bumblebees should be incapable of flight]].]]
* It is not true that aerodynamic theory predicts [[Bumblebees can't fly|that bumblebees should not be able to fly]]; the physics of insect flight is quite well understood. The misconception appears to come from a calculation based on a fixed-wing aircraft mentioned in a 1934 book, and was further popularized in the 2007 film ''[[Bee Movie]]''.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Chatfield |first=Matthew |date=January 4, 2008 |title=Some scientist once proved that bees can't fly...? |url=http://www.naturenet.net/blogs/2008/01/some-scientist-once-proved-that-bees-cant-fly}}. ''naturenet.net''. The Ranger's Blog.
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||author=Ivars Peterson |date=September 13, 2004 |title=Flight of the Bumblebee |url=https://www.sciencenews.org/article/flight-bumblebee |access-date=November 18, 2011 |work=Ivars Peterson's MathTrek |publisher=[[Mathematical Association of America]]}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Dickinson|first1=Michael|date=June 2001|title=Solving the Mystery of Insect Flight|journal=Scientific American|volume=284|issue=6|pages=48–50, 53–57|doi=10.1038/scientificamerican0601-48 |jstor=26059248|pmid=11396342 |bibcode=2001SciAm.284f..48D |quote=a 1934 book by entomologist Antoine Magnan... refers to a calculation by his assistant André Sainte-Laguë, who was an engineer. The conclusion was presumably based on the fact that the maximum possible lift produced by aircraft wings as small as a bumblebee's wings and traveling as slowly as a bee in flight would be much less than the weight of a bee.}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=December 27, 2017 |author=Lindsay Dodgson |title=This myth about bees is scientifically incorrect — here's why |url=https://www.businessinsider.com/bees-cant-fly-scientifically-incorrect-2017-12 |access-date=2024-01-23 |website=Business Insider |language=en-US}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=2023-05-26 |title=The Strange Myth That Bees Shouldn't Be Able To Fly According To Physics |url=https://www.iflscience.com/the-strange-myth-that-bees-shouldnt-be-able-to-fly-according-to-physics-69107 |access-date=2024-01-23 |website=IFLScience |language=en}}</ref></noinclude>
* While certainly critical to the [[pollination]] of many plant species, [[Western honey bee#Claims of human dependency|European honey bees]] are not essential to human food production, despite claims that [[colony collapse disorder|without their pollination]], humanity would starve or die out "within four years".<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Shilton |first=AC |date=March 2, 2017 |title=What Would Happen if All the Bees Died Tomorrow? |url=https://tonic.vice.com/en_us/article/d7ezaq/what-would-happen-if-all-the-bees-died-tomorrow |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20170706230348/https://tonic.vice.com/en_us/article/d7ezaq/what-would-happen-if-all-the-bees-died-tomorrow |archive-date=July 6, 2017 |access-date=June 16, 2024 |work=[[Vice Media]]}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Palmer |first=Brian |date=May 2, 2022 |title=A World Without Bees? Here's What Happens If Bees Go Extinct |url=https://www.nrdc.org/stories/world-without-bees-heres-what-happens-if-bees-go-extinct |access-date=June 16, 2024 |work=[[Natural Resources Defense Council]]}}</ref></noinclude> In fact, the most essential staple food crops on the planet, like wheat, maize, rice, soybeans and sorghum<noinclude><ref name=FAOstats>{{#invoke:cite web||title=FAOstats Food Supply - Crops Primary Equivalent |url=http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#data/CC |author=[[Food and Agriculture Organization]] of the [[United Nations]], Statistics Division |year=2017 }}</ref><ref>FAO 2015. FAO Statistical Pocketbook 2015, {{ISBN|978-92-5-108802-9}}, p. 28</ref></noinclude> are wind pollinated or self pollinating, and only slightly over 10% of the total human diet of plant crops is dependent upon insect pollination.<noinclude><ref name=FAOstats /></noinclude>
* Bees do not always die if they use their sting. This only happens for a very small minority of species, which includes the honey bee, when they sting mammals, as they have thick skin. They are able to survive when they sting other insects.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last1=Maguire |first1=Sarah |title=Do all bees die after they sting humans? |url=https://jerseybiodiversitycentre.org.je/article/do-all-bees-die-after-they-sting-humans |website=The Jersey Biodiversity Centre |access-date=June 20, 2024 |date=July 9, 2018}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last1=Dorey |first1=James |last2=Gilpin |first2=Amy-Marie |last3=Gloag |first3=Rosalyn |date=April 26, 2024 |title=Most bees don't die after stinging - and other surprising bee facts |url=https://www.uow.edu.au/media/2024/most-bees-dont-die-after-stinging---and-other-surprising-beefacts.php |access-date=June 20, 2024 |work=[[University of Wollongong]]}}</ref><ref name="sciencedirect.com">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=van Loon |first1=Mariëtte H |last2=Dunlosky |first2=John |last3=van Gog |first3=Tamara |last4=van Merriënboer |first4=Jeroen JG |last5=de Bruin |first5=Anique BH |date=April 14, 2015 |title=Refutations in science texts lead to hypercorrection of misconceptions held with high confidence |url=https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0361476X15000235 |journal=[[Contemporary Educational Psychology]] |volume=42 |pages=39, 47 |doi=10.1016/j.cedpsych.2015.04.003 |via=Elsevier Science Direct}}</ref></noinclude>
* Earwigs are not known to purposely climb into external ear canals, though there have been anecdotal reports of earwigs being found in the ear.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Fisher|first=JR|title=Earwig in the ear|journal=Western Journal of Medicine|year=1986|volume=145|issue=2|page=245|pmid=3765607|pmc=1306897}}</ref></noinclude> The name may be a reference to the appearance of their hindwings, which are unique and distinctive among insects, and resemble a human ear when unfolded.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last=Costa|first=J.T.|title=The Other Insect Societies|publisher=Harvard University Press|location=Cambridge, MA|date=2006}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.ento.csiro.au/education/insects/dermaptera.html|title=Dermaptera: earwigs|publisher=[[CSIRO]]|work=Insects and their Allies|access-date=November 16, 2015}}</ref></noinclude>
* Ticks do not jump or fall from trees onto their hosts. Instead, they lie in wait to grasp and climb onto any passing host or otherwise trace down hosts via, for example, [[olfactory]] stimuli, the host's body heat, or [[carbon dioxide]] in the host's breath.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Roberts|first=Catherine|title=4 Common Myths About Ticks Debunked|url=https://www.consumerreports.org/outdoor-safety/tick-myths-debunked/|access-date=June 7, 2021|website=Consumer Reports|date=June 2, 2018 |language=en-US}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.cvbd.org/en/tick-borne-diseases/about-ticks/tick-feeding/host-seeking/ |title=Host seeking |work=CVBD: Companion Vector-Borne Diseases |access-date=8 December 2016}}</ref></noinclude>
* Though they are often called "white ants",<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/termite?show=0&t=1420442739 |work=Merriam-Webster.com |title=Termite |date=August 27, 2023 }}</ref></noinclude> termites are not ants, nor are they closely related to ants. Termites are actually [[Apomorphy and synapomorphy|highly derived]] [[cockroach]]es.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Cleveland|first1=L.R.|last2=Hall|first2=S.K.|last3=Sanders|first3=E.P.|last4=Collier|first4=J.|title=The Wood-Feeding Roach ''Cryptocercus'', its protozoa, and the symbiosis between protozoa and roach|journal=Memoirs of the American Academy of Arts and Sciences|date=1934|volume=17|issue=2|pages=185–382|doi=10.1093/aesa/28.2.216|doi-access=free}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=McKittrick|first1=F.A.|date=1965|title=A contribution to the understanding of cockroach-termite affinities.|journal=Annals of the Entomological Society of America|volume=58|issue=1|pages=18–22|doi=10.1093/aesa/58.1.18|pmid=5834489|doi-access=free}}</ref></noinclude>
* Cockroaches would not be the only [[Chernobyl Exclusion Zone#Radioactive contamination|organisms capable of surviving]] in [[Nuclear fallout effects on an ecosystem|an environment contaminated]] with nuclear fallout. While cockroaches have a much higher [[radiation]] resistance than vertebrates, they are not immune to radiation poisoning, nor are they exceptionally radiation-resistant compared to other insects.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||title=Cockroaches are not radiation-proof and most are not pests |url=http://www.bbc.com/earth/story/20160926-cockroaches-are-not-radiation-proof-and-most-are-not-pests |access-date=1 June 2018 |publisher=BBC Earth}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Riley |first=Daniel |date=2008-07-08 |title=Will cockroaches really be the last survivors on Earth? |url=https://slate.com/news-and-politics/2008/07/will-cockroaches-really-be-the-last-survivors-on-earth.html |access-date=2022-08-28 |website=Slate Magazine |language=en}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[urine therapy|Applying urine]] to jellyfish stings does not relieve pain; indeed, it may make the pain worse.<noinclude><ref name="UAMSHealth">{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://uamshealth.com/medical-myths/does-urine-help-a-jellyfish-sting/|date = 8 March 2019|title= Does Urine Help a Jellyfish Sting?|website = UAMS Health}}</ref><ref name="Curtin 2007">{{#invoke:cite journal||journal=Scientific American|title=Fact or Fiction?: Urinating on a Jellyfish Sting is an Effective Treatment |date=4 January 2007|first=Ciara|last=Curtin|url=http://www.scientificamerican.com/article.cfm?id=fact-or-fiction-urinating}}</ref><ref name="Cleveland Clinic 2024">{{#invoke:cite web||date=20 May 2024 |title=Should You Pee on a Jellyfish Sting? |url=https://health.clevelandclinic.org/pee-jellyfish-sting |access-date=10 July 2024 |website=Cleveland Clinic}}</ref></noinclude> The best immediate treatment for jellyfish stings is to rinse them in salt water.<noinclude><ref name="UAMSHealth" /><ref name="Curtin 2007" /><ref name="Cleveland Clinic 2024" /></noinclude>

==== Plants ====
* [[Carnivorous plant]]s can survive without food. Catching insects, however, supports their growth.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Carnivorous Plants {{!}} Encyclopedia.com|url=https://www.encyclopedia.com/plants-and-animals/plants/plants/carnivorous-plants#:~:text=Carnivorous%20plants%20catch%20mostly%20insects,referred%20to%20as%20insectivorous%20plants.&text=However,%20only%20true%20carnivorous%20plants,can%20survive%20without%20catching%20prey.|access-date=May 25, 2021|website=www.encyclopedia.com|quote=It is known that carnivorous plants can survive without catching prey.}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Euphorbia pulcherrima#Toxicity claims|Poinsettias]] are not highly toxic to humans or cats. While it is true that they are mildly irritating to the skin or stomach,<noinclude><ref name="southern">{{cite encyclopedia |title=Euphorbia |encyclopedia=The Southern Living Garden Book |editor-last=Bender |editor-first=Steve |year=2004 |edition=2nd |isbn=978-0-376-03910-1 |publisher=Oxmoor House |location=Birmingham, AL |page=306}}</ref></noinclude> and may sometimes cause [[diarrhea]] and vomiting if eaten, they rarely cause serious medical problems.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web|| url=http://www.medicinenet.com/script/main/art.asp?articlekey=55606| title= Are Poinsettia Plants Poisonous? Fact or Fiction? |publisher=MedicineNet|access-date=December 21, 2007 | archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20130509062941/http://www.medicinenet.com/script/main/art.asp?articlekey=55606 | archive-date = May 9, 2013| url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal||author=Krenzelok E.P. |author2=Jacobsen T.D. |author3=Aronis J. M. |title=Poinsettia exposures have good outcomes...just as we thought |journal=[[American Journal of Emergency Medicine|Am J Emerg Med]] |volume=14 |issue=7 |pages=671–74 |date=November 1996 |pmid=8906768 |doi=10.1016/S0735-6757(96)90086-8 }}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Ask the Expert: Poison Control > Poinsettia |work=ASPCA |url=http://www.aspca.org/Pet-care/ask-the-expert/ask-the-expert-poison-control/poinsettia.aspx |publisher=[[ASPCA]] |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110110210836/http://www.aspca.org/Pet-care/ask-the-expert/ask-the-expert-poison-control/poinsettia.aspx |archive-date=January 10, 2011 }}</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Helianthus annuus sunflower.jpg|thumbnail|[[Sunflower]]s with the [[Sun]] behind them]]
* Sunflowers do not always point to the Sun. Flowering sunflowers face a fixed direction (often east) all day long, but do not necessarily face the Sun.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Gerard |first=John |year=1597 |title=Herball, or Generall Historie of Plantes |url=http://www.botanicus.org/page/1956748 |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140427083100/http://www.botanicus.org/page/1956748 |archive-date=April 27, 2014 |access-date=January 7, 2021 |publisher=John Norton |pages=612–14}} Popular botany book in 17th century England.
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Hangarter |first=Roger P. |title=Solar tracking: sunflower plants |url=http://plantsinmotion.bio.indiana.edu/plantmotion/movements/tropism/solartrack/solartrack.html |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131019131750/http://plantsinmotion.bio.indiana.edu/plantmotion/movements/tropism/solartrack/solartrack.html |archive-date=October 19, 2013 |access-date=August 22, 2012 |work=Plants-In-Motion website |publisher=Indiana University}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Polikarpov |first1=G.G. |date=1978 |title=Sunflower's blooming floscule is a compass |journal=Nature |volume=272 |issue=5649 |page=122 |bibcode=1978Natur.272..122P |doi=10.1038/272122c0 |doi-access=free}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Lang |first1=A.R.G. |last2=Begg |first2=J.E. |date=1979 |title=Movements of Helianthus annuus Leaves and Heads |url=http://www.flowers-org.com/helianthus-sunflower.html |journal=Journal of Applied Ecology |volume=16 |issue=1 |pages=299–305 |bibcode=1979JApEc..16..299L |doi=10.2307/2402749 |jstor=2402749 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130523191033/http://www.flowers-org.com/helianthus-sunflower.html |archive-date=May 23, 2013 |access-date=December 10, 2016 |quote=Dinural E-W oscillations of the heads occurred initially but ceased as the flowers opened and anthesis commenced, leaving the heads facing east}}</ref></noinclude> However, in an earlier developmental stage, before the appearance of flower heads, the immature buds ''do'' track the [[Sun]] (a phenomenon called [[heliotropism]]). Mature flowers face east.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=When the plant is in the bud stage, it tends to track the movement of the sun across the horizon. Once the flower opens into the radiance of yellow petals, it faces east |url=http://www.sunflowernsa.com/all-about/faq/#11 |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131103083114/http://www.sunflowernsa.com/all-about/faq/ |archive-date=November 3, 2013 |publisher=National Sunflower Association}}</ref></noinclude>
* Mushrooms, molds, and other fungi are not plants, despite [[Fungus#Characteristics|similarities in their morphology and lifestyle]]. The historical classification of fungi as plants is defunct, and although they are still commonly included in botany curricula and textbooks, modern molecular evidence shows that [[Opisthokont|fungi are more closely related to animals than to plants]].<noinclude><ref name="pmid18461162">{{#invoke:cite journal||vauthors=Shalchian-Tabrizi K, Minge MA, Espelund M, etal |date=7 May 2008 |editor1-last=Aramayo |editor1-first=Rodolfo |title=Multigene phylogeny of choanozoa and the origin of animals |journal=PLOS ONE |volume=3 |issue=5 |pages=e2098 |bibcode=2008PLoSO...3.2098S |doi=10.1371/journal.pone.0002098 |pmc=2346548 |pmid=18461162 |doi-access=free}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Fifth-Grade Elementary School Students' Conceptions and Misconceptions about the Fungus Kingdom |url=https://www.researchgate.net/publication/26571382 |access-date=2022-10-05}}</ref></noinclude>

==== Evolution and paleontology ====
{{Further|Introduction to evolution|Objections to evolution}}
* The word ''theory'' in "the theory of evolution" does not imply scientific doubt regarding its validity; the concepts of ''theory'' and ''hypothesis'' have specific meanings in a scientific context. While ''theory'' in colloquial usage may denote a hunch or conjecture, a ''scientific theory'' is a set of principles that explains an ''observable phenomenon'' in natural terms.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.msu.edu/~pennock5/research/papers/Pennock_TeachingEvoNatureSci.pdf|title=Evolutionary Science and Society: Educating a New Generation (TOC)|publisher=MSU.edu|work=Revised Proceedings of the BSCS, AIBS Symposium|date=November 2004|access-date=January 13, 2011|pages=11–12}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://chandra.harvard.edu/chronicle/0308/theo/index.html|title=It Is Not Just a Theory... It Is a Theory!|date=July 7, 2008|publisher=[[Harvard-Smithsonian Center for Astrophysics]]|work=Chandra Chronicles|access-date=April 8, 2009 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131214120846/http://chandra.harvard.edu/chronicle/0308/theo/index.html |archive-date=December 14, 2013|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> "Scientific fact and theory are not categorically separable",<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||title=The Structure of Scientific Revolutions|edition=3rd|author-link=Thomas S. Kuhn|last=Kuhn|first=Thomas S.|date=1996|publisher=University of Chicago Press|location=Chicago|isbn=978-0-226-45808-3|page=7}}</ref></noinclude> and evolution is a theory in the same sense as [[germ theory]] or the theory of [[gravitation]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://evoled.dbs.umt.edu/lessons/miscon.htm#3|title=Misconceptions about the Nature of Science|publisher=[[University of Montana]], Div. Biological Sciences|work=UMT.edu|access-date=April 8, 2009|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20111017233007/http://evoled.dbs.umt.edu/lessons/miscon.htm#3|archive-date=October 17, 2011}}</ref></noinclude>
* The theory of evolution does not attempt to explain the origin of life<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://evolution.berkeley.edu/evolibrary/misconceptions_faq.php#a1 |title=Misconceptions about evolution |publisher=Evolution.berkeley.edu |access-date=February 9, 2012 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131027002124/http://evolution.berkeley.edu/evolibrary/misconceptions_faq.php |archive-date=October 27, 2013|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> or the origin and development of the universe. The theory of evolution deals primarily with changes in successive generations over time after life has already originated.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.talkorigins.org/faqs/faq-misconceptions.html |title=Five Major Misconceptions about Evolution |publisher=TalkOrigins |date=October 1, 2003 |access-date=February 9, 2012 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131003174642/http://www.talkorigins.org/faqs/faq-misconceptions.html |archive-date=October 3, 2013|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> The scientific model concerned with the origin of the first organisms from organic or inorganic molecules is known as [[abiogenesis]], and the prevailing theory for explaining the early development of the universe is the [[Big Bang]] model.
* Evolution is not a [[Great chain of being|progression from inferior to superior organisms]], and it also does not necessarily result in an [[evolution of complexity|increase in complexity]]. Evolution through [[natural selection]] only causes successive generations of a population of organisms to become more [[Fitness (biology)|fit]] for their environment than previous generations.<noinclude><ref name=Michigan>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Evolution and Natural Selection |url=http://www.globalchange.umich.edu/globalchange1/current/lectures/selection/selection.html |publisher=University of Michigan |access-date=November 9, 2016 |date=October 10, 2010}}</ref></noinclude> A population can evolve to become simpler or to have a smaller [[genome]],<noinclude><ref name="pmid11893328">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Moran |first1=Nancy A. |title=Microbial MinimalismGenome Reduction in Bacterial Pathogens |journal=Cell |volume=108 |issue=5 |pages=583–86 |date=2002 |pmid=11893328 |doi=10.1016/S0092-8674(02)00665-7|s2cid=18688744 |doi-access=free }}</ref></noinclude> and [[Atavism|atavistic]] ancestral genetic traits can reappear after having been lost through evolutionary change in previous generations.<noinclude><ref name="Briankhall1">{{Citation |title=Developmental mechanisms underlying the atavisms |author=Brian K. Hall |journal=Biological Reviews |year=1984 |volume=59 |issue=1 |pages=89–124 |doi=10.1111/j.1469-185x.1984.tb00402.x |pmid=6367843 |s2cid=29258934}}</ref></noinclude> Biological [[Devolution (biology)|devolution or de-evolution]] is a [[misnomer]], not only because it implies that organisms can only evolve backward or forward, but also because it implies that evolution may cause organisms to evolve in the "wrong" direction.<noinclude><ref name=Glossary>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.talkorigins.org/faqs/precursors/glossary.html |title=Darwin's precursors and influences: Glossary |access-date=January 18, 2010}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.scientificamerican.com/article.cfm?id=is-the-human-race-evolvin |title=Is the human race evolving or devolving? |date=July 20, 1998 |work=[[Scientific American]] |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20121010233421/http://www.scientificamerican.com/article.cfm?id=is-the-human-race-evolvin |archive-date=October 10, 2012 |url-status=live}} see also [[biological devolution]].</ref></noinclude>
* The phrase "[[survival of the fittest]]" refers to [[Fitness (biology)|biological fitness]], not [[physical fitness]]. Biological fitness is the [[Numerical data|quantitative]] measure of individual [[reproductive success]], e.g. the tendency of lineages containing individuals that produce more offspring [[Ecosystem|in a particular environment]] to persist and thrive in that environment. Further, while the related concepts of "survival of the fittest" and "[[natural selection]]" are often used interchangeably, they are not the same: natural selection is not the only form of selection that determines biological fitness (see [[sexual selection]], [[fecundity selection]], [[Natural selection#By life cycle stage|viability selection]], and [[Artificial Selection|artificial selection]]).<noinclude><ref name="IntroEvolutionaryBiology">{{Citation |last=Colby |first=Chris |title=Introduction to Evolutionary Biology |date=1996–1997 |url=http://www.talkorigins.org/faqs/faq-intro-to-biology.html |publisher=[[TalkOrigins Archive]] |access-date=22 February 2009}}</ref><ref>Hartl, D. L. (1981) ''A Primer of Population Genetics'' {{ISBN|978-0-87893-271-9}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Haldane |first=J. B. S. |author-link=J. B. S. Haldane |date=November 1992 |title=The Cost of Natural Selection |journal=Current Science |volume=63 |issue=9/10 |pages=612–625}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Orthogenesis|Evolution does not "plan" to improve an organism's fitness]] to survive.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://evolution.berkeley.edu/evolibrary/misconceptions_faq.php#b1 |title=Misconceptions about natural selection and adaptation: ''Natural selection involves organisms 'trying' to adapt.'' |work=Misconceptions about evolution |publisher=University of California Museum of Paleontology |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131027002124/http://evolution.berkeley.edu/evolibrary/misconceptions_faq.php |archive-date=October 27, 2013|url-status=live}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://evolution.berkeley.edu/evolibrary/misconceptions_faq.php#b2|title=Misconceptions about natural selection and adaptation: ''Natural selection gives organisms what they 'need.' '' |work=Misconceptions about evolution |publisher=University of California Museum of Paleontology |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131027002124/http://evolution.berkeley.edu/evolibrary/misconceptions_faq.php |archive-date=October 27, 2013|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> This misconception is encouraged as it is common shorthand for [[Teleology in biology|biologists to speak of a purpose]] as a concise form of expression (sometimes called the "metaphor of purpose");<noinclude><ref name="Hanke2004">{{#invoke:cite book||date=2004 |author=Hanke, David |chapter=Teleology: The explanation that bedevils biology |pages=143–55 |title=Explanations: Styles of explanation in science |editor=John Cornwell |place=Oxford & New York |publisher=Oxford University Press |isbn=978-0-19-860778-6 |chapter-url=https://books.google.com/books?id=ZWpq14vS7WQC&pg=PA143}}</ref></noinclude> it is less cumbersome to say "Dinosaurs may have evolved feathers for courtship" than "Feathers may have been selected for when they arose as they gave dinosaurs a selective advantage during courtship over their non-feathered rivals".<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||url=https://www.newscientist.com/article/dn22428-dinosaurs-may-have-evolved-feathers-for-courtship.htmlNew |title=Scientist: "Dinosaurs may have evolved feathers for courtship" |doi=10.1126/science.1225376 |date=October 25, 2012 |access-date=August 18, 2013|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131214120634/http://www.newscientist.com/article/dn22428-dinosaurs-may-have-evolved-feathers-for-courtship.htmlNew |archive-date=December 14, 2013|url-status=live |volume=338 |issue=6106 |journal=Science |pages=510–14 |pmid=23112330|bibcode=2012Sci...338..510Z |author1=Zelenitsky DK |author2=Therrien F |author3=Erickson GM |author4=DeBuhr CL |author5=Kobayashi Y |author6=Eberth DA |author7=Hadfield F|s2cid=2057698 }}</ref></noinclude> However, this can result in many students explaining evolution as an intentional and purposeful process.<noinclude><ref name="Hartelt Martens 2024 pp. 1134–1180">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Hartelt |first1=Tim |last2=Martens |first2=Helge |title=Influence of self-assessment and conditional metaconceptual knowledge on students' self-regulation of intuitive and scientific conceptions of evolution |journal=Journal of Research in Science Teaching |volume=61 |issue=5 |date=2024 |issn=0022-4308 |doi=10.1002/tea.21938 |pages=1134–1180|doi-access=free |bibcode=2024JRScT..61.1134H }}</ref></noinclude>
* Mutations are not entirely random, nor do they occur at the same frequency everywhere in the genome. Certain regions of an organism's genome will be more or less likely to undergo mutation depending on the presence of [[DNA repair]] mechanisms and other [[mutation bias]]es. For instance, in a study on ''[[Arabidopsis thaliana]]'', biologically important regions of the plant's genome were found to be protected from mutations, and beneficial mutations were found to be more likely, i.e. mutation was "non-random in a way that benefits the plant".<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||title=Study challenges evolutionary theory that DNA mutations are random |url=https://phys.org/news/2022-01-evolutionary-theory-dna-mutations-random.html |access-date=February 12, 2022 |work=[[U.C. Davis]] |language=en}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Monroe |first1=J. Grey |last2=Srikant |first2=Thanvi |last3=Carbonell-Bejerano |first3=Pablo |last4=Becker |first4=Claude |last5=Lensink |first5=Mariele |last6=Exposito-Alonso |first6=Moises |last7=Klein |first7=Marie |last8=Hildebrandt |first8=Julia |last9=Neumann |first9=Manuela |last10=Kliebenstein |first10=Daniel |last11=Weng |first11=Mao-Lun |last12=Imbert |first12=Eric |last13=Ågren |first13=Jon |last14=Rutter |first14=Matthew T. |last15=Fenster |first15=Charles B. |last16=Weigel |first16=Detlef |title=Mutation bias reflects natural selection in Arabidopsis thaliana |journal=Nature |date=February 2022 |volume=602 |issue=7895 |pages=101–105 |doi=10.1038/s41586-021-04269-6 |pmid=35022609 |pmc=8810380 |bibcode=2022Natur.602..101M |language=en |issn=1476-4687}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Bundell|first1=Shamini|last2=Thompson|first2=Benjamin|date=January 19, 2022|title=Why mutation is not as random as we thought|url=https://www.nature.com/articles/d41586-022-00142-2|journal=Nature|language=en|doi=10.1038/d41586-022-00142-2|pmid=35046581|s2cid=263483178 }}</ref></noinclude>
* Although [[Cultural depictions of dinosaurs#Public perception of dinosaurs|the word ''dinosaur'' can be used pejoratively]] to describe something that is becoming obsolete due to failing to adapt to changing conditions, non-avian dinosaurs themselves did not go extinct due to an inability to adapt to environmental change [[Timeline of Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event research|as was initially theorized]]. Moreover, not all dinosaurs are extinct (see below).<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Benton |first=M. J. |date=1990 |title=Scientific methodologies in collision: the history of the study of the extinction of the dinosaurs |url=http://palaeo.gly.bris.ac.uk/Essays/Dino90.html |journal=Evolutionary Biology |volume=24 |access-date=2017-01-14 |ref=benton-1990 |archive-date=July 23, 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190723025927/http://palaeo.gly.bris.ac.uk/Essays/Dino90.html }}</ref><ref name="SGL00" /><ref name="MacLeod">{{#invoke:cite journal||author1=MacLeod, N |author2=Rawson, PF |display-authors=etal |year=1997 |title=The Cretaceous–Tertiary biotic transition |journal=Journal of the Geological Society |volume=154 |issue=2 |pages=265–292 |doi=10.1144/gsjgs.154.2.0265|bibcode=1997JGSoc.154..265M |s2cid=129654916 }}</ref><ref name="dictionary2">{{#invoke:cite web||title=dinosaur |url=http://dictionary.reference.com/search?q=dinosaur |access-date=2008-08-06 |work=Dictionary.com |publisher=Lexico Publishing Group, LLC}}</ref></noinclude>
* Birds are [[Theropoda|theropod dinosaurs]], and consequently dinosaurs are not extinct. The word ''dinosaur'' is commonly used to refer only to non-avian dinosaurs, reflecting an outdated conception of the [[Phylogenetic tree|ancestry]] of avian dinosaurs, the birds. [[Origin of birds|The evolutionary origin of birds was an open question in paleontology for over a century]], but the modern scientific consensus is that [[Evolution of birds|birds evolved]] from small [[Origin of avian flight|feathered theropods]] in the [[Jurassic]]. Not all dinosaur lineages were cut short at the end of the [[Cretaceous]] during the [[Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event]], and over 11,000 species of avian theropods survive as part of the modern fauna.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||author=Padian K. |author2=Chiappe L. M. |editor=Currie PJ |editor2=Padian K |title=Encyclopedia of Dinosaurs|date=1997|publisher=Academic Press|location=San Diego|pages=41–96|chapter=Bird Origins}}</ref><ref name="NYT-20160328">{{#invoke:cite news||last=Wilford |first=John Noble |date=March 28, 2016 |title='Dinosaurs Among Us' Retraces an Evolutionary Path |work=[[The New York Times]] |url=https://www.nytimes.com/2016/03/29/science/dinosaurs-birds-evolution-american-museum-of-natural-history.html |access-date=March 28, 2016}}</ref><ref name="Chiappe2009">{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Chiappe |first=Luis M. |year=2009 |title=Downsized Dinosaurs: The Evolutionary Transition to Modern Birds |journal=Evolution: Education and Outreach |volume=2 |issue=2 |pages=248–256 |doi=10.1007/s12052-009-0133-4 |doi-access=free}}</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Ichthyosaur and Plesiosaur 1863.jpg|thumb|An [[ichthyosaur]] and [[plesiosaur]] by [[Édouard Riou]], 1863. This old representation of a plesiosaur lifting its head is not accurate.]]
*  Despite their [[cultural depictions of dinosaurs|cultural depictions]] as "swimming dinosaurs", [[mosasaurs]], [[ichthyosaurs]], [[plesiosaurs]], and [[Marine reptile#Extinct groups|other]] aquatic [[Mesozoic]] [[diapsids]] were not dinosaurs. Mosasaurs were actually lizards, and ichthyosaurs and plesiosaurs were [[Sauropterygia|even more distantly related]] to dinosaurs. [[Semiaquatic|Though some dinosaurs were or are semiaquatic]] (''[[Hesperornis]]'', ''[[Spinosaurus]]'', [[auks]], [[penguins]]), none are known to have been fully marine.<noinclude><ref name="DL90">{{#invoke:cite book||last=Lambert|first=David|title=The Dinosaur Data Book |author2=the Diagram Group|publisher=Avon Books|year=1990|isbn=978-0-380-75896-8|location=New York|pages=[https://archive.org/details/dinosaurdatabook00lamb/page/290 290–301] |url=https://archive.org/details/dinosaurdatabook00lamb/}}</ref><ref name="SGL00">{{#invoke:cite book||last=Lucas |first=Spencer G.|chapter-url=https://archive.org/details/dinosaurstextboo00luca/|title=Dinosaurs: The Textbook |publisher=McGraw-Hill|year=2000|isbn=978-0-07-303642-7|edition=3rd|location=Boston|pages=[https://archive.org/details/dinosaurstextboo00luca/page/247 247–260]|chapter=Dinosaurs in the public eye|author-link=Spencer G. Lucas}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Pterosaurs]] (informally called [[Pterodactyl (disambiguation)|''pterodactyls'']]) are often called "flying dinosaurs" by popular media and the general public, but while pterosaurs were [[Avemetatarsalia|closely related to dinosaurs]], dinosaurs are defined as the descendants of the [[last common ancestor]] of the [[Saurischia]] and the [[Ornithischia]], which excludes the pterosaurs.<noinclude><ref name="MJB04dino">{{#invoke:cite book||last=Benton|first=Michael J. |url=https://archive.org/details/dinosauriandedit00weis|title=The Dinosauria|publisher=University of California Press |year=2004|isbn=978-0-520-24209-8|editor=Weishampel, David B.|edition=2nd|location=Berkeley|pages=[https://archive.org/details/dinosauriandedit00weis/page/n25 7]–19|chapter=Origin and relationships of Dinosauria|author-link=Michael J. Benton|editor2=Dodson, Peter|editor3=Osmólska, Halszka|url-access=limited}}</ref></noinclude>

[[File:Dimetrodon8DB.jpg|thumb|right|''[[Dimetrodon]]'', the iconic [[Neural spine sail|sail-backed]] [[synapsid]], was not a dinosaur, nor did it live at the same time as the dinosaurs.]]
* ''[[Dimetrodon]]'' is often mistakenly called a dinosaur or considered to be a contemporary of dinosaurs in popular culture, but it became extinct some 40 million years before the first appearance of dinosaurs. Being a [[synapsid]], ''Dimetrodon'' is actually more closely related to [[mammals]] than to dinosaurs, birds, lizards, or other [[diapsids]].<noinclude><ref name="AKD09">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Angielczyk|first1=K. D.|year=2009|title=Dimetrodon is Not a Dinosaur: Using Tree Thinking to Understand the Ancient Relatives of Mammals and their Evolution|journal=Evolution: Education and Outreach|volume=2|issue=2|pages=257–271|doi=10.1007/s12052-009-0117-4|doi-access=free}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last1=Black|first1=Riley|title=The Dimetrodon in Your Family Tree|url=https://www.smithsonianmag.com/science-nature/the-dimetrodon-in-your-family-tree-54302176/|access-date=December 5, 2021|website=Smithsonian Magazine|language=en}}</ref></noinclude>
* Humans and [[Bird|avian]] [[dinosaurs]] currently [[Human-dinosaur coexistence|coexist]], but humans and non-avian dinosaurs did not coexist at any point.<noinclude><ref name="science_daily">{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2009/03/090312115133.htm |title=American Adults Flunk Basic Science |work=[[Science Daily]]|date=March 13, 2009 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140402041004/https://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2009/03/090312115133.htm |archive-date=April 2, 2014|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> The last of the non-avian dinosaurs died {{period start|Paleogene}} million years ago in the course of the [[Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event]], whereas the earliest members of the genus ''[[Homo]]'' (humans) evolved between 2.3 and 2.4 million years ago. This places a 63-million-year expanse of time between the last non-avian dinosaurs and the earliest humans. Humans did coexist with [[woolly mammoth]]s and [[saber-toothed cat]]s: extinct mammals often erroneously depicted alongside non-avian dinosaurs.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.nationalgeographic.com.au/history/why-did-the-woolly-mammoth-die-out.aspx|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160529073311/http://www.nationalgeographic.com.au/history/why-did-the-woolly-mammoth-die-out.aspx|archive-date=May 29, 2016|title=Why Did the Woolly Mammoth Die Out?|date=March 26, 2011|website=National Geographic|access-date=August 2, 2016}}</ref></noinclude>
* Fossil fuels do not originate from dinosaur fossils. Petroleum is formed when algae and zooplankton die and sink in [[Dead zone (ecology)|anoxic conditions]] to be buried on the ocean floor without being decomposed by [[Aerobic organism|aerobic bacteria]], and only a tiny amount of the world's deposits of coal contain [[Iguanodon#Bernissart mine discoveries and Dollo's new reconstruction|dinosaur fossils]]; the vast majority of coal is [[Coal#Formation|fossilized plant matter]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last1=Strauss |first1=Bob |date=August 15, 2019 |title=Does Oil Really Come From Dinosaurs? |website=ThoughtCo |url=https://www.thoughtco.com/does-oil-come-from-dinosaurs-1092003 |access-date=September 4, 2019}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=April 21, 2017 |url=https://www.eia.gov/energyexplained/index.cfm?page=coal_home |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20171208115825/https://www.eia.gov/energyexplained/index.cfm?page=coal_home |archive-date=December 8, 2017 |access-date=November 13, 2017 |title=Coal Explained |work=Energy Explained |publisher=[[US Energy Information Administration]]}}</ref></noinclude>
* Mammals did not evolve from any modern group of reptiles; rather, mammals descend from a [[Reptiliomorpha|Reptiliomorph]], "reptile-like," ancestor. The term ''reptile'' is problematic, [[Paraphyly|since its conventional usage unnaturally excludes birds and mammals]], and the modern consensus is that the reptiles are not a [[Monophyly|natural group]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last=Romer |first=A. S. |orig-date=1949 |title=The Vertebrate Body |publisher=W.B. Saunders |year=1970 |edition=4th <!--|pages=-->}}</ref></noinclude> After the first [[Amniote#Adaptation for terrestrial living|fully terrestrial]] [[tetrapod]]s evolved, one of their lineages split into the [[synapsids]] (the line leading to mammals) and the [[diapsids]] (the line leading to crocodiles, birds and other dinosaurs, [[Rhynchocephalia|tuatara]], lizards, and snakes). The synapsids and the diapsids diverged about 320 million years ago, in the mid-[[Carboniferous]] period.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last=Cowen|first=R.|year=2000|title=History of Life |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=qvyBS4gwPF4C&pg=PA154 |publisher=[[Blackwell Science]] |location=Oxford, UK|isbn=978-0-632-04444-3|page=154}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://ucmp.berkeley.edu/taxa/verts/diapsida.php|title=Diapsida|website=ucmp.berkeley.edu}}</ref></noinclude> Only later, in the [[Triassic]], did the modern diapsid groups (the [[Lepidosauria|lepidosaurs]] and the [[archosaur]]s) emerge and diversify.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Jones |first1=Marc EH |last2=Anderson |first2=Cajsa Lisa |last3=Hipsley |first3=Christy A |last4=Müller |first4=Johannes |last5=Evans |first5=Susan E |last6=Schoch |first6=Rainer R |title=Integration of molecules and new fossils supports a Triassic origin for Lepidosauria (lizards, snakes, and tuatara) |journal=BMC Evolutionary Biology |date=September 25, 2013 |volume=13 |issue=1 |page=208 |doi=10.1186/1471-2148-13-208 |pmid=24063680 |pmc=4016551 |bibcode=2013BMCEE..13..208J |doi-access=free }}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||author1=Richard J. Butler |author2=Stephen L. Brusatte |author3=Mike Reich |author4=Sterling J. Nesbitt |author5=Rainer R. Schoch |author6=Jahn J. Hornung |year=2011 |title=The sail-backed reptile ''Ctenosauriscus'' from the latest Early Triassic of Germany and the timing and biogeography of the early archosaur radiation |journal=PLOS ONE |volume=6 |issue=10 |pages=e25693 |doi=10.1371/journal.pone.0025693 |pmid=22022431 |pmc=3194824 |bibcode=2011PLoSO...625693B |doi-access=free }}</ref></noinclude> The mammals themselves are the only survivors of the synapsid line.<noinclude><ref>[[Alfred Romer|Romer, A.S.]] & Parsons, T.S. (1977). ''The Vertebrate Body''. 5th ed. Saunders, Philadelphia. (6th ed. 1985)</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Aegyptopithecus NT.jpg|thumb|''[[Aegyptopithecus]]'', a prehistoric monkey predating the split between [[Catarrhini|apes and other Old World monkeys]] and the division of Old and [[New World monkey]]s, making it more closely related to humans than to New World monkeys.<noinclude><ref name="Seiffert" /></noinclude>]] 

* Humans and other apes are [[Catarrhini|Old World monkeys]]. The word ''monkey'' is often used colloquially to describe only those simians which possess tails, thus excluding [[Barbary macaque|Barbary apes]] and true apes, but this distinction is [[Paraphyly|taxonomically invalid]].<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1= Perez |first1= S.I. |last2= Tejedor |first2= M.F. |display-authors=etal |date= June 2013 |title= Divergence times and the evolutionary radiation of New World monkeys (Platyrrhini, Primates): an analysis of fossil and molecular data |journal= PLOS ONE |volume= 8 |issue= 6 |pages= e68029 |doi= 10.1371/journal.pone.0068029|pmid= 23826358 |pmc= 3694915 |bibcode= 2013PLoSO...868029P |doi-access= free }}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Romer |first=A. S. |orig-date=1949 |title=The Vertebrate Body |publisher=W.B. Saunders |year=1970 |edition=4th}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Terry |first=M. W. |chapter=Use of common and scientific nomenclature to designate laboratory primates |pages=3–4 |editor-first=A. M. |editor-last=Schrier |title=Behavioral Primatology: Advances in Research and Theory |volume=1 |location=Hillsdale, N.J., USA |publisher=Lawrence Erlbaum |year=1977}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite journal||title=Phylogeny of the macaques (Cercopithecidae: ''Macaca'') based on Alu elements |year=2009 |last1=Li |first1=Jing |last2=Han |first2=Kyudong |last3=Xing |first3=Jinchuan |last4=Kim |first4=Heui-Soo |last5=Rogers |first5=Jeffrey |last6=Ryder |first6=Oliver A. |last7=Disotell |first7=Todd |last8=Yue |first8=Bisong |last9=Batzer |first9=Mark A. |journal=Gene |volume=448 |issue=2 |pages=242–249 |pmid=19497354 |pmc=2783879 |doi=10.1016/j.gene.2009.05.013}}
<br />e. {{#invoke:cite book||first1=J. K. |last1=Hodges |first2=J. |last2=Cortes |title=The Barbary macaque: biology, management and conservation |publisher=Nottingham University Press |location=Nottingham, UK |year=2006}}</ref></noinclude> While apes were traditionally thought to be a [[sister group]] to monkeys, modern paleontological and [[Molecular biology|molecular]] evidence shows that apes are deeply nested within the monkey family tree. Old World monkeys like baboons are more closely related to all apes than they are to all [[New World monkey]]s, and extinct Old World monkeys like ''[[Aegyptopithecus]]'' predate the split between apes and [[Old World monkey|all other extant Old World monkeys]].<noinclude><ref name="Seiffert">{{#invoke:cite journal||author=Seiffert Erik R. |title=Revised age estimates for the later Paleogene mammal faunas of Egypt and Oman|journal=Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America |volume=103 |issue=13 |pages=5000–5005 |date=Jan 2006 | doi=10.1073/pnas.0600689103 |pmid=16549773|pmc=1458784|bibcode=2006PNAS..103.5000S|doi-access=free}}</ref><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://anthro.palomar.edu/earlyprimates/early_2.htm|title=Early Primate Evolution: The First Primates|website=anthro.palomar.edu|access-date=August 12, 2017|archive-date=December 25, 2015|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20151225115518/http://anthro.palomar.edu/earlyprimates/early_2.htm}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book|| last = Benton| first = Michael J. | chapter = Chapter 3: Primate evolution | pages=378–380 | title = Vertebrate palaeontology | publisher = Wiley-Blackwell | year = 2005 | isbn = 978-0-632-05637-8 | chapter-url=https://books.google.com/books?id=SyJO3vpCk8AC |access-date=July 10, 2011 }}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Osman Hill|first=W. C.|title=Primates Comparative Anatomy and Taxonomy I—Strepsirhini|publisher=Edinburgh University Press|year=1953|series=Edinburgh Univ Pubs Science & Maths, No 3|page=53|oclc=500576914|author-link=William Charles Osman Hill}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Martin|first=W. C. L.|url=https://archive.org/details/generalintroduct00martrich|title=A General Introduction to the Natural History of Mammiferous Animals, With a Particular View of the Physical History of man, and the More Closely Allied Genera of the Order Quadrumana, or Monkeys|publisher=Wright and Co. printers|year=1841|location=London|pages=340, 361}}
<br />e. {{#invoke:cite journal||last=Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire|first=M. É.|date=1812 |title=Tableau des quadrumanes, ou des animaux composant le premier ordre de la classe des Mammifères|url=https://www.biodiversitylibrary.org/item/23270#page/107/mode/1up|journal=Annales du Muséum d'Histoire Naturelle |location=Paris|volume=19|pages=85–122}}
<br />f. {{#invoke:cite journal||last=Bugge|first=J.|date=1974|title=Chapter 4|journal=Cells Tissues Organs|volume=87|issue=Suppl. 62|pages=32–43|doi=10.1159/000144209|issn=1422-6405}}</ref></noinclude> There is [[Rejection of evolution by religious groups|a concerted social and religious effort to deny evidence which connects humans to their ancestors and fellow extant simians]], but there is no way to [[Monophyly|naturally define]] the monkeys while excluding humans and other apes.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://linnaeus.c18.net/Letters/display_txt.php?id_letter=L0783 |title=Letter, Carl Linnaeus to Johann Georg Gmelin. Uppsala, Sweden, 25 February 1747 |publisher=Swedish Linnaean Society}}</ref></noinclude>
* Humans did not evolve from either of the living species of [[Pan (genus)|chimpanzees]] ([[common chimpanzee]]s and [[bonobo]]s) or any other living species of apes.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Johnson|first1=N. A. |first2=J. J.|last2=Smith |first3=B.|last3=Pobiner |first4=C.|last4=Schrein |title=Why Are Chimps Still Chimps?|journal=The American Biology Teacher|date=Feb 2012|volume=74|issue=2 |pages=74–80|doi=10.1525/abt.2012.74.2.3|jstor=3738744 |s2cid=86832904 }}</ref></noinclude> Humans and chimpanzees did, however, evolve from a [[Chimpanzee–human last common ancestor|common ancestor]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||pages=124–26|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=LavQGJVq5ScC&pg=PA124|title=Tree of Origin: What Primate Behavior Can Tell Us About Human Social Evolution|isbn=978-0-674-01004-8|last1=De Waal|first1=Frans B. M|year=2002| publisher=Harvard University Press }}</ref><ref>William H. Calvin, 2002. "A Brain for All Seasons: Human Evolution and Abrupt Climate Change." University of Chicago Press. Chicago.</ref></noinclude> This [[most recent common ancestor]] of living humans and chimpanzees would have lived between 5 and 8 million years ago.<noinclude><ref name="pbs_evolution_faq">{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.pbs.org/wgbh/evolution/library/faq/cat02.html#Q01|title=Evolution: Frequently Asked Questions|publisher=PBS.org|access-date=August 29, 2009 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131214115407/http://www.pbs.org/wgbh/evolution/library/faq/cat02.html |archive-date=December 14, 2013|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> Extinct [[Hominidae|great apes]] such as ''[[Graecopithecus]]'' and ''[[Sahelanthropus tchadensis]]'' have been proposed as candidates for the chimpanzee–human last common ancestor (CHLCA); however, no fossil has yet conclusively been identified as the CHLCA.<noinclude><ref name="graeco">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Fuss |first1=Jochen |last2=Spassov |first2=Nikolai |last3=Begun |first3=David R. |last4=Böhme |first4=Madelaine |year=2017 |title=Potential hominin affinities of Graecopithecus from the Late Miocene of Europe |journal=PLOS ONE |volume=12 |issue=5 |pages=e0177127 |bibcode=2017PLoSO..1277127F |doi=10.1371/journal.pone.0177127 |pmc=5439669 |pmid=28531170 |doi-access=free}}</ref><ref name="Daver Guy Mackaye Likius 2022">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Daver |first1=G. |last2=Guy |first2=F. |last3=Mackaye |first3=H. T. |last4=Likius |first4=A. |last5=Boisserie |first5=J. -R. |last6=Moussa |first6=A. |last7=Pallas |first7=L. |last8=Vignaud |first8=P. |last9=Clarisse |first9=N. D. |date=2022-08-24 |title=Postcranial evidence of late Miocene hominin bipedalism in Chad |url=https://hal.archives-ouvertes.fr/hal-03037386/file/383956_0_merged_1598881798.pdf |journal=Nature |publisher=Springer Science and Business Media LLC |volume=609 |issue=7925 |pages=94–100 |doi=10.1038/s41586-022-04901-z |issn=0028-0836 |pmid=36002567|bibcode=2022Natur.609...94D |s2cid=234630242 }}</ref></noinclude>
* Humans are animals, despite the fact that the word ''animal'' is colloquially used as an antonym for ''human''.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Thesaurus results for HUMAN |url=https://www.merriam-webster.com/thesaurus/human |access-date=May 21, 2022 |website=www.merriam-webster.com |language=en}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=September 19, 2021 |title=Misconceptions about evolution – Understanding Evolution |url=https://evolution.berkeley.edu/teach-evolution/misconceptions-about-evolution/ |access-date=May 21, 2022 |language=en-US}}</ref></noinclude>
* Ecosystems do not naturally move back towards an equilibrium using [[negative feedback]].<noinclude><ref>{{Cite web |date=2019-07-29 |title=The 'balance of nature' is an enduring concept. But it's wrong, says modern ecological science. |website=[[National Geographic Society]] |url=https://www.nationalgeographic.com/environment/global-warming/balance-of-nature-explained/ |access-date=2024-09-05 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190729040404/https://www.nationalgeographic.com/environment/global-warming/balance-of-nature-explained/ |archive-date=July 29, 2019 }}</ref></noinclude> The concept of an inherent "[[balance of nature]]" has been superseded by [[chaos theory]].<noinclude><ref>{{Cite journal |last1=Wu |first1=Jianguo |last2=Loucks |first2=Orie L. |date=1995 |title=From Balance of Nature to Hierarchical Patch Dynamics: A Paradigm Shift in Ecology |url=https://www.journals.uchicago.edu/doi/10.1086/419172 |journal=The Quarterly Review of Biology |language=en |volume=70 |issue=4 |pages=439–466 |doi=10.1086/419172 |issn=0033-5770}}</ref></noinclude>

=== Chemistry and materials science ===
* [[Himalayan salt]] does not have lower levels of sodium than conventional table salt.<noinclude><ref>{{cite journal | vauthors=Vala DR, Azam MS | title=Salt and Cardiovascular Disease | journal=Indian Journal of Clinical Cardiology | volume=5 | issue=2 | date=2024 | issn=2632-4636 | doi=10.1177/26324636241259583 | pages=160–166| doi-access=free }}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Glass#Reputed_flow|Glass does not flow]] at room temperature as a high-viscosity liquid.<noinclude><ref name="Curtin">{{cite magazine |last=Curtin |first=Ciara |date=February 2007 |title=Fact or Fiction?: Glass Is a (Supercooled) Liquid |url=http://www.scientificamerican.com/article.cfm?id=fact-fiction-glass-liquid |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131214122430/http://www.scientificamerican.com/article.cfm?id=fact-fiction-glass-liquid |archive-date=December 14, 2013 |magazine=[[Scientific American]]}}</ref></noinclude> Although [[Amorphous solid|glass shares some molecular properties with liquids]], it is a solid at room temperature and only begins to flow at [[Glass transition|hundreds of degrees above room temperature]].<noinclude><ref name="Halem">{{#invoke:cite web||author=Halem, Henry|url=http://www.glassnotes.com/WindowPanes.html |title=Does Glass Flow|publisher=Glassnotes.com|date=May 30, 1998 |access-date=August 29, 2009 | archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20131022034746/http://www.glassnotes.com/WindowPanes.html |archive-date = October 22, 2013| url-status=live}}</ref><ref name="NYTglass">{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://www.nytimes.com/2008/07/29/science/29glass.html|work=The New York Times|title=The Nature of Glass Remains Anything but Clear|first=Kenneth|last=Chang |date=July 29, 2008 |access-date=April 4, 2010}}</ref></noinclude> Old glass which is thicker at the bottom than at the top comes from the production process, not from slow flow;<noinclude><ref name="Halem" /><ref name="NYTglass" /></noinclude> no such distortion is observed in other glass objects of similar or even greater age.<noinclude><ref name="Halem" /><noinclude><ref name="NYTglass" /><ref name="Zanotto">{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Zanotto|first= E.D.|date=May 1998 |title=Do cathedral glasses flow? |journal=[[American Journal of Physics]]|volume=66 |issue= 5|page=392|doi=10.1119/1.19026 |bibcode = 1998AmJPh..66..392Z }}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Diamond#Geology|Diamonds]] are not formed from highly compressed coal. Almost all commercially mined diamonds were [[Metamorphism|formed in the conditions of extreme heat and pressure]] about {{convert|150|km|||abbr=|sp=us}} below the earth's surface. Coal is formed from prehistoric plants buried much closer to the surface, and is unlikely to migrate below {{Convert|3.2|km||abbr=|sp=us}} through geological processes. Most diamonds that have been dated are older than the [[Evolutionary history of plants#Colonization of land|first land plants]], and are therefore older than coal.
* Neither "tin" foil nor "tin" cans still use tin as a primary material. Aluminum foil has replaced tin foil in almost all uses since the 20th century; tin cans now primarily use steel or aluminum as their main metal.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||url=https://books.google.com/books?id=4Ikg0vIlGHMC&q=No+cans+currently+in+wide+use+are+made+of+tin&pg=PT61|title=Putting Food By: Fifth Edition|last1=Hertzberg|first1=Ruth|last2=Greene|first2=Janet|last3=Vaughan|first3=Beatrice|date=2010|publisher=Penguin |isbn=978-1-101-53990-3|language=en|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180206163535/https://books.google.com/books?id=4Ikg0vIlGHMC&pg=PT61&dq=No+cans+currently+in+wide+use+are+made+of+tin&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwjCpf2dif3UAhUO0mMKHeszBk4Q6AEITjAI#v=onepage&q=No%20cans%20currently%20in%20wide%20use%20are%20made%20of%20tin&f=false|archive-date=February 6, 2018}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| url=https://www.allrecipes.com/article/what-happened-to-tin-foil/ | title=Why Don't We Use Tin Foil Anymore? }}</ref><ref>https://krcgtv.com/features/beyond-the-trivia/beyond-the-trivia-the-tin-can "Are today's so-called "tin cans" actually made with tin? No... The modern metal can is made of steel or aluminum."</ref></noinclude>
* There is no [[Urine-indicator dye|special compound]] added to the water in swimming pools that will reveal the presence of urine and catch those who urinate in the pool.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| url=https://www.snopes.com/fact-check/piscine-of-the-crime/ | title=FACT CHECK: Does a Urine-Revealing Pool Chemical Exist? | date=December 14, 2000 }}</ref></noinclude>
* Although the core of a wooden pencil is commonly referred to as "lead", wooden pencils do not contain the chemical element lead, nor have they ever contained it; "black lead" was formerly a name of graphite, which is commonly used for pencil leads.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Have pencils ever contained lead?|url=https://www.sciencefocus.com/science/have-pencils-ever-contained-lead/|access-date=2020-08-27|website=BBC Science Focus Magazine|date=March 2019 |language=en}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite news||title = Ever wondered about the lead in pencils?|url = https://www.washingtonpost.com/lifestyle/kidspost/ever-wondered-about-the-lead-in-pencils/2014/11/26/f8b5869c-548a-11e4-809b-8cc0a295c773_story.html|newspaper = The Washington Post|date = 26 November 2014|access-date = 5 October 2015|issn = 0190-8286|first = Howard J.|last = Bennett|url-status = live|archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20151106222853/https://www.washingtonpost.com/lifestyle/kidspost/ever-wondered-about-the-lead-in-pencils/2014/11/26/f8b5869c-548a-11e4-809b-8cc0a295c773_story.html|archive-date = 6 November 2015|df = dmy-all}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||title = Pencil swallowing: MedlinePlus Medical Encyclopedia|url = https://www.nlm.nih.gov/medlineplus/ency/article/002817.htm|website = www.nlm.nih.gov|access-date = 5 October 2015|url-status = live|archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20151006022505/https://www.nlm.nih.gov/medlineplus/ency/article/002817.htm|archive-date = 6 October 2015|df = dmy-all}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.sciencekids.co.nz/sciencefacts/metals/lead.html|title=Lead Facts – Uses, Properties, Element Pb, Plumbing, Pipes, Weights|website=www.sciencekids.co.nz|access-date=2016-10-29|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20161030075725/http://www.sciencekids.co.nz/sciencefacts/metals/lead.html|archive-date=30 October 2016}}
<br />e. ''[[Oxford English Dictionary]]'', 3rd ed., ''s.v.'' '[https://www.oed.com/view/Entry/106584 lead]' 3 and '[https://www.oed.com/view/Entry/19735 black lead]' 2a</ref></noinclude>

=== Computing and the Internet ===
* The [[macOS]] and [[Linux]] operating systems are not immune to [[malware]] such as [[Trojan horse (computing)|trojan horses]] or [[computer virus]]es.<noinclude><ref name="malware">a. {{#invoke:cite journal||url=http://news.techworld.com/security/1798/mac-os-x-security-myth-exposed/|title=Mac OS X Security Myth Exposed|journal=TechWorld|first=Matthew|last=Broersma|date=June 24, 2004|archive-url=https://www.webcitation.org/5yyPTKBhT?url=http://arstechnica.com/apple/news/2011/05/fake-mac-defender-antivirus-app-scams-users-for-money-cc-numbers.ars|archive-date=May 26, 2011|access-date=August 12, 2011}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=https://arstechnica.com/apple/news/2011/05/fake-mac-defender-antivirus-app-scams-users-for-money-cc-numbers.ars|title=Fake "MAC Defender" antivirus app scams users for money, CC numbers|date=May 2, 2011|first=Chris|last=Foresman|website=Ars Technica|archive-url=https://www.webcitation.org/5yyPTKBhT?url=http://arstechnica.com/apple/news/2011/05/fake-mac-defender-antivirus-app-scams-users-for-money-cc-numbers.ars|archive-date=May 26, 2011|url-status=live}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.linux.com/learn/tutorials/284124-myth-busting-is-linux-immune-to-viruses |title=Myth Busting: Is Linux Immune to Viruses? |website=Linux.com |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140430080334/http://www.linux.com/learn/tutorials/284124-myth-busting-is-linux-immune-to-viruses |archive-date=April 30, 2014}}</ref></noinclude> Specialized malware designed to attack those systems does exist. However, the vast majority of viruses are developed for [[Microsoft Windows]].<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Mookhey |first=K. K. |title=Linux: Security, Audit and Control Features |publisher=ISACA |year=2005 |isbn=978-1-893209-78-7 |page=128 |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=-kD0sxQ0EkIC&pg=PA128 |display-authors=etal |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20161201043333/https://books.google.com/books?id=-kD0sxQ0EkIC&pg=PA128|archive-date=December 1, 2016}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||author=Toxen, Bob|title=Real World Linux Security: Intrusion Prevention, Detection, and Recovery |publisher=Prentice Hall Professional |year=2003|isbn=978-0-13-046456-9|page=365|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=_-1jwRwNaEoC&pg=PA365|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20161201111057/https://books.google.com/books?id=_-1jwRwNaEoC&pg=PA365|archive-date=December 1, 2016}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite news||author=Noyes, Katherine|title=Why Linux Is More Secure Than Windows|work=PCWorld|date=August 3, 2010|url=https://www.pcworld.com/article/202452/why_linux_is_more_secure_than_windows.html|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130901151841/http://www.pcworld.com/article/202452/why_linux_is_more_secure_than_windows.html|archive-date=September 1, 2013}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Wallen |first=Jack |date=February 9, 2010 |title=Myth Busting: Is Linux Immune to Viruses? |url=https://www.linux.com/training-tutorials/myth-busting-linux-immune-viruses/ |access-date=April 9, 2022 |website=Linux.com |language=en-US}}
<br />e. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Do I need anti-virus software? |url=https://help.ubuntu.com/stable/ubuntu-help/net-antivirus.html.en#:~:text=Anti-virus%20software%20does%20exist,one%20writes%20viruses%20for%20it. |access-date=April 9, 2022 |website=help.ubuntu.com}}
<br />f. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Hoffman |first=Chris |title=Why You Don't Need an Antivirus On Linux (Usually) |url=https://www.howtogeek.com/135392/htg-explains-why-you-dont-need-an-antivirus-on-linux-and-when-you-do/ |access-date=April 9, 2022 |website=How-To Geek |date=September 21, 2016 |language=en-US}}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[deep web]] is not primarily full of pornography, illegal drug trade websites, and stolen bank details. This information is primarily found in a small portion of the deep web known as the "[[dark web]]". Much of the deep web consists of academic libraries, databases, and anything that is not indexed by normal search engines, including most private email accounts and direct messages.<noinclude><ref>a. {{cite magazine |last1=Greenberg |first1=Andy |title=Hacker Lexicon: What Is the Dark Web? |url=https://www.wired.com/2014/11/hacker-lexicon-whats-dark-web/ |magazine=Wired}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Callaghan|first=Greg|url=https://www.smh.com.au/technology/the-dark-web-uncovering-monsters-and-myths-in-the-net-s-evil-twin-20180307-p4z39d.html|title=The dark web: uncovering monsters (and myths) in the Net's 'evil twin'|website=[[The Sydney Morning Herald]]|date=March 9, 2018|access-date=August 8, 2022}}
<br />c. {{cite magazine |last1=Cox |first1=Joseph |date=June 18, 2015 |title=The Dark Web as You Know It Is a Myth |url=https://www.wired.com/2015/06/dark-web-know-myth/ |access-date=August 8, 2022 |magazine=Wired}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite web||last1=Beckett|first1=Andy|title=The dark side of the internet|url=https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2009/nov/26/dark-side-internet-freenet/|website=The Guardian|date=November 25, 2009|access-date=August 8, 2022}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Private browsing]] (such as [[Google Chrome|Chrome]]'s "Incognito Mode") does not protect users from being [[Web tracking|tracked]] by websites, governments, or one's [[internet service provider]] (ISP), nor does it hide one's information when using devices or networks owned or maintained by one's employer, school, or other entity, such as a coffee shop.
