Such entities can still use information such as [[IP address]]es and [[user accounts]] to uniquely identify users.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.fastcompany.com/90311396/incognito-mode-wont-keep-you-private-try-browser-compartmentalization|title=Incognito mode won't keep your browsing private. Do this instead |last=Grothaus |first=Michael|date=April 12, 2019|website=Fast Company|language=en-US|access-date=August 14, 2019}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.privacyflake.com/2018/08/incognito-mode-browsing-myths.html|title=Incognito mode while browsing – Myths Busted |first=Anirudh |last=B|work=Privacyflake |access-date=September 6, 2019|archive-date=May 16, 2020|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20200516215450/https://www.privacyflake.com/2018/08/incognito-mode-browsing-myths.html}}</ref></noinclude> Private browsing also does not provide additional protection against viruses or malware.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||title=Proceedings of the 2018 World Wide Web Conference: Your Secrets Are Safe: How Browsers' Explanations Impact Misconceptions About Private Browsing Mode|url=https://dl.acm.org/doi/pdf/10.1145/3178876.3186088|first1=Yuxi|last1=Wu|first2=Panya|last2=Gupta|first3=Miranda|last3=Wei|first4=Yasemin|last4=Acar|first5=Sascha|last5=Fahl|first6=Blase|last6=Ur|date=23 April 2018|isbn=978-1-4503-5639-8|publisher=International World Wide Web Conferences Steering Committee|location=Republic and Canton of Geneva, CHE in Lyon, France|doi=10.1145/3178876.3186088|pages=217–226|series=WWW '18|s2cid=4881375 }}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Phone repair with rice|Submerging a phone in rice]] after it has suffered from water damage has not been shown to be effective in repairing it.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Zelenko |first=Michael |date=September 14, 2015 |title=Putting your wet phone in rice probably won't save it. But do it anyway |url=https://www.theverge.com/2015/9/14/9326035/can-rice-actually-save-your-wet-phone |access-date=March 18, 2022 |website=[[The Verge]] |language=en}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Chugh |first=Ritesh |title=Phone wet and won't turn on? Here's how to deal with water damage (hint: soaking it in rice won't work) |url=http://theconversation.com/phone-wet-and-wont-turn-on-heres-how-to-deal-with-water-damage-hint-soaking-it-in-rice-wont-work-158633 |access-date=March 18, 2022 |website=[[The Conversation (website)|The Conversation]] |date=April 8, 2021 |language=en}}</ref></noinclude> Even if submerging them in a desiccant were more effective than leaving them to dry in open air, common desiccants such as silica gel or cat litter are better than rice.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Aguilar |first=Nelson |url=https://smartphones.gadgethacks.com/how-to/myth-debunked-uncooked-rice-isnt-best-way-save-your-water-damaged-phone-0154799/ |title=Myth Debunked: Uncooked Rice Isn't the Best Way to Save Your Water-Damaged Phone « Smartphones :: Gadget Hacks |publisher=Smartphones.gadgethacks.com |date=April 30, 2014 |access-date=May 16, 2022}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Mobile phone]]s do not create considerable [[electromagnetic interference]] when [[Mobile phone#In hospitals|used in hospitals]].<noinclude><ref name="Vreeman 2007">Rachel C. Vreeman, Aaron E. Carroll, "Medical Myths", ''[[The British Medical Journal]]'' (now called ''The BMJ'') '''335''':1288 (December 20, 2007), {{doi|10.1136/bmj.39420.420370.25}}</ref></noinclude>

=== Economics ===
[[File:Total population living in extreme poverty, by world region (PovcalNet, World Bank (1987 to 2013)), OWID.svg|thumb|upright=1.35|Total population living in extreme poverty, by world region 1987 to 2015<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Total population living in extreme poverty, by world region |url=https://ourworldindata.org/grapher/total-population-living-in-extreme-poverty-by-world-region |website=Our World in Data |access-date=March 7, 2020}}</ref></noinclude>]]
* The total number of people living in [[extreme poverty|extreme]] [[Poverty#Absolute poverty|absolute poverty]] globally, by the widely used metric of $1.00/day (in 1990 U.S. dollars) has decreased over the last several decades, but most people surveyed in several countries incorrectly think it has increased or stayed the same.<noinclude><ref>* {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Roser |first1=Max |last2=Ortiz-Ospina |first2=Esteban |date=May 25, 2013 |title=Global Extreme Poverty |url=https://ourworldindata.org/extreme-poverty |journal=Our World in Data}}
* {{#invoke:cite book||last1=Rosling |first1=Hans |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=j-4yDwAAQBAJ&pg=PA7 |title=Factfulness: Ten Reasons We're Wrong About the World{{snd}}and Why Things Are Better Than You Think |last2=Rönnlund |first2=Anna |last3=Rosling |first3=Ola |publisher=Flatiron Books |year=2018 |isbn=978-1-250-12381-7 |page=7}}
* {{#invoke:cite web||last=Bailey |first=Ronald |date=January 31, 2019 |title=Global Poverty Decline Denialism |url=https://reason.com/2019/01/31/global-poverty-decline-denialism/ |website=[[Reason (magazine)|Reason]]}}</ref></noinclude> However, this depends on the poverty line calculation used. For instance, if the metric used is instead one that prioritizes meeting a standard life expectancy that no longer significantly rises with additional consumption enabled by income, the number of individuals in poverty has risen by nearly 1 billion.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=2015-11-01 |title=Could you live on $1.90 a day? That's the international poverty line |url=http://www.theguardian.com/global-development-professionals-network/2015/nov/01/global-poverty-is-worse-than-you-think-could-you-live-on-190-a-day |access-date=2022-12-06 |website=the Guardian |language=en}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Edward |first=Peter |date=8 August 2006 |title=The ethical poverty line: a moral quantification of absolute poverty |url=http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/01436590500432739 |journal=Third World Quarterly |language=en |volume=27 |issue=2 |pages=377–393 |doi=10.1080/01436590500432739 |s2cid=154522588 |issn=0143-6597}}</ref></noinclude>
* Although the human population of Earth is increasing, the rate of [[Population growth|human population growth]] is decreasing and the world population is expected to peak and then begin falling during the 21st century. Improvements in agricultural productivity and technology are expected to be able to meet anticipated increased demand for resources, making a global [[human overpopulation]] scenario unlikely.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Scudellari|first=Megan|date=December 1, 2015|title=The science myths that will not die|journal=Nature|language=en|volume=528|issue=7582|pages=322–325|doi=10.1038/528322a|pmid=26672537|bibcode=2015Natur.528..322S|s2cid=1414926|issn=1476-4687|doi-access=free}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Human Overpopulation: Still an Issue of Concern?|url=https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/human-overpopulation-still-an-issue-of-concern/|access-date=October 21, 2021|website=Scientific American|language=en}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Piper|first=Kelsey|date=August 20, 2019|title=We've worried about overpopulation for centuries. And we've always been wrong.|url=https://www.vox.com/future-perfect/2019/8/20/20802413/overpopulation-demographic-transition-population-explained|access-date=October 21, 2021|website=Vox|language=en}}</ref></noinclude>
* For any given [[production set]], there is not a set amount of labor input (a "[[lump of labour fallacy|lump of labor]]") to produce that output. This fallacy is commonly seen in [[Luddite]] and later, related movements as an argument either that automation causes permanent, structural unemployment, or that labor-limiting regulation can decrease unemployment. In fact, changes in capital allocation, efficiency, and [[learning economy|economies of learning]] can change the amount of labor input for a given set of production.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite news||url=http://www.economist.com/economics-a-to-z/l|title=Economics A-Z terms beginning with L|newspaper=The Economist|access-date=December 21, 2016}}
<br />b. {{cite encyclopedia |last1=Bishop |first1=Matthew |author-link1=Matthew Bishop (journalist) |title=Essential Economics: An A to Z Guide |year=2004 |publisher=Bloomberg Press |isbn=978-1-86197-580-5 |chapter-url=http://www.economist.com/economics-a-to-z/l#node-21529454 |chapter=Lump of labour fallacy |quote=One of the best-known fallacies in ECONOMICS is the notion that there is a fixed amount of work to be done – a lump of LABOUR – which can be shared out in different ways to create fewer or more jobs... |url-access=registration |url=https://archive.org/details/essentialeconomi00bish}}</ref></noinclude>
* Income is not a direct factor in determining [[credit score in the United States]]. Rather, credit score is affected by the amount of unused available credit, which is in turn affected by income.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://okcfox.com/news/consumer-watch/consumer-watch-many-americans-think-income-affects-credit-score|title=Consumer Watch: Many Americans think income affects credit score|first=Mireya|last=Garcia|date=July 15, 2019|website=KOKH}}</ref></noinclude> Income is also considered when evaluating creditworthiness more generally.
* The US public vastly overestimates the amount spent on [[United States foreign aid#Public opinion|foreign aid]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last1=DiJulio|first1=Bianca|last2=Norton|first2=Mira|last3=Brodie|first3=Mollyann|date=January 20, 2016|title=Americans' Views on the U.S. Role in Global Health|url=https://www.kff.org/global-health-policy/poll-finding/americans-views-on-the-u-s-role-in-global-health/|access-date=January 12, 2021|website=[[Kaiser Family Foundation]]|language=en-US}}</ref></noinclude>
* In the US, an increase in [[gross income]] will never reduce a taxpayer's post-tax earnings ([[net income]]) by putting them in a higher [[tax bracket]]. Tax brackets specify [[marginal tax rate|''marginal'' tax rates]]: only income earned in the higher tax bracket is taxed at the higher rate.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.inc.com/mediafeed/lance-cothern/4-myths-about-how-taxes-work-debunked.html|title = 4 Common Misconceptions About Taxes, Debunked |website=Inc. Magazine |date = March 25, 2019}}</ref></noinclude> An increase in gross income can reduce net income in a [[welfare cliff]], however, when benefits are withdrawn when passing a certain income threshold.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Addressing Benefits Cliffs |url=https://www.ncsl.org/research/human-services/addressing-benefits-cliffs.aspx |website=[[National Conference of State Legislatures]] |access-date=September 15, 2021 |date=August 20, 2019}}</ref></noinclude> Prevalence of the misconception varies by political party affiliation.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| url=https://today.yougov.com/politics/articles/5057-understanding-how-marginal-taxes-work-its-all-part | title=Understanding How Marginal Taxes Work: It's all Party (Yet Again) &#124; YouGov }}</ref></noinclude>
* Constructing new housing decreases the cost of rent and the price of homes in both the immediate neighborhood and in the city as a whole. In [[real estate economics]], "supply skepticism" leads many Americans to misunderstand the effect of increasing the supply of housing on housing costs. The misconception is unique to the housing market.<noinclude><ref>{{Cite SSRN |last1=Nall |first1=Clayton |last2=Elmendorf |first2=Christopher |last3=Oklobdzija |first3=Stan |date=15 Nov 2022 |title=Folk Economics and the Persistence of Political Opposition to New Housing |ssrn=4266459}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Levitz |first=Eric |date=2023-08-04 |title=Rent Growth Is Slowing (Where Housing Got Built) |url=https://nymag.com/intelligencer/2023/08/rent-growth-is-slowing-where-housing-got-built.html |access-date=2023-08-04 |website=Intelligencer |language=en-us}}</ref></noinclude>
* Businesses do not get a tax benefit by [[Checkout charity|collecting charitable donations from their customers]]. Corporation taxes are based on [[profit (accounting)|profit]]; the customer's donation would not change the amount of profit and therefore the tax payable. A business would need to donate ''its own'' money to receive a tax break.<noinclude><ref name="corptaxusatoday">{{cite news |last=Link |first=Devon |date=2021-06-10 |title=Fact check: Stores cannot use checkout charity funds to offset their own taxes |url=https://eu.usatoday.com/story/news/factcheck/2021/06/10/fact-check-false-claim-checkout-charities-offset-corporate-taxes/7622379002/|work=[[USA Today]] |access-date=2023-04-01}}</ref><ref name="corptaxtaxpolicy">{{cite web | url          = https://www.taxpolicycenter.org/taxvox/who-gets-tax-benefit-those-checkout-donations-0| title        = Who Gets the Tax Benefit For Those Checkout Donations?| last         = Zaretsky| first        = Renu| date         = 2020-11-04| website      = [[Tax Policy Center]]| access-date  = 2023-04-01}}</ref><ref name="corptaxap">{{cite news | last=Swenson | first=Ali | date=2021-11-30 |title =Stores can't write off customer donations made at checkout |url=https://apnews.com/article/fact-checking-000329849244|work=[[Associated Press]]| access-date=2024-11-14}}</ref></noinclude>
* There is a near-unanimous consensus among economists that [[tariff|import tariffs]] have a net-negative effect on economic growth and welfare, and harm consumers through higher prices by more than they benefit domestic producers and governments. Additionally, import tariffs are taxes paid to the government by importers, not by exporting countries or manufacturers as is claimed by some, including [[Donald Trump]].<noinclude><ref>{{cite news |last=Wiseman | first=Paul | date=2024-09-27 | title=Trump favors huge new tariffs. How do they work?| url=https://www.pbs.org/newshour/economy/trump-favors-huge-new-tariffs-how-do-they-work| work=[[PBS]]| access-date = 2024-12-02}}</ref><ref>{{cite news | last=Lotz | first=Avery |date = 2024-11-06 | title=Trump's tariffs: How they work, and who would pay| url=https://www.axios.com/2024/09/28/how-tariffs-work-trump-china | work=[[Axios (website)|Axios]]| access-date = 2024-12-02}}</ref></noinclude>

=== Earth and environmental sciences ===
{{See also|Tornado myths}}
[[File:Common Era Temperature.svg|thumb|left|[[Global surface temperature]] reconstruction over the last 2000 years using proxy data from tree rings, corals, and ice cores in blue.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Neukom |first1=Raphael |last2=Barboza |first2=Luis A. |last3=Erb |first3=Michael P. |last4=Shi|first4=Feng |last5=Emile-Geay|first5=Julien |last6=Evans|first6=Michael N. |last7=Franke|first7=Jörg |last8=Kaufman|first8=Darrell S. |last9=Lücke|first9=Lucie |last10=Rehfeld|first10=Kira |last11=Schurer|first11=Andrew |date=2019 |title=Consistent multidecadal variability in global temperature reconstructions and simulations over the Common Era |journal=Nature Geoscience |volume=12 |issue=8 |pages=643–649|doi=10.1038/s41561-019-0400-0|pmid=31372180 |pmc=6675609 |bibcode=2019NatGe..12..643P |issn=1752-0908}}</ref></noinclude> Directly observed data is in red.<noinclude><ref name="nasa temperatures">{{#invoke:cite web||title=Global Annual Mean Surface Air Temperature Change |publisher=NASA |url=https://data.giss.nasa.gov/gistemp/graphs_v4/ |access-date=February 23, 2020}}</ref></noinclude>]]
* Contemporary [[global warming]] is [[Human impact on the environment|driven by human activities]],<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Powell |first1=James |date=November 20, 2019 |title=Scientists Reach 100% Consensus on Anthropogenic Global Warming |journal=Bulletin of Science, Technology & Society |volume=37 |issue=4 |pages=183–184 |doi=10.1177/0270467619886266 |s2cid=213454806}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Lynas |first1=Mark|last2=Houlton |first2=Benjamin Z.|last3=Perry |first3=Simon |date=2021 |title=Greater than 99% consensus on human caused climate change in the peer-reviewed scientific literature |journal=Environmental Research Letters|volume=16 |issue=11|at=114005 |doi=10.1088/1748-9326/ac2966 |bibcode=2021ERL....16k4005L|s2cid=239032360|issn=1748-9326|doi-access=free }}</ref></noinclude> despite claims that it is [[Climate change denial|not occurring]], [[Global warming conspiracy theory|does not have strong scientific consensus]], or that warming is [[Global warming controversy|mostly caused by non-human factors]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||publisher=Pew Research Center |url=https://www.pewresearch.org/science/2015/01/29/public-and-scientists-views-on-science-and-society/ |title=Public and Scientists' Views on Science and Society|date=January 29, 2015}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||author=Lydia Saad |url=https://news.gallup.com/poll/355427/americans-concerned-global-warming.aspx |title=Are Americans Concerned About Global Warming?|publisher=Gallup|date=October 5, 2021}}</ref><ref>{{cite magazine |last=Begley |first=Sharon |author-link=Sharon Begley |date=August 13, 2007 |title=The Truth About Denial |url=http://www.newsweek.com/id/32482 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20071021024942/http://www.newsweek.com/id/32482 |archive-date=October 21, 2007 |magazine=[[Newsweek]]}} ([https://web.archive.org/web/20070820002929/http://www.msnbc.msn.com/id/20122975/site/newsweek/page/0/ MSNBC single page version, archived 20 August 2007])</ref></noinclude> No scientific body of national or international standing disagrees with the decades-old, near-complete [[scientific consensus on climate change]].<noinclude><ref>a. {{cite report |title=Understanding and responding to climate change: Highlights of National Academies Reports, 2008 edition |year=2008 |publisher=National Academy of Sciences |page=2 |url=http://dels.nas.edu/resources/static-assets/materials-based-on-reports/booklets/climate_change_2008_final.pdf |access-date=November 9, 2010 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20171011182257/http://dels.nas.edu/resources/static-assets/materials-based-on-reports/booklets/climate_change_2008_final.pdf |archive-date=October 11, 2017}}<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||chapter=The scientific consensus on climate change: How do we know we're not wrong? |last1=Oreskes |first1=Naomi |author1-link=Naomi Oreskes |title=Climate Change: What It Means for Us, Our Children, and Our Grandchildren |editor-last1=DiMento |editor-first1=Joseph F. C. |editor-last2=Doughman |editor-first2=Pamela M. |publisher=The MIT Press |year=2007 |isbn=978-0-262-54193-0 |page=[https://books.google.com/books?id=PXJIqCkb7YIC&pg=PA68 68]}}<br />c. {{#invoke:cite news||last1=Gleick |first1=Peter |title=Statements on Climate Change from Major Scientific Academies, Societies, and Associations (January 2017 update) |date=January 7, 2017 |work=ScienceBlogs |access-date=April 2, 2020|url=https://scienceblogs.com/significantfigures/index.php/2017/01/07/statements-on-climate-change-from-major-scientific-academies-societies-and-associations-january-2017-update}}</ref></noinclude> Global warming is primarily a result of the increase in atmospheric [[greenhouse gas|greenhouse-gas]] concentrations (like [[Carbon dioxide|CO<sub>2</sub>]] and [[methane]]) via the [[Industrial Revolution|burning]] of [[fossil fuel]]s as well as other [[Anthropogenic hazard|human activities]] such as [[Deforestation and climate change|deforestation]], with secondary [[climate change feedback]] mechanisms (such as the [[Ice–albedo feedback|melting of the polar ice increasing the Earth's absorption of sunlight]]) assisting to perpetuate the change.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://www.un.org/en/global-issues/climate-change|title=Climate Change|newspaper=United Nations }}</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Area of the ozone hole.jpg|thumb|[[Ozone depletion]] is not a cause of [[global warming]].]]
* [[Global warming]] is not caused by the [[Ozone depletion|hole in the ozone layer]]. Ozone depletion is a separate problem caused by [[chlorofluorocarbon]]s (CFCs)<noinclude><ref name="Jacob">Jacob, Daniel J. ''Introduction to Atmospheric Chemistry''. pp. 177–87. Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1999.</ref></noinclude> which have been released into the atmosphere.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Chlorofluorocarbons |website=Greenhouse Gases |publisher=NOAA National Centers for Environmental Information |url=https://www.ncdc.noaa.gov/monitoring-references/faq/greenhouse-gases.php?section=cfc |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150830030045/https://www.ncdc.noaa.gov/monitoring-references/faq/greenhouse-gases.php?section=cfc |archive-date=August 30, 2015 |access-date=October 21, 2018 }}</ref></noinclude> However, CFCs are strong [[greenhouse gas]]es.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://blogs.nasa.gov/whatonearth/tag/cfcs/ |title=CFCS – What on Earth|date=February 24, 2010 }}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url= https://www.bbc.co.uk/bitesize/guides/z3bbb9q/revision/3 |title=The enhanced greenhouse effect and global warming – Physical and human causes of climate change – Higher Geography Revision |website=BBC Bitesize |date=January 1, 1970 |access-date=February 9, 2022}}</ref></noinclude> Further, the hole in the ozone layer is shrinking and in 2019 was the smallest it had been since 1982,<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=2019-10-21 |title=2019 Ozone Hole is the Smallest on Record Since Its Discovery – NASA |url=https://www.nasa.gov/centers-and-facilities/goddard/2019-ozone-hole-is-the-smallest-on-record-since-its-discovery/ |access-date=2024-04-14 |language=en-US}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Ansari |first=Talal |title=Ozone Hole Above Antarctica Shrinks to Smallest Size on Record |url=https://www.wsj.com/articles/ozone-hole-above-antarctica-shrinks-to-smallest-size-on-record-11571847944 |access-date=2024-04-14 |work=WSJ |language=en-US}}</ref></noinclude> while global warming continues.
[[File:Cows and cooling towers, Cottam - geograph.org.uk - 450176.jpg|thumb|upright|Cooling towers from a nuclear power plant. The white clouds are harmless water vapor from the cooling process.]]
* [[Cooling tower]]s in [[power station]]s and other facilities do not emit smoke or harmful fumes; they emit water vapor and do not contribute to climate change.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=February 15, 2007 |title=Myth of cooling towers is symptomatic of global warming information shortage |url=https://www.rsc.org/news-events/articles/2007/02-february/cooling-towers/ |access-date=March 2, 2022 |website=[[Royal Society of Chemistry]] |language=en-GB}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| url=https://climate.mit.edu/ask-mit/why-do-we-blame-climate-change-carbon-dioxide-when-water-vapor-much-more-common-greenhouse | title=Why do we blame climate change on carbon dioxide, when water vapor is a much more common greenhouse gas? &#124; MIT Climate Portal }}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Nuclear power]] is [[Nuclear power#Safety|one of the safest sources of energy]], resulting in orders of magnitude fewer deaths than conventional power sources per unit of energy produced. Extremely few people are killed or injured due to nuclear power on a yearly basis.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Energy |first=Duke |title=Common Myths About Nuclear Energy |url=https://nuclear.duke-energy.com/2013/06/12/common-myths-about-nuclear-energy |access-date=2023-04-23 |website=Duke Energy {{!}} Nuclear Information Center |language=en}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=10 myths about nuclear energy {{!}} Argonne National Laboratory |url=https://www.anl.gov/article/10-myths-about-nuclear-energy |access-date=2023-04-23 |website=www.anl.gov |date=September 9, 2013 |language=en}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=What are the safest sources of energy? |url=https://ourworldindata.org/safest-sources-of-energy |access-date=2020-05-27 |website=Our World in Data}}</ref><ref name="Frank N. von Hippel 27–36">{{#invoke:cite journal||author=Frank N. von Hippel |date=September–October 2011 |title=The radiological and psychological consequences of the Fukushima Daiichi accident |url=http://bos.sagepub.com/content/67/5/27.full |journal=Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists |volume=67 |issue=5 |pages=27–36 |bibcode=2011BuAtS..67e..27V |doi=10.1177/0096340211421588 |s2cid=218769799}}</ref></noinclude> (See also: [[Radiophobia]])
* [[Earthquake]] strength (or magnitude) is not commonly measured using the [[Richter scale]]. Although the Richter scale was used ''historically'' to measure earthquake magnitude (although, notably, not [[Modified Mercalli intensity scale|earthquake damage]]), it was found in the 1970s that it does not reliably represent the magnitude of large earthquakes. It has therefore been largely replaced by the [[moment magnitude scale]],<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=2016-04-28 |title=USGS Earthquake Magnitude Policy (implemented on January 18, 2002) |url=http://earthquake.usgs.gov:80/aboutus/docs/020204mag_policy.php |access-date=2023-11-08 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160428095841/http://earthquake.usgs.gov:80/aboutus/docs/020204mag_policy.php |archive-date=April 28, 2016 }}</ref></noinclude> although very small earthquakes are still sometimes measured using the Richter scale.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=British Geological Survey Seismology Monitoring Page |url=https://www.earthquakes.bgs.ac.uk/education/faqs/faq15.html |access-date=2023-11-08 |website=www.earthquakes.bgs.ac.uk}}</ref></noinclude> Nevertheless, earthquake magnitude is still widely misattributed to the Richter scale.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=2011-05-10 |title=Explained: Measuring earthquakes |url=https://news.mit.edu/2011/exp-measuring-earthquakes-0510 |access-date=2023-11-08 |website=MIT News {{!}} Massachusetts Institute of Technology |language=en}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Reich |first=Kenneth |date=1988-02-29 |title=Science / Medicine: The Richter Scale: Caltech Seismologists Say Temblor Rating Has a Magnitude of Problems |url=https://www.latimes.com/archives/la-xpm-1988-02-29-me-179-story.html |access-date=2023-11-08 |website=Los Angeles Times |language=en-US}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=2015-05-15 |title=What are the faultlines with the Richter scale? |url=https://www.euronews.com/2015/05/15/what-are-the-faultlines-with-the-richter-scale |access-date=2023-11-08 |website=euronews |language=en}}</ref></noinclude>[[File:5-Bar-chart-–-What-is-the-safest-form-of-energy.png|thumb|Death rates from air pollution and accidents related to energy production, measured in deaths per terawatt hours (TWh) (left). Carbon emissions measured in tons per gigawatt hour (GWh) (right).]]
* [[Lightning]] can, and often does, strike the same place twice. Lightning in a thunderstorm is more likely to strike objects and spots that are more prominent or conductive. For instance, lightning strikes the [[Empire State Building]] in [[New York City]] on average 23 times per year.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.weather.gov/safety/lightning-myths|title=Lightning Myths and Facts|publisher=National Weather Service|quote=Fact: Lightning often strikes the same place repeatedly, especially if it's a tall, pointy, isolated object. The Empire State Building is hit an average of 23 times a year}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Lightning Often Strikes Twice|work=NASA Spinoff|publisher=Office of the Chief Technologist, NASA|url=http://www.sti.nasa.gov/tto/Spinoff2005/ps_3.html|access-date=June 23, 2010|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120325015918/http://www.sti.nasa.gov/tto/Spinoff2005/ps_3.html|archive-date=March 25, 2012}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||author=WeatherBug Meteorologists|title=The Myths and Facts of Lightning|website=WeatherBug|url=http://weather.weatherbug.com/weather-news/weather-reports.html?story=6571|publisher=Earth Works|date=May 17, 2010|access-date=June 23, 2010|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20100711061017/http://weather.weatherbug.com/weather-news/weather-reports.html?story=6571|archive-date=2010-07-11|url-status=live}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite web||author=Tristan Simpson|date=Apr 29, 2022|url=https://www.theweathernetwork.com/ca/news/article/can-lightning-strike-the-same-place-twice|title=Can lightning strike the same place twice?|website=The Weather Network}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Heat lightning]] does not exist as a distinct phenomenon. What is mistaken for "heat lightning" is usually ordinary lightning from storms too distant to hear the associated [[thunder]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=What Is Heat Lightning?|url=https://weather.com/science/weather-explainers/news/heat-lightning-explainer |publisher=[[Weather.com]] }}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Yellowstone Caldera]] is [[Yellowstone Caldera#Volcanoes|not overdue]] for a [[supervolcano]] eruption.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date= May 8, 2015 |title= Five Things Most People Get Wrong About the Yellowstone Volcano (2015) |url=https://www.usgs.gov/volcanoes/yellowstone/five-things-most-people-get-wrong-about-yellowstone-volcano-2015 |publisher=[[Yellowstone Volcano Observatory]] (USGS) |access-date=April 2, 2022}}</ref></noinclude> There is also no evidence that it will erupt in the near future. In fact, data indicates there will not be an eruption in the coming centuries.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Modeling the Ash Distribution of a Yellowstone Supereruption (2014) {{!}} U.S. Geological Survey |url=https://www.usgs.gov/volcanoes/yellowstone/modeling-ash-distribution-yellowstone-supereruption-2014 |access-date=2023-11-27 |website=www.usgs.gov}}</ref></noinclude> The most likely eruption would be [[Hydrothermal explosion|hydrothermal]] rather than volcanic. A [[caldera]]-forming volcanic eruption (and subsequent impacts on global weather patterns and agricultural production) is the least likely scenario and has an extremely low likelihood.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Questions About Future Volcanic Activity at Yellowstone {{!}} U.S. Geological Survey |url=https://www.usgs.gov/volcanoes/yellowstone/questions-about-future-volcanic-activity-yellowstone |access-date=2023-11-27 |website=www.usgs.gov}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Could a large Yellowstone eruption significantly change the climate? {{!}} U.S. Geological Survey |url=https://www.usgs.gov/faqs/could-a-large-yellowstone-eruption-significantly-change-climate |access-date=2023-11-27 |website=www.usgs.gov}}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Structure of Earth|Earth's interior]] is not molten rock. This misconception may originate from a misunderstanding based on the fact that the [[mantle convection|Earth's mantle convects]], and the incorrect assumption that only liquids and gases can convect. In fact, a solid with a large [[Rayleigh number]] can also convect, given enough time, which is what occurs in the solid mantle due to the very large thermal gradient across it.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last1=Gupta |first1=H.K. |title=Encyclopedia of Solid Earth Geophysics |date=2011 |publisher=Springer Dordrecht |isbn=978-90-481-8701-0 |page=1539}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last1=Robertson |first1=E.C. |title=The Interior of the Earth |url=https://pubs.usgs.gov/gip/interior/ |publisher=USGS |access-date=July 8, 2022}}</ref></noinclude> There are small pockets of [[Magma|molten rock]] in the upper mantle, but these make up a tiny fraction of the mantle's volume.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Hata |first1=M. |last2=Uyeshima |first2=M. |title=Temperature and melt fraction distributions in a mantle wedge determined from the electrical conductivity structure: Application to one nonvolcanic and two volcanic regions in the Kyushu subduction zone, Japan |journal=Geophysical Research Letters |date=March 31, 2015 |volume=42 |issue=8 |pages=2709–2717 |doi=10.1002/2015GL063308|bibcode=2015GeoRL..42.2709H |s2cid=128585826 }}</ref></noinclude> The Earth's [[Earth's outer core|outer core]] ''is'' liquid, but it is liquid metal, not rock.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Clement |first1=B.M. |last2=Holzheid |first2=A. |last3=Tilgner |first3=A. |title=Core geophysics |journal=PNAS |date=October 25, 1997 |volume=94 |issue=24 |pages=12742–12743 |doi=10.1073/pnas.94.24.12742|pmid=9370529 |pmc=34384 |bibcode=1997PNAS...9412742C |doi-access=free }}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Amazon rainforest]] does not provide 20% of Earth's oxygen. This is a misinterpretation of a 2010 study which found that approximately 34% of photosynthesis by terrestrial plants occurs in tropical rainforests (so the Amazon rainforest would account for approximately half of this). Due to respiration by the resident organisms, all ecosystems (including the Amazon rainforest) have a net output of oxygen of approximately zero. The oxygen currently present in the atmosphere was accumulated over billions of years.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Katarina|first=Zimmer|date=August 28, 2019|title=Why the Amazon doesn't really produce 20% of the world's oxygen |url=https://www.nationalgeographic.com/environment/article/why-amazon-doesnt-produce-20-percent-worlds-oxygen|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210218183525/https://www.nationalgeographic.com/environment/article/why-amazon-doesnt-produce-20-percent-worlds-oxygen|archive-date=February 18, 2021|access-date=October 8, 2021|website=National Geographic}}</ref></noinclude>

=== Geography ===
{{See also|Mercator projection#Examples of size distortion}}
[[File:CapeHopeDetail.png|thumb|upright=1.35|The [[Cape of Good Hope]] and [[Cape Agulhas]], the southernmost point of Africa]]
* The [[Cape of Good Hope]] is not the southern tip of Africa, which is actually [[Cape Agulhas]], about {{convert|150|km|-1}} to the east-southeast.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.airpano.com/360photo/cape-good-hope-rsa|title=Cape of Good Hope, South Africa. The Most South-Western Point of The African Continent|website=AirPano}}</ref></noinclude>
* The majority of the [[Sahara]] consists of [[Hamada|rocks]], rather than [[Erg (landform)|sand]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=June 19, 2024 |title=Journey through a stunning diversity of cultures and sounds in the Sahara Desert |url=https://www.abc.net.au/listen/programs/passenger/passenger-sahara-cultures-and-sounds/103948632 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240619110922/https://www.abc.net.au/listen/programs/passenger/passenger-sahara-cultures-and-sounds/103948632 |archive-date=June 19, 2024 |access-date=June 20, 2024 |website=[[Australian Broadcasting Corporation]]}}</ref><ref name="sciencedirect.com"/></noinclude>
* Rivers do not predominantly flow from north to south. [[River#The flow of rivers|Rivers flow downhill]] in all compass directions, often changing direction along their course.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal|| url=https://journals.flvc.org/flgeog/article/view/76274 | title=More Than a Few Northerly Running Rivers in the World | journal=The Florida Geographer | date=May 23, 2008 | volume=39 | last1=Kamiar | first1=Mohammad }}</ref><ref name="Rosenberg 2020">{{#invoke:cite web||last=Rosenberg |first=Matt |date=April 5, 2020 |title=Major Rivers That Flow North |url=https://www.thoughtco.com/rivers-flowing-north-1435099 |access-date=November 8, 2022 |website=ThoughtCo.}}</ref></noinclude> Many major rivers flow northward, including the [[Nile]], the [[Yenisey]], the [[Ob (river)|Ob]], the [[Rhine]], the [[Lena (river)|Lena]], and the [[Orinoco]].<noinclude><ref name="Rosenberg 2020" /><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.worldatlas.com/rivers/rivers-that-flow-north.html|title=Rivers That Flow North|date=May 6, 2021 }}</ref></noinclude>

=== Human body and health ===
{{See also|Urban legends about drugs|Common misconceptions about birth control}}
[[File:Korean fans closeup.jpg|thumb|[[Fan death|Leaving electric fans on while asleep is not dangerous]].]]

* It is not just [[heavy metals]] which can be toxic; other metals (for example [[beryllium]] and [[lithium]]) can be toxic too.<noinclude><ref>{{cite encyclopedia |year=2024 |entry=Metal Toxicity |title=Dictionary of Toxicology |publisher=Springer |doi=10.1007/978-981-99-9283-6_1678}}</ref></noinclude>
* Sleeping in a closed room with an electric fan running does not result in "[[fan death]]", as is widely believed in South Korea among older people.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.esquire.com/news-politics/q-and-a/a5522/korean-fan-death-0209/|title=Why Do Koreans Think Electric Fans Will Kill Them?|date=January 22, 2009|website=Esquire|access-date=August 2, 2016}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Shapiro |first=Ari |date=August 9, 2015 |title=South Korea's Quirky Notions About Electric Fans |url=https://www.npr.org/sections/parallels/2015/08/09/430341089/south-koreas-quirky-notions-about-electric-fans |access-date=June 15, 2024 |work=[[NPR]]}}</ref></noinclude> As of 2019 this belief was in decline.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last=Paolucci |first=Paul |url=https://brill.com/display/title/54437 |title=Acquiring Modernity |chapter=Acquiring Modernity: An Investigation into the Rise, Structure, and Future of the Modern World |publisher=[[Brill Publishers]] |isbn=978-90-04-39395-0 |series=Studies in Critical Social Sciences, Volume: 136 |location=Leiden, The Netherlands |publication-date=July 18, 2019 |page=9}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Nocturia]] (waking up at night to urinate) is equally prevalent in women and men,<noinclude><ref>{{cite journal |vauthors=van Kerrebroeck P, Hashim H, Holm-Larsen T, Robinson D, Stanley N |title=Thinking beyond the bladder: antidiuretic treatment of nocturia |journal=Int J Clin Pract |volume=64 |issue=6 |pages=807–16 |date=May 2010 |pmid=20337753 |doi=10.1111/j.1742-1241.2010.02336.x |url=}}</ref></noinclude> although it is more common among both men and women over 50.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke cite web ||url= |publisher=Cleveland Clinic |title=Nocturia|access-date=Aug 1, 2024 }}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Sleepwalking#Treatment|Waking up a sleepwalker does not harm them]]. Sleepwalkers may be confused or disoriented for a short time after awakening, but the health risks associated with sleepwalking are from injury or insomnia, not from being awakened.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.medicinenet.com/sleepwalking/article.htm|title=Sleepwalking: Causes, Symptoms, and Treatments|publisher=MedicineNet, Inc| author1=Mersch, John |access-date=May 10, 2009 | archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20131026073747/http://www.medicinenet.com/sleepwalking/article.htm | archive-date = October 26, 2013| url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.sleepfoundation.org/article/sleep-related-problems/sleepwalking|title=Sleepwalking|publisher=National Sleep Foundation|access-date=May 10, 2009 | archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20131229075629/http://www.sleepfoundation.org/article/sleep-related-problems/sleepwalking | archive-date = December 29, 2013| url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Seizure]]s cannot cause a person to swallow their own tongue,<noinclude><ref>{{ cite book |vauthors=Tarulli A |year=2021 |chapter=Seizures and Epilepsy |title=Neurology |publisher=Springer |doi=10.1007/978-3-030-55598-6_20 |isbn=978-3-030-55597-9 |pages=289–315}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Rossi |first1=Kyle C. |last2=Baumgartner |first2=Alexander J. |last3=Goldenholz |first3=Shira R. |last4=Goldenholz |first4=Daniel M. |date=December 2020 |title=Recognizing and refuting the myth of tongue swallowing during a seizure |journal=Seizure |volume=83 |pages=32–37 |doi=10.1016/j.seizure.2020.09.023 |issn=1532-2688 |pmid=33080482|s2cid=222137755 |doi-access=free }}</ref></noinclude> and it is dangerous to attempt to place a foreign object into a convulsing person's mouth. Instead it is recommended to gently lay a convulsing person on their side to minimize the risk of asphyxiation.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=2022-01-03 |title=Seizure First Aid {{!}} Epilepsy {{!}} CDC |url=https://www.cdc.gov/epilepsy/about/first-aid.htm |access-date=2023-11-10 |website=www.cdc.gov |language=en-us}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Drowning]] is often inconspicuous to onlookers.<noinclude><ref name="Vittone">{{#invoke:cite journal||url=http://www.uscg.mil/hq/cg5/cg534/On%20Scene/OSFall06.pdf |title=It Doesn't Look Like They're Drowning |last=Vittone |first=Mario |page=14 |journal=On Scene: The Journal of U.S. Coast Guard Search and Rescue}}</ref></noinclude> In most cases, the [[instinctive drowning response]] prevents the victim from waving or yelling (known as "aquatic distress"),<noinclude><ref name="Vittone" /></noinclude> which are therefore not dependable signs of trouble; indeed, most drowning victims undergoing the response do not show prior evidence of distress.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||editor-last1=Fletemeyer|editor-first1=John R. |editor-last2=Freas |editor-first2=Samuel J.|title=Drowning: new perspectives on intervention and prevention |volume=1998|date=1999|page=234|chapter-url=https://books.google.com/books?id=Xcnwm8zE24QC|last=Pia|first=Frank|chapter=Chapter 14: Reflections on Lifeguard surveillance programs|publisher=Taylor & Francis |isbn=978-1-57444-223-6}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Herbal medicine]]s are not necessarily safe and side-effect free; such medicines can have [[adverse effect]]s.<noinclude><ref>{{cite book |vauthors=Kumar P, Nandave M, Kumar A, Nandave D |year=2024 |chapter= Herbovigilance|veditors=Nandave M, Kumar A |title=Pharmacovigilance Essentials |publisher=Springer | doi=10.1007/978-981-99-8949-2_12 |pages=243–267|isbn=978-981-99-8948-5 }}</ref></noinclude>
* Human blood in veins is not actually blue. Blood is red due to the presence of [[hemoglobin]]; deoxygenated blood (in veins) has a deep red color, and oxygenated blood (in [[arteries]]) has a light cherry-red color. Veins below the skin can appear blue or green due to [[subsurface scattering]] of light through the skin, and aspects of human color perception. Many medical diagrams also use blue to show veins, and red to show arteries, which contributes to this misconception.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Kienle|first1=Alwin|first2=Lothar|last2=Lilge|first3=I.Alex|last3=Vitkin|first4=Michael S.|last4=Patterson|first5=Brian C.|last5=Wilson|first6=Raimund|last6=Hibst|first7=Rudolf|last7=Steiner|date=March 1, 1996|title=Why do veins appear blue? A new look at an old question|journal=Applied Optics|volume=35|issue=7|pages=1151–60|url=http://www.imt.liu.se/edu/courses/TBMT36/pdf/blue.pdf|doi=10.1364/AO.35.001151|pmid=21085227|bibcode=1996ApOpt..35.1151K|access-date=April 30, 2012|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120210145120/https://www.imt.liu.se/edu/courses/TBMT36/pdf/blue.pdf|archive-date=February 10, 2012}}</ref></noinclude>
* Exposure to a vacuum, or experiencing all but the most extreme [[uncontrolled decompression]], does not cause the body to explode or internal fluids to boil (although the fluids in the mouth and lungs will indeed boil at altitudes above the [[Armstrong limit]]); rather, it will lead to a loss of [[consciousness]] once the body has depleted the supply of oxygen in the blood, followed by death from [[hypoxia (medical)|hypoxia]] within minutes.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||url=https://imagine.gsfc.nasa.gov/ask_astro/space_travel.html|title=Ask an Astrophysicist: How would the unprotected human body react to the vacuum of outer space?|publisher=[[NASA]]|url-status=live|archive-url=https://www.webcitation.org/68Aef3glC?url=http://imagine.gsfc.nasa.gov/docs/ask_astro/answers/970603.html|archive-date=June 4, 2012}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.abc.net.au/science/articles/2005/04/07/1320013.htm|title=Exploding Body in Vacuum|work=ABC Science|date=April 6, 2005|url-status=live|archive-url=https://www.webcitation.org/68Aef3glC?url=http://imagine.gsfc.nasa.gov/docs/ask_astro/answers/970603.html|archive-date=June 4, 2012}}</ref></noinclude>
* Exercise-induced [[delayed onset muscle soreness]] is not caused by [[lactic acid]] build-up. Muscular lactic acid levels return to normal levels within an hour after exercise; delayed onset muscle soreness is thought to be due to [[microtrauma]] from unaccustomed or strenuous exercise.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||last=Cheung|first=K|author2=Hume, P |author3=Maxwell, L |title=Delayed onset muscle soreness: treatment strategies and performance factors|journal=Sports Medicine|year=2003|volume=33|issue=2|pages=145–64|pmid=12617692|doi=10.2165/00007256-200333020-00005|s2cid=26525519}}
<br />b.{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=McHugh |first1=Malachy P. |title=Recent advances in the understanding of the repeated bout effect: the protective effect against muscle damage from a single bout of eccentric exercise |journal=Scandinavian Journal of Medicine & Science in Sports |date=2003 |volume=13 |issue=2 |pages=88–97 |issn=0905-7188|pmid=12641640 |doi=10.1034/j.1600-0838.2003.02477.x |s2cid=6697478 }}</ref></noinclude>
* Stretching before or after exercise does not reduce [[delayed onset muscle soreness]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Henschke |first1=Nicholas |last2=Lin |first2=C. Christine |title=Stretching before or after exercise does not reduce delayed-onset muscle soreness |journal=Br J Sports Med |date=December 1, 2011 |volume=45 |issue=15 |pages=1249–50 |doi=10.1136/bjsports-2011-090599 |pmid=22006932 |s2cid=32498886 |url=https://bjsm.bmj.com/content/45/15/1249.full |access-date=September 11, 2018 |language=en |issn=0306-3674}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Urine#Bacteria and pathogens|Urine is not sterile]], not even in the bladder.<noinclude><ref name="Engelhaupt 2014">{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://www.sciencenews.org/blog/gory-details/urine-not-sterile-and-neither-rest-you|title=Urine is not sterile, and neither is the rest of you|last1=Engelhaupt|first1=Erika|date=May 22, 2014|website=Science News|language=en|access-date=July 31, 2020|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180622083801/https://www.sciencenews.org/blog/gory-details/urine-not-sterile-and-neither-rest-you|archive-date=June 22, 2018|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> This misconception may derive from urine bacterial screening tests, which return "negative" when bacteria levels are low, but nonzero.<noinclude><ref name="Engelhaupt 2014" /></noinclude>
* Sudden immersion into freezing water does not typically cause death by [[hypothermia]], but rather from the [[cold shock response]], which can cause [[cardiac arrest]], [[heart attack]], or [[hyperventilation]] leading to [[drowning]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Tipton|first1=M. J.|last2=Collier|first2=N.|last3=Massey|first3=H.|last4=Corbett|first4=J.|last5=Harper|first5=M.|date=November 1, 2017|title=Cold water immersion: kill or cure?: Cold water immersion: kill or cure?|journal=Experimental Physiology|language=en|volume=102|issue=11|pages=1335–55|doi=10.1113/EP086283|pmid=28833689|doi-access=free}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Cremation|Cremated remains]] are not ashes in the usual sense. After the incineration is completed, the dry bone fragments are swept out of the retort and pulverized by a machine called a ''cremulator'' (essentially a high-capacity, high-speed blender) to process them into "ashes" or "cremated remains".<noinclude><ref name="green2006">{{#invoke:cite book||last=Green |first=Jennifer |title=Dealing With Death: Practices and Procedures |author2=Green, Michael |publisher=Jessica Kingsley Publishers |year=2006 |isbn=978-1-84310-381-3 |page=112}}</ref></noinclude>
* The lung's [[Pulmonary alveolus|alveoli]] are not tiny balloons that expand and contract under positive pressure following the [[Young–Laplace equation]], as is taught in some physiology and medical textbooks. The tissue structure is more like a sponge with polygonal spaces that unfold and fold under negative pressure from the chest wall.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||doi=10.1152/advan.00024.2002 |pmid=12594072 |volume=27 |issue=1 |title=Laplace's Law and the Alveolus: A Misconception of Anatomy and a Misapplication of Physics |journal=Advances in Physiology Education |pages=34–40 |last1=Prange |first1=Henry D. |year=2003|s2cid=7791096 }}</ref></noinclude>
* Half of [[body heat]] is not lost through the head, and covering the head is no more effective at preventing [[Hypothermia|heat loss]] than covering any other portion of the body. Heat is lost from the body in proportion to the amount of exposed skin.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||first6=Joseph|vauthors=Sessler DI, Moayeri A, Støen R, Glosten B, Hynson J, McGuire J|year=1990|title=Thermoregulatory vasoconstriction decreases cutaneous heat loss|journal=Anesthesiology |volume=73|issue=4|pages=656–60|doi=10.1097/00000542-199010000-00011|pmid=2221434|doi-access=free}}</ref><ref name="Gammon 2013">{{#invoke:cite web||first=Katharine|last=Gammon|date=February 1, 2013|title=Do We Really Lose Half our Body Heat From our Heads?|url=https://www.livescience.com/34411-body-heat-loss-head.html|access-date=January 18, 2022|website=[[Live Science]]|language=en}}</ref></noinclude> The head accounts for around 7–9% of the body's surface, and studies have shown that having one's head submerged in cold water only causes a person to lose 10% more heat overall.<noinclude><ref name="Sample 2008">{{cite news |last1=Sample |first1=Ian |date=17 December 2008 |title=Scientists debunk the myth that you lose most heat through your head |url=https://www.theguardian.com/science/2008/dec/17/medicalresearch-humanbehaviour |access-date=29 July 2024 |work=The Guardian}}</ref></noinclude>{{Medical citation needed|date=July 2024}}
* [[Adrenochrome]] is not harvested from living people and has no use as a recreational drug. [[Hunter S. Thompson]] conceived a fictional drug of the same name in his book ''[[Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas]]'', apparently as a metaphor and unaware that a real substance by that name existed; it is Thompson's fictional adrenochrome, and not the real chemical compound, that is the source of numerous conspiracy theories revolving around human trafficking to harvest the fictional drug.<noinclude><ref name=adams>{{#invoke:cite news||last1=Adams |first1=Josie |date=April 7, 2020 |title=The truth about adrenochrome |url=https://thespinoff.co.nz/society/07-04-2020/explainer-adrenochrome-the-drug-that-doesnt-exist/ |access-date=June 28, 2020 |work=The Spinoff}}</ref><ref name="friedberg">{{cite magazine |last=Friedberg |first=Brian |title=The Dark Virality of a Hollywood Blood-Harvesting Conspiracy |magazine=Wired |url=https://www.wired.com/story/opinion-the-dark-virality-of-a-hollywood-blood-harvesting-conspiracy/ |access-date=February 21, 2021}}</ref></noinclude>
* Men and women have the same number of ribs: 24, or 12 pairs. The erroneous idea that women have one more rib than men may stem from the [[Genesis creation narrative|biblical creation story]] of [[Adam and Eve]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=March 12, 2020 |title=How many ribs do humans have? Men, women, and anatomy |first=Danielle |last=Dresden |url=https://www.medicalnewstoday.com/articles/how-many-ribs-do-humans-have#men-vs-women |access-date=June 5, 2022 |website=Medical News Today |language=en |quote=Although many people might think that males have fewer ribs than females — most likely sparked by the biblical story of Adam and Eve — there is no factual evidence.}}</ref></noinclude>
* The use of [[cotton swab]]s (aka cotton buds or Q-Tips) in the ear canal has no associated medical benefits and poses definite medical risks.<noinclude><ref>a. {{citation |last=Moser |first=Rod |url=http://blogs.webmd.com/all-ears/2006/11/q-tips-weapons-of-ear-destruction.html |title=Q-Tips – Weapons of Ear Destruction? |publisher=WebMD |date=13 November 2006}}
<br />b. {{citation |first=Joel |last=Stein |url=http://content.time.com/time/nation/article/0,8599,105556,00.html |title=Something Evil in the Ear Canal |magazine=Time |date=26 March 2001}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite journal||last=American Academy of Family Physicians|title=Information from Your Family Doctor—Earwax: What You Should Know|journal=American Family Physician|date=May 2007|volume=75|issue=10|page=1530|url=http://www.aafp.org/afp/2007/0515/p1523.html|pmid=17555145}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite web||url = http://commonhealth.wbur.org/2012/11/q-tips-ears-danger|title = Why You Really, Truly Should Not Put Q-Tips Into Your Ears|website = commonhealth|access-date = 6 March 2016|archive-date = March 3, 2016|archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20160303043728/http://commonhealth.wbur.org/2012/11/q-tips-ears-danger}}</ref></noinclude>
* The idea that a precise number of stages of grief exist is not supported in peer-reviewed research or objective clinical observation, let alone the [[five stages of grief]] model.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite news||url=http://www.time.com/time/magazine/article/0,9171,2042372-2,00.html|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110131162817/http://www.time.com/time/magazine/article/0,9171,2042372-2,00.html|archive-date=January 31, 2011|title=New Ways to Think About Grief|first=Ruth Davis|last=Konigsberg |date=January 29, 2011|access-date=27 November 2016|via=www.time.com}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal|| vauthors = Corr CA |date=23 October 2018|title=The 'five stages' in coping with dying and bereavement: strengths, weaknesses and some alternatives|journal=Mortality|volume=24|issue=4|pages=405–417|doi=10.1080/13576275.2018.1527826|s2cid=149545381}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||last1=Shermer |first1=Michael |title=Five Fallacies of Grief: Debunking Psychological Stages |url=https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/five-fallacies-of-grief/ |website=www.scientificamerican.com |publisher=[[Scientific American]] |date=1 November 2008 |access-date=29 December 2016 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20200511014903/https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/five-fallacies-of-grief/ |archive-date=11 May 2020 }}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite journal|| vauthors = Wortman CB, Silver RC | title = The myths of coping with loss | journal = Journal of Consulting and Clinical Psychology | volume = 57 | issue = 3 | pages = 349–357 | date = June 1989 | pmid = 2661609 | doi = 10.1037/0022-006x.57.3.349 }}</ref></noinclude>
* {{Convert|98.6|F|C}} is not the normal or average [[Human body temperature|temperature of the human body]]. That figure comes from an 1860 study,<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||url=https://archive.org/details/dasverhaltender00wundgoog|first=Carl Reinhold August|last=Wunderlich|title=Das Verhalten der Eigenwärme in Krankheiten |trans-title=The behavior of the self-warmth in diseases|location=Leipzig|year=1868 <!-- Original print date, per [http://www.uni-leipzig.de/~agintern/uni600/ug174.htm University of Leipzig] -->|publisher=O. Wigand }}; its 1871 2nd edition translated into English and published with the title [https://archive.org/details/ontemperaturein01wundgoog ''On the temperature in diseases: a manual of medical thermometry''].</ref></noinclude> but modern research shows that the average internal temperature is {{convert|36.4|C}}, with small fluctuations.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last1=Weintraub |first1=Karen |title=Are Human Body Temperatures Cooling Down? |url=https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/are-human-body-temperatures-cooling-down/ |website=Scientific American |date=April 2020 |access-date=10 March 2022}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal|| pmid=1302471 | date=1992 | title=A critical appraisal of 98.6 degrees F, the upper limit of the normal body temperature, and other legacies of Carl Reinhold August Wunderlich | journal=JAMA | volume=268 | issue=12 | pages=1578–1580 | doi=10.1001/jama.1992.03490120092034 | vauthors = MacKowiak PA, Wasserman SS, Levine MM }}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| url=https://www.realclearscience.com/blog/2015/08/the_average_body_temperature_is_not_986_degrees.html | title=The Average Body Temperature is Not 98.6 &#124; RealClearScience | date=August 26, 2015 }}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Human microbiome|cells in the human body]] are not outnumbered 10 to 1 by [[microorganism]]s. The 10 to 1 ratio was an estimate made in 1972; current estimates put the ratio at either 3 to 1 or 1.3 to 1.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal|| url=https://www.nature.com/articles/nature.2016.19136 | doi=10.1038/nature.2016.19136 | title=Scientists bust myth that our bodies have more bacteria than human cells | date=2016 | last1=Abbott | first1=Alison | journal=Nature }}<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||last1=Engelhaupt |first1=Erika |title=Gory details: adventures from the dark side of science |title-link=Gory Details |last2=Morrow-Cribbs |first2=Briony |date=2020 |publisher=National geographic partners |isbn=978-1-4262-2097-5 |location=Washington}}<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web|| url=https://www.sciencenews.org/article/bodys-bacteria-dont-outnumber-human-cells-so-much-after-all | title=Body's bacteria don't outnumber human cells so much after all | date=January 8, 2016 }}</ref></noinclude>
* The total length of capillaries in the human body is not 100,000 km. That figure comes from a 1929 book by August Krogh, who used an unrealistically large model person and an inaccurately high density of capillaries. The true number is believed to be between 9,000 and 19,000 km. <noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal|| pmid=33242636 | date=24 Nov 2020 | title=August Krogh: Muscle capillary function and oxygen delivery | journal=Comp Biochem Physiol a Mol Integr Physiol | doi=10.1016/j.cbpa.2020.110852 | vauthors = Poole DC, Kano Y, Koga S, Musch TI | volume=253 | pmc=7867635 }}</ref></noinclude>

==== Disease and preventive healthcare ====
{{See also|Misconceptions about HIV and AIDS|COVID-19 misinformation}}
* [[Tuberculosis]] is not purely a disease of the lungs that has symptoms of coughing. It may instead infect a wide range of other organs in the body.<noinclude><ref>{{cite book |vauthors=Kamboj A, Lause M, Kamboj K |year=2023 |chapter=The Problem of Tuberculosis: Myths, Stigma, and Mimics |veditors=Rezaei N |title=Tuberculosis |series=Integrated Science |volume=11 |publisher=Springer |doi=10.1007/978-3-031-15955-8_50 |pages=1046–1062 |isbn=978-3-031-15954-1}}</ref></noinclude>
* Cancer cannot be treated by restricting food intake and so supposedly "starving" tumors. Rather, the health of people with cancer is best served by maintaining a [[healthy diet]].<noinclude><ref>{{cite journal |vauthors=Grimes DR, O'Riordan E |title=Starving cancer and other dangerous dietary misconceptions |journal=Lancet Oncol |volume=24 |issue=11 |pages=1177–1178 |date=November 2023 |pmid=37922928 |doi=10.1016/S1470-2045(23)00483-7 |url=}}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Common cold#Weather|common cold]] and the [[common flu]] are caused by [[virus]]es, not exposure to cold temperatures. However, low temperatures may somewhat weaken the immune system, and someone already infected with a cold or influenza virus but showing no symptoms can become symptomatic after they are exposed to low temperatures.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||title=Temperature-dependent innate defense against the common cold virus limits viral replication at warm temperature in mouse airway cells |year=2015 |pmc=4311828 |last1=Foxman |first1=E. F. |last2=Storer |first2=J. A. |last3=Fitzgerald |first3=M. E. |last4=Wasik |first4=B. R. |last5=Hou |first5=L. |last6=Zhao |first6=H. |last7=Turner |first7=P. E. |last8=Pyle |first8=A. M. |last9=Iwasaki |first9=A. |journal=Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America |volume=112 |issue=3 |pages=827–832 |doi=10.1073/pnas.1411030112 |pmid=25561542 |bibcode=2015PNAS..112..827F |doi-access=free }}</ref><ref name="Mourtzoukou">{{#invoke:cite journal|| vauthors = Mourtzoukou EG, Falagas ME | title = Exposure to cold and respiratory tract infections | journal = The International Journal of Tuberculosis and Lung Disease | volume = 11 | issue = 9 | pages = 938–43 | date = September 2007 | pmid = 17705968 }}</ref></noinclude> Viruses are more likely to spread during the winter for a variety of reasons such as dry air, less air circulation in homes, people spending more time indoors, and lower [[vitamin D]] levels in humans.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||doi = 10.1177/1090198111407187|pmid = 21586668|title = Age and Ethnic Differences in Cold Weather and Contagion Theories of Colds and Flu|journal = Health Education & Behavior|volume = 39|issue = 1|pages = 67–76 |year = 2012|last1 = Sigelman|first1 = Carol K.|s2cid = 206684728}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal|| pmc=1011011 | pmid=6364570 | volume=139 | issue=6 | title=Traditional health beliefs and practices among lower class black Americans | year=1983 | author=Snow LF | journal=West J Med | pages=820–28}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite journal|| pmc=1011011 | pmid=6364570 | volume=139 | title=Traditional health beliefs and practices among lower class black Americans | year=1983 | author=Snow LF | journal=West J Med | issue=6 | pages=820–28}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://theconversation.com/mondays-medical-myth-you-can-catch-a-cold-by-getting-cold-2488|title=Monday's medical myth: you can catch a cold by getting cold|first=Merlin|last=Thomas|website=The Conversation|date=July 25, 2011 }}
<br />e. {{#invoke:cite web||url=https://blogs.unimelb.edu.au/sciencecommunication/2017/08/17/feeling-cold-causing-colds/|title=Feeling cold causing colds? – Scientific Scribbles}}
<br />f. {{#invoke:cite web||url=https://well.blogs.nytimes.com/2009/06/29/11-health-myths-that-may-surprise-you/|archive-url=https://archive.today/20190418031550/https://well.blogs.nytimes.com/2009/06/29/11-health-myths-that-may-surprise-you/?mtrref=www.google.com&gwh=930ECABDD5CB84FF9CAB5CCC66B35FE7&gwt=pay|title=11 Health Myths That May Surprise You|first=Tara|last=Parker-Pope|date=June 29, 2009|archive-date = April 18, 2019}}

<br />g. {{#invoke:cite web||date=December 6, 2021 |title=Winter Illness Guide |url=https://www.hopkinsmedicine.org/health/conditions-and-diseases/winter-illness-guide |access-date=June 21, 2022 |website=www.hopkinsmedicine.org |language=en}}

<br />h. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Acharya |first1=B. |last2=Thapa |first2=K. |date=January 2016 |title=Indoor Staying During Winter Season Makes People More Susceptible to Flu |journal=[[Journal of Nepal Health Research Council]] |volume=14 |issue=32 |pages=69–70 |issn=1999-6217 |pmid=27426715}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Antibiotics]] will not cure a cold; they treat bacterial diseases and are ineffectual against viruses.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.cdc.gov/antibiotic-use/colds.html|title=Suffering from a cold?|work=Centers for Disease Control and Prevention |date=October 6, 2021}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||url=https://www.fda.gov/drugs/resources-you-drugs/warning-antibiotics-dont-work-viruses-colds-and-flu|title = WARNING: Antibiotics don't work for viruses like colds and the flu|journal = FDA|date = November 3, 2018}}</ref></noinclude> However, they are sometimes prescribed to prevent or treat [[opportunistic infection|secondary infections]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Arroll |first1=B |last2=Kenealy |first2=T |title=Antibiotics for the common cold and acute purulent rhinitis |journal=The Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews |date=October 21, 2002 |issue=3 |pages=CD000247 |doi=10.1002/14651858.CD000247 |pmid=12137610 }}</ref></noinclude>
* There is little to no evidence that any illnesses are curable through [[essential oil]]s or [[aromatherapy]],<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Lee|first1=Myeong Soo|last2=Choi|first2=Jiae Choi|title=Aromatherapy for health care: an overview of systematic reviews|journal=Maturitas|date=2012|volume=3|issue=71|pages=257–260|doi=10.1016/j.maturitas.2011.12.018|pmid=22285469}}</ref></noinclude> and [[Fish oil (medical use)|fish oil]] has not been shown to cure [[dementia]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/do-essential-oils-work-heres-what-science-says/|title=Do Essential Oils Work? Here's What Science Says|publisher=Scientific American|date=March 7, 2020}}</ref></noinclude>
* In those with the common cold, the color of the [[sputum]] or nasal secretion may vary from clear to yellow to green and does not indicate the class of agent causing the infection.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||editor-first=Ronald |editor-last=Eccles |editor2-first=Olaf |editor2-last=Weber|title=Common cold|page=7|date=2009|publisher=Birkhäuser|location=Basel|isbn=978-3-7643-9894-1 }}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Rutter|first=Paul|title=Community pharmacy: symptoms, diagnosis and treatment|date=2009|publisher=Elsevier/Churchill Livingstone|location=Edinburgh|isbn=978-0-7020-2995-0|page=7|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=5nYPvgAACAAJ|edition=2nd}}</ref></noinclude> The color of the sputum is determined by [[White blood cell|immune cells]] fighting an infection in the nasal area.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Davis |first=Daniel M. |date=2021-11-05 |title=Immunology meets the masses|journal=Science |volume=374 |issue=6568 |page=697 |doi=10.1126/science.abm0134 |pmid=34735237 |bibcode=2021Sci...374..697D |s2cid=243761609 |issn=0036-8075}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Vitamin C]] [[Vitamin C and the common cold|does not prevent or treat]] the common cold, although it may have a protective effect during intense cold-weather exercise. If taken daily, it may slightly reduce the duration and severity of colds, but it has no effect if taken after the cold starts.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.webmd.com/cold-and-flu/cold-guide/vitamin-c-for-common-cold |title=Vitamin C for the Common Cold |website=WebMD |access-date=September 27, 2013 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20101024044649/http://www.webmd.com/cold-and-flu/cold-guide/vitamin-c-for-common-cold |archive-date=October 24, 2010 |url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal||title=Vitamin C for preventing and treating the common cold |author=Harri Hemilä |author2=Elizabeth Chalker |journal=[[The Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews]] |date=Jan 2013 |doi=10.1002/14651858.CD000980.pub4 |pmid=23440782 |volume=1 |issue=1 |page=CD000980 |pmc=1160577 }}</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Bombina bombina 1 (Marek Szczepanek).jpg|thumb|The bumps on a toad are not [[wart]]s and cannot cause warts on humans.]]
* Humans cannot catch warts from toads or other animals; the bumps on a toad are not warts.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.webmd.com/skin-problems-and-treatments/warts-faq-questions-answers#1 | title=Warts: 10 Answers to Common Questions}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.londondrugs.com/Cultures/en-US/FocusOnHealth/Fall2002/Warts.htm|title=Putting an End to Warts|publisher=Londondrugs.com|access-date=August 29, 2009 | archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20120609023506/http://www.londondrugs.com/Cultures/en-US/FocusOnHealth/Fall2002/Warts.htm | archive-date = June 9, 2012| url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> [[Wart]]s on human skin are caused by [[human papillomavirus]], which is only known to affect humans.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Pink Book (Human Papillomavirus) |url=https://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/pubs/pinkbook/downloads/hpv.pdf |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20170321110050/https://www.cdc.gov/vaccines/pubs/pinkbook/downloads/hpv.pdf |archive-date=21 March 2017 |access-date=18 April 2017 |website=[[Centers for Disease Control and Prevention]]}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[cracking joints|Cracking one's knuckles]] does not cause [[osteoarthritis]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Rizvi|first1=Asad|last2=Loukas|first2=Marios|last3=Oskouian|first3=Rod J.|last4=Tubbs|first4=R. Shane|date=August 2018|title=Let's get a hand on this: Review of the clinical anatomy of "knuckle cracking"|journal=Clinical Anatomy|language=en|volume=31|issue=6|pages=942–945|doi=10.1002/ca.23243|pmid=30080300|s2cid=51920825|issn=0897-3806}}</ref><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||author=Bosomworth NJ |title=Exercise and knee osteoarthritis: benefit or hazard? |journal=Can Fam Physician |volume=55 |issue=9 |pages=871–78 |date=September 2009 |pmid=19752252 |pmc=2743580}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal||last=Deweber|first=K|author2=Olszewski, M |author3=Ortolano, R |title=Knuckle cracking and hand osteoarthritis|journal=Journal of the American Board of Family Medicine|date=Mar–Apr 2011 |volume=24 |issue=2|pages=169–74|pmid=21383216|doi=10.3122/jabfm.2011.02.100156|doi-access=free}}</ref></noinclude>
* In people with [[eczema]], bathing does not dry the skin as long as a moisturizer is applied soon after. If moisturizer is not applied after bathing, then the evaporation of water from the skin can result in dryness.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||vauthors=Eichenfield LF, Tom WL, Berger TG, Krol A, Paller AS, Schwarzenberger K | display-authors=etal| title=Guidelines of care for the management of atopic dermatitis: section 2. Management and treatment of atopic dermatitis with topical therapies. | journal=J Am Acad Dermatol | year= 2014 | volume= 71 | issue= 1 | pages= 116–32 | pmid=24813302 | doi=10.1016/j.jaad.2014.03.023 | pmc=4326095 }}</ref></noinclude>
* There have never been any programs in the US that provide access to [[Kidney dialysis|dialysis]] machines in exchange for pull tabs on beverage cans.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| url=http://www.snopes.com/business/redeem/pulltabs.asp | title=Keeping Tabs | website=[[Snopes]] | date=March 24, 2012 | access-date=March 30, 2014 | author=Mikkelson, Barbara}}</ref></noinclude> This rumor has existed since at least the 1970s, and usually cites the [[National Kidney Foundation]] as the organization offering the program. The Foundation itself has denied the rumor, noting that dialysis machines are primarily funded by Medicare.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| url=https://www.kidney.org/news/newsroom/newsitemArchive.cfm?id=100 | title=NKF Dispels Pull Tabs for Dialysis Time Rumor | work=National Kidney Foundation | date=June 1, 1998 | access-date=March 30, 2014| archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20140407102004/https://www.kidney.org/news/newsroom/newsitemArchive.cfm?id=100 | archive-date = April 7, 2014| url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* High dietary protein intake is not associated with [[kidney disease]] in healthy people.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal|| url=https://academic.oup.com/ndt/article/35/1/1/5614387 | title=High-protein diet is bad for kidney health: unleashing the taboo | journal=Nephrology Dialysis Transplantation | date=November 7, 2019 | access-date=May 5, 2022 | author=Kamyar Kalantar-Zadeh| volume=35 | issue=1 | pages=1–4 | doi=10.1093/ndt/gfz216 | pmid=31697325 }}</ref></noinclude> While significantly increased protein intake in the short-term is associated with changes in renal function, there is no evidence to suggest this effect persists in the long-term and results in kidney damage or disease.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| url=https://examine.com/nutrition/can-eating-too-much-protein-be-bad-for-you/ | title=Can eating too much protein be bad for you? | website=Examine.com | date=November 2, 2020 | access-date=May 5, 2022 | author=Kamal Patel}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Rhinoceros horn]] in powdered form is not used as an [[aphrodisiac]] in [[traditional Chinese medicine]] as ''Cornu Rhinoceri Asiatici'' (犀角, ''xījiǎo'', "rhinoceros horn"). It is prescribed for fevers and convulsions,<noinclude><ref>Bensky, Dan; Clavey, Steven; Stoger, Erich and Gamble, Andrew (2004) ''Chinese Herbal Medicine: Materia Medica'', 3rd Edition. Eastland Press. {{ISBN|978-0-939616-42-8}}</ref></noinclude> a treatment not supported by [[evidence-based medicine]].
* [[Leprosy]] is not auto-degenerative as commonly supposed, meaning that it will not (on its own) cause body parts to be damaged or fall off.<noinclude><ref name="Solnit 2013">{{Cite magazine |url=https://harpers.org/archive/2013/06/the-separating-sickness/ |title=The Separating Sickness |first=Rebecca |last=Solnit |magazine=Harper's Magazine |date=June 12, 2013 |access-date=29 July 2024}}</ref></noinclude> Leprosy causes rashes to form and may degrade cartilage and, if untreated, [[inflammation|inflame tissue]]. In addition, leprosy is only mildly contagious, partly because 95% of those infected with the mycobacteria that causes leprosy do not develop the disease.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.merckmanuals.com/home/infections/tuberculosis-and-related-infections/leprosy|title=Leprosy – Infections|website=Merck Manuals Consumer Version}}</ref><ref name="Solnit 2013" /></noinclude> [[Tzaraath]], a Biblical disease that disfigures the skin, is often identified as leprosy, and may be the source of many myths about the disease.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Grzybowski|first1=Andrzej|last2=Nita|first2=Małgorzata|title=Leprosy in the Bible|journal=Clinics in Dermatology|volume=34|issue=1|year=2016|pages=3–7|issn=0738-081X |doi=10.1016/j.clindermatol.2015.10.003 |pmid=26773616}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||title=HaTalmud V'Chachmas HaRefuah|location=Berlin|year=1928|page=323|publisher=JALKUT G.m.b.H.|first=Katznelson|last=Yehuda Liv Binyamin Ben Israel}} [http://hebrewbooks.org/20797 Title at hebrewbooks.org, pp. 339-340.]</ref></noinclude>
* [[Rust]] itself does not cause [[tetanus]] infection. The ''[[Clostridium tetani]]'' bacterium is generally found in dirty environments that lack oxygen. Since the same conditions that harbor tetanus bacteria also promote rusting of metal, many people associate rust with tetanus. ''C. tetani'' requires [[hypoxia (environmental)|anoxic]] conditions to reproduce and these are found in the permeable layers of rust that form on oxygen-absorbing, unprotected ironwork.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Tetanus – Can a Rusty Nail Cause Tetanus?|url=http://www.ehso.com/disease/tetanus.php|website=Environmental Safety and Health Online|access-date=November 9, 2015}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Quarantine]] has never been a standard procedure for those with [[severe combined immunodeficiency]], despite the condition's popular nickname ("bubble boy syndrome") and its portrayal in films. A [[bone marrow transplant]] in the earliest months of life is the standard course of treatment. The exceptional case of [[David Vetter]], who lived much of his life encased in a sterile environment because he would not receive a transplant until age 12, was an inspiration for the "bubble boy" trope.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://www.cnn.com/2019/04/17/health/bubble-boy-disease-cure-study/index.html|title=Doctors develop 'cure' for babies with 'bubble boy' disease|author=Jacqueline Howard|publisher=CNN|date=April 17, 2019}}</ref></noinclude>
* Statements in [[medication package insert]]s listing the frequency of [[side effect]]s describe how often the effect occurs after taking a drug, but are not making any assertion that there is a causal connection between taking the drug and the occurrence of the side effect. In other words, what is being reported on is correlation, not necessarily causation.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Mühlbauer|first1=Viktoria |last2=Prinz |first2=Roman|last3=Mühlhauser|first3=Ingrid|last4=Wegwarth|first4=Odette|date=September 13, 2018|title=Alternative package leaflets improve people's understanding of drug side effects{{snd}}A randomized controlled exploratory survey |journal=PLOS ONE|volume=13|issue=9|page=e0203800|doi=10.1371/journal.pone.0203800|issn=1932-6203|pmc=6136776|pmid=30212555|bibcode=2018PLoSO..1303800M|doi-access=free}}</ref></noinclude>
* There is no peer-reviewed scientific evidence that [[crystal healing]] has any effect beyond acting as a placebo.<noinclude><ref name="LS">{{#invoke:cite web||date=June 23, 2017 |title=Live Science |url=https://www.livescience.com/40347-crystal-healing.html |access-date=July 29, 2018 |website=[[Live Science]]}}</ref><ref name="Spellman">Spellman, Frank R; Price-Bayer, Joni. (2010). ''In Defense of Science: Why Scientific Literacy Matters''. The Scarecrow Press. p. 81. {{ISBN|978-1-60590-735-2}} "There is no scientific evidence that crystal healing has any effect. It has been called a pseudoscience. Pleasant feelings or the apparent successes of crystal healing can be attributed to the placebo effect or cognitive bias—a believer wanting it to be true."</ref><ref name="Regal">Regal, Brian. (2009). ''Pseudoscience: A Critical Encyclopedia''. Greenwood. p. 51. {{ISBN|978-0-313-35507-3}}</ref></noinclude>
* There is a scientific consensus<noinclude><ref>a.
{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Nicolia |first1=Alessandro |last2=Manzo |first2=Alberto |last3=Veronesi |first3=Fabio |last4=Rosellini |first4=Daniele |date=March 2014 |title=An overview of the last 10 years of genetically engineered crop safety research |journal=[[Critical Reviews in Biotechnology]] |volume=34 |issue=1 |page=84 |doi=10.3109/07388551.2013.823595 |pmid=24041244 }}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal||last=Ronald |first=Pamela |date=May 1, 2011 |title=Plant Genetics, Sustainable Agriculture and Global Food Security |journal=[[Genetics (journal)|Genetics]] |volume=188 |issue=1 |page=12 |doi=10.1534/genetics.111.128553|pmid=21546547 |pmc=3120150 }}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Domingo |first1=José L |last2=Bordonaba |first2=Jordi Giné |date=2011 |title=A literature review on the safety assessment of genetically modified plants |url=https://gaiapresse.ca/images/nouvelles/28563.pdf |journal=[[Environment International]] |volume=37 |issue=4 |page=741 |doi=10.1016/j.envint.2011.01.003|pmid=21296423 |bibcode=2011EnInt..37..734D }}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Freedman |first=David H |date=September 1, 2013 |title=The Truth about Genetically Modified Food |url=https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/the-truth-about-genetically-modified-food/ |access-date=July 6, 2024 |work=[[Scientific American]]}}</ref></noinclude> that currently available food derived from [[Genetically modified food|genetically modified crops]] poses no greater risk to human health than conventional food.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||date=October 20, 2012 |title=Statement by the AAAS Board of Directors On Labeling of Genetically Modified Foods |url=https://www.aaas.org/sites/default/files/AAAS_GM_statement.pdf |access-date=July 7, 2024 |publisher=[[American Association for the Advancement of Science]]}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||author1=European Commission. Directorate-General for Research|url=http://ec.europa.eu/research/biosociety/pdf/a_decade_of_eu-funded_gmo_research.pdf|title=A decade of EU-funded GMO research (2001–2010)|date=2010|publisher=Directorate-General for Research and Innovation. Biotechnologies, Agriculture, Food. European Commission, European Union.|isbn=978-92-79-16344-9|doi=10.2777/97784|access-date=August 30, 2019}}
c. {{Cite report |url=http://www.ama-assn.org/resources/doc/csaph/a12-csaph2-bioengineeredfoods.pdf |title=Report 2 of the Council on Science and Public Health (A-12): Labeling of Bioengineered Foods |date=2012 |publisher=[[American Medical Association]] |access-date=July 7, 2024 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120907023039/http://www.ama-assn.org/resources/doc/csaph/a12-csaph2-bioengineeredfoods.pdf |archive-date=September 7, 2012 }}
