In fact, rotation is determined by whatever minor rotation is initially present at the time the water starts to drain, as the magnitude of the coriolis acceleration is [[rossby number|negligibly small]] compared to the inertial acceleration of flow within a typical basin.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Bad Coriolis |url=http://www.ems.psu.edu/~fraser/Bad/BadCoriolis.html |access-date=2016-12-21 |work=www.ems.psu.edu}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||date=April 28, 2003 |title=Flush Bosh |url=http://www.snopes.com/science/coriolis.asp |access-date=2016-12-21 |work=[[Snopes]]}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||date=2009-07-20 |title=Does the rotation of the Earth affect toilets and baseball games? |url=http://science.howstuffworks.com/science-vs-myth/everyday-myths/rotation-earth-toilet-baseball2.htm |access-date=2016-12-21}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Can somebody finally settle this question: Does water flowing down a drain spin in different directions depending on which hemisphere you're in? And if so, why? |url=https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/can-somebody-finally-sett/ |access-date=2016-12-21 |work=www.scientificamerican.com}}
<br />e. {{#invoke:cite book||author1=Larry D. Kirkpatrick |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=8hOLs-bmiYYC&pg=PA168 |title=Physics: A World View |author2=Gregory E. Francis |publisher=Cengage Learning |year=2006 |isbn=978-0-495-01088-3 |pages=168–9}}
<br />f. {{#invoke:cite journal||author1=Y. A. Stepanyants |author2=G. H. Yeoh |year=2008 |title=Stationary bathtub vortices and a critical regime of liquid discharge |url=http://eprints.usq.edu.au/5726/2/Stepanyants_Yeoh_2008_PV.pdf |journal=Journal of Fluid Mechanics |volume=604 |issue=1 |pages=77–98 |bibcode=2008JFM...604...77S |doi=10.1017/S0022112008001080 |s2cid=53071268 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220723091453/https://eprints.usq.edu.au/5726/2/Stepanyants_Yeoh_2008_PV.pdf |archive-date=July 23, 2022 |access-date=July 23, 2022}}
<br />g. {{#invoke:cite book||author=Creative Media Applications |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=fF0TTZVQuZoC&pg=PA22 |title=A Student's Guide to Earth Science: Words and terms |publisher=Greenwood Publishing Group |year=2004 |isbn=978-0-313-32902-9 |page=22}}</ref></noinclude>
* Neither [[Bicycle and motorcycle dynamics#Gyroscopic effects|gyroscopic forces]] nor [[Trail (steering)|geometric trail]] are required for a rider to [[bicycle and motorcycle dynamics#Other hypotheses|balance]] a bicycle or for it to demonstrate [[Bicycle and motorcycle dynamics#Self-stability|self-stability]].<noinclude><ref name="KMPRS">{{#invoke:cite journal||journal=[[Science (journal)|Science]] |author=J. D. G. Kooijman |author2=J. P. Meijaard |author3=J. M. Papadopoulos| author4=A. Ruina |author5=A. L. Schwab |date=April 15, 2011 |title=A bicycle can be self-stable without gyroscopic or caster effects |volume=332 |issue=6027 |pages=339–342 |doi=10.1126/science.1201959 |pmid=21493856 |bibcode=2011Sci...332..339K |s2cid=12296078 |url=http://bicycle.tudelft.nl/stablebicycle/StableBicyclev34Revised.pdf |access-date=January 22, 2013}}</ref><ref name="BikeGyro">a. {{#invoke:cite book||title=Bicycling Science|edition=2nd|last1=Whitt|first1=Frank R.|first2=David G.|last2=Wilson|date=1982|publisher=Massachusetts Institute of Technology|isbn=978-0-262-23111-4|pages=198–233}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Klein |first=Richard E. |display-authors=etal |title=Bicycle Science |url=http://www.losethetrainingwheels.org/default.aspx?Lev=2&ID=34 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20061010070125/http://www.losethetrainingwheels.org/default.aspx?Lev=2&ID=34 |archive-date=October 10, 2006 |access-date=August 4, 2006 |publisher=LoseTheTrainingWheels.org}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Jones |first1=David E. H. |date=1970 |title=The Stability of the Bicycle |url=http://socrates.berkeley.edu/%7Efajans/Teaching/MoreBikeFiles/JonesBikeBW.pdf |journal=Physics Today |volume=23 |issue=4 |pages=34–40 |bibcode=1970PhT....23d..34J |doi=10.1063/1.3022064}}</ref></noinclude> Although gyroscopic forces and trail can be contributing factors, it [[Two-mass-skate bicycle|has been demonstrated]] that those factors are neither required nor sufficient by themselves.<noinclude><ref name="KMPRS" /></noinclude>
* A penny dropped from the [[Empire State Building]] would not kill a person or crack the sidewalk. A penny is too light and has too much air resistance to acquire enough speed to do much damage since it reaches [[terminal velocity]] after falling about {{convert|50|ft|0|disp=flip}}. Heavier or more aerodynamic objects could cause significant damage if dropped from that height.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Could a Penny Dropped off a Skyscraper Actually Kill You? |author=Wolchover, Natalie|date=March 5, 2012|url=https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/could-a-penny-dropped-off/ |website=[[Scientific American]]}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=What would happen if you were hit by a penny falling from a skyscraper?|url=https://www.usatoday.com/story/news/nation-now/2017/03/15/what-would-happen-if-you-were-hit-penny-falling-skyscraper/99160982/|website=[[USA Today]]}}</ref></noinclude>
* Using a [[programmable thermostat]]'s setback feature to limit heating or cooling in a temporarily unoccupied building does not waste as much energy as leaving the temperature constant. Using setback saves energy (5–15%) because [[heat transfer]] across the surface of the building is roughly proportional to the temperature difference between its inside and the outside.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Thermostats|website=Energy.gov |url=https://www.energy.gov/energysaver/thermostats}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.achrnews.com/articles/98114-programmable-thermostat-myths-know-the-facts-and-boost-your-profits|title=Programmable thermostat myths: Know the facts and boost your profits|website=www.achrnews.com}}</ref></noinclude>
* It is not possible for a person to completely submerge in quicksand, as commonly depicted in fiction,<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Quicksand myth exposed|author=Patricia Reaney|date=September 29, 2005|agency=Reuters |url=https://www.abc.net.au/science/articles/2005/09/29/1471116.htm|website=www.abc.net.au|language=en-AU|access-date=April 8, 2020}}</ref></noinclude> although sand entrapment in the nearshore of a body of water can be a drowning hazard as the tide rises.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.bbc.com/future/article/20160323-can-quicksand-really-suck-you-to-your-death|title=Can quicksand really suck you to your death?|last=Hammond|first=Claudia|website=www.bbc.com|language=en|access-date=April 8, 2020}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Quantum nonlocality]] caused by [[quantum entanglement]] does not allow [[faster-than-light communication]] or imply instant [[action at a distance]], despite its common characterization as "spooky action at a distance". Rather, it means that [[Bell test|certain experiments]] cannot be explained by [[local realism]].<noinclude><ref name="ASPECT">{{#invoke:cite journal||author1=Aspect, Alain |author2=Dalibard, Jean |author3=Roger, Gérard |date=December 1982 |title=Experimental Test of Bell's Inequalities Using Time- Varying Analyzers |journal=Physical Review Letters |volume=49 |issue=25| pages=1804–1807| doi=10.1103/PhysRevLett.49.1804| bibcode=1982PhRvL..49.1804A|doi-access=free}}</ref><ref name='Bohr1935'>{{#invoke:cite journal||title=Can Quantum-Mechanical Description of Physical Reality be Considered Complete? |date=October 13, 1935 |first=N. |last=Bohr |journal=[[Physical Review]] |volume=48 |issue=8 |pages=696–702 |doi=10.1103/PhysRev.48.696|bibcode=1935PhRv...48..696B |url=https://cds.cern.ch/record/1060284/files/PhysRev.48.696.pdf |doi-access=free}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Ice#Friction properties|The slipperiness of ice]] is not due to [[Pressure melting point|pressure melting]]. While it is true that increased pressure, such as that exerted by someone standing on a sheet of ice, will lower the melting point of ice, experiments show that the effect is too weak to account for the lowered friction. [[Materials science|Materials scientists]] still debate whether [[Premelting#Ice skating|premelting or the heat of friction]] is the dominant cause of ice's slipperiness.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||title=Pressure melting and ice skating |vauthors=Colbeck SC |journal=American Journal of Physics |volume=63 |page=888 |date=1995 |issue=10 |quote=Pressure melting cannot be responsible for the low friction of ice. The pressure needed to reach the melting temperature is above the compressive failure stress..." |doi=10.1119/1.18028|bibcode=1995AmJPh..63..888C }}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Explaining Ice: The Answers Are Slippery |author=Kenneth Chang |date=Feb 21, 2006 |url=https://www.nytimes.com/2006/02/21/science/21ice.html |url-access=subscription |work=The New York Times |quote=According to the frequently cited — if incorrect — explanation of why ice is slippery under an ice skate, the pressure exerted along the blade lowers the melting temperature of the top layer of ice, the ice melts and the blade glides on a thin layer of water that refreezes to ice as soon as the blade passes... But the explanation fails... because the pressure-melting effect is small.}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite journal||author=Robert Rosenberg |url=https://www.asc.ohio-state.edu/jayaprakash.1/846/skating.pdf |date=December 2005 |title=Why is Ice slippery? |journal=Physics Today |pages=50–55}}</ref><ref name="Bl">{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Bluhm |first=H. |author2=T. Inoue |author3=M. Salmeron |date=2000 |title=Friction of ice measured using lateral force microscopy |journal=Phys. Rev. B |volume=61 |issue=11 |page=7760 |bibcode=2000PhRvB..61.7760B |doi=10.1103/PhysRevB.61.7760 |url=https://zenodo.org/record/1233739}}</ref></noinclude>

=== Psychology and neuroscience ===
* [[Cannabis in pregnancy|Cannabis use in pregnancy]] is not low risk. The tetrahydrocannabinol ([[THC]]) within cannabis crosses the placenta, directly exposing the developing fetus to this chemical. Cannabis use in pregnancy is linked to increased risk of preterm delivery and lower birth weight, along with a higher likelihood of infants needing placement in the neonatal intensive care unit.<noinclude><ref>{{Cite journal |last1=Gunn |first1=J. K. L. |last2=Rosales |first2=C. B. |last3=Center |first3=K. E. |last4=Nuñez |first4=A. |last5=Gibson |first5=S. J. |last6=Christ |first6=C. |last7=Ehiri |first7=J. E. |date=2016-04-01 |title=Prenatal exposure to cannabis and maternal and child health outcomes: a systematic review and meta-analysis |url=https://bmjopen.bmj.com/content/6/4/e009986 |journal=BMJ Open |language=en |volume=6 |issue=4 |pages=e009986 |doi=10.1136/bmjopen-2015-009986 |issn=2044-6055 |pmid=27048634|pmc=4823436 }}</ref></noinclude> Additionally, infants exposed to cannabis in pregnancy may have increased rates of behavioral conditions such as hyperactivity and impulsivity, attention deficits, sleep disorders, emotional disturbances, and a higher chance of substance use.<noinclude><ref>{{Cite journal |last1=Renard |first1=Justine |last2=Konefal |first2=Sarah |date=2022 |title=Clearing the Smoke on Cannabis: Cannabis Use During Pregnancy and Breastfeeding - An Update |url=https://www.ccsa.ca/sites/default/files/2022-05/CCSA-Cannabis-Use-Pregnancy-Breastfeeding-Report-2022-en.pdf |journal=Canadian Centre on Substance Use and Addiction}}</ref></noinclude> As of 2015, 70% of American women thought that consumption of cannabis once or twice per week  while pregnant is harmless.<noinclude><ref name="pmid25772211">{{cite journal| author=Ko JY| author2=Farr SL|author3=Tong VT|author4=Creanga AA|author5=Callaghan WM| title=Prevalence and patterns of marijuana use among pregnant and nonpregnant women of reproductive age. | journal=Am J Obstet Gynecol | year= 2015 | volume= 213 | issue= 2 | pages= 201.e1–201.e10 | pmid=25772211 | doi=10.1016/j.ajog.2015.03.021 | pmc=7469257 }} </ref></noinclude>
* True [[Eidetic memory|photographic memory]] (the ability to remember endless images, particularly pages or numbers, with such a high degree of precision that the image mimics a photo) has never been demonstrated to exist in any individual,<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Photographic Memory |url=http://indianapublicmedia.org/amomentofscience/photographic-memory/|publisher=indianapublicmedia.org |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140427082738/http://indianapublicmedia.org/amomentofscience/photographic-memory/ |archive-date=April 27, 2014|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> although a small number of young children have [[eidetic memory]], where they can recall an object with high precision for a few minutes after it is no longer present.<noinclude><ref name="Simola">{{#invoke:cite book||author=Anthony Simola|title=The Roving Mind: A Modern Approach to Cognitive Enhancement|publisher=ST Press|isbn=978-0-692-40905-3|year=2015|page=117|access-date=May 10, 2016 |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=p4ZMBwAAQBAJ&pg=PT117}}</ref></noinclude>  Many people have claimed to have a photographic memory, but those people have been shown to have high precision memories as a result of [[mnemonic]] devices rather than a natural capacity for detailed memory encoding.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.slate.com/id/2140685/ |title=Kaavya Syndrome: The accused Harvard plagiarist doesn't have a photographic memory. No one does |author=Foer, Joshua |date=April 27, 2006 |work=Slate |access-date=January 20, 2013 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110924094531/http://www.slate.com/id/2140685 |archive-date=September 24, 2011 |url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> There are rare cases of individuals with [[exceptional memory]], but none of them have a memory that mimics that of a camera.
* The phase of the Moon does not influence fertility, cause a fluctuation in crime, or affect the stock market. There is no correlation between the [[Lunar effect|lunar cycle]] and human biology or behavior. However, the increased amount of illumination during the full moon may account for increased epileptic episodes, motorcycle accidents, or sleep disorders.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Martens|first1=R.|last2=Kelly|first2=I. W.|last3=Saklofske|first3=D. H.|date=December 1988|title=Lunar Phase and Birthrate: A 50-Year Critical Review|journal=Psychological Reports|volume=63|issue=3|pages=923–934|doi=10.2466/pr0.1988.63.3.923|pmid=3070616|s2cid=34184527}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Rotton|first1=James|last2=Kelly|first2=I. W.|date=1985|title=Much ado about the full moon: A meta-analysis of lunar-lunacy research|journal=Psychological Bulletin|volume=97|issue=2|pages=286–306|doi=10.1037/0033-2909.97.2.286|pmid=3885282}}
<br />c. {{Citation |last1=Kelly |first1=Ivan |title=The Moon Was Full and Nothing Happened: A Review of Studies on the Moon and Human Behavior |date=1986 |journal=Skeptical Inquirer |volume=10 |issue=2 |pages=129–43 |last2=Rotton |first2=James |last3=Culver |first3=Roger}}. Reprinted in ''The Hundredth Monkey – and other paradigms of the paranormal'', edited by [[Kendrick Frazier]], Prometheus Books. Revised and updated in ''The Outer Edge: Classic Investigations of the Paranormal'', edited by [[Joe Nickell]], [[Barry Karr]], and [[Tom Genoni]], 1996, [[CSICOP]].
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Foster|first1=Russell G.|last2=Roenneberg|first2=Till|date=2008|title=Human Responses to the Geophysical Daily, Annual and Lunar Cycles|journal=Current Biology|volume=18|issue=17|pages=R784–R794|bibcode=2008CBio...18.R784F|doi=10.1016/j.cub.2008.07.003|pmid=18786384|s2cid=15429616|doi-access=free}}</ref></noinclude>

==== Mental disorders ====
* [[Vaccines and autism|Vaccines do not cause autism]]. There have been no successful attempts to [[Reproducibility|reproduce]] [[MMR vaccine controversy|fraudulent research]] by British ex-doctor [[Andrew Wakefield]], where the misconception likely originates. Wakefield's research was ultimately shown to have been manipulated.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Godlee |first1=F. |last2=Smith |first2=J. |last3=Marcovitch |first3=H. |year=2011 |title=British Medical Journal: Wakefield's article linking MMR vaccine and autism was fraudulent |url=http://www.bmj.com/content/342/bmj.c7452.full |url-status=live |journal=BMJ |volume=342 |page=c7452 |doi=10.1136/bmj.c7452 |pmid=21209060 |s2cid=43640126 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131111093448/http://www.bmj.com/content/342/bmj.c7452.full |archive-date=November 11, 2013 |access-date=January 5, 2011}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Dyslexia]] is not defined or diagnosed as [[mirror writing]] or reading letters or words backwards.<noinclude><ref name="LilienfeldLynn2011">{{#invoke:cite book||last1=Lilienfeld |first1=Scott O. |author-link1=Scott Lilienfeld |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=8DlS0gfO_QUC&pg=PT88 |title=50 Great Myths of Popular Psychology: Shattering Widespread Misconceptions about Human Behavior |last2=Lynn |first2=Steven Jay |last3=Ruscio |first3=John |last4=Beyerstein |first4=Barry L. |author-link4=Barry Beyerstein |date=15 September 2011 |publisher=[[Wiley (publisher)|John Wiley & Sons]] |isbn=978-1-4443-6074-5 |pages=88–91 |access-date=19 May 2016 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20170109130327/https://books.google.com/books?id=8DlS0gfO_QUC&pg=PT88 |archive-date=9 January 2017 |url-status=live}}</ref><ref name="Handler2011">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Handler |first1=Sheryl M |last2=Fierson |first2=Walter M |date=March 1, 2011 |title=Learning disabilities, dyslexia, and vision. |journal=Pediatrics |publisher=[[American Academy of Pediatrics]] |volume=127 |issue=3 |pages=e820 |doi=10.1542/peds.2010-3670 |pmid=21357342 |doi-access=free}}</ref></noinclude> Mirror writing and reading letters or words backwards are behaviors seen in many children (dyslexic or not) as they learn to read and write.<noinclude><ref name="LilienfeldLynn2011" /><noinclude><ref name="Handler2011" /></noinclude> [[Dyslexia]] is a neurodevelopmental disorder of people who have at least average intelligence and who have difficulty in reading and writing that is not otherwise explained by low intelligence.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Dinelli |first=Beth |title=Common Misconceptions about Dyslexia |url=https://www.commlearn.com/common-misconceptions-about-dyslexia/ |access-date=August 2, 2019 |website=Commonwealth Learning Center}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Self-harm]] is not generally an [[attention-seeking]] behavior. People who engage in self-harm are typically very self-conscious of their wounds and scars and feel guilty about their behavior, leading them to go to great lengths to conceal it from others.<noinclude><ref name="MHF">{{#invoke:cite book||url=http://www.mentalhealth.org.uk/publications/?EntryId5=38712 |title=Truth Hurts Report |publisher=Mental Health Foundation |year=2006 |isbn=978-1-903645-81-9 |access-date=June 11, 2008}}</ref></noinclude> They may offer alternative explanations for their injuries, or conceal their scars with clothing.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||author=Helen Spandler |title=Who's Hurting Who? Young people, self-harm and suicide |publisher=42nd Street |year=1996 |isbn=978-1-900782-00-5 |place=Manchester}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||url=https://archive.org/details/selfharmperspect0000unse |title=Self-harm – Perspectives from personal experience |publisher=Chipmunka/Survivors Speak Out |year=1994 |isbn=978-1-904697-04-6 |editor=Pembroke, L. R. |url-access=registration}}</ref></noinclude>
* There is no evidence that a chemical imbalance or neurotransmitter deficiency is the sole factor in depression and other mental disorders, but rather a combination of biological, psychological, and social factors.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last=Stahl |first=S. M. |title=Stahl's essential psychopharmacology: Neuroscientific basis and practical applications |publisher=Cambridge University Press |year=2021 |isbn=978-1-108-98585-7 |edition=5th |page=264}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=March 17, 2022 |title=The Myth of the Chemical Imbalance {{!}} Ronald Pies |url=https://return.life/2022/03/17/the-myth-of-the-chemical-imbalance/ |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20221201222841/https://return.life/2022/03/17/the-myth-of-the-chemical-imbalance/ |archive-date=1 December 2022 |access-date=June 1, 2022 |website=Return}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Schizophrenia]] is characterized by continuous or relapsing episodes of psychosis. Major symptoms include hallucinations (typically hearing voices), delusions, paranoia, and disorganized thinking. Other symptoms include social withdrawal, decreased emotional expression, and apathy.<noinclude><ref>Diagnostic and Statistical Manual 5th edition.</ref></noinclude> The term was coined from the Greek roots ''schizein'' and ''phrēn'', "to split" and "mind", in reference to a "splitting of mental functions" seen in schizophrenia, not a splitting of the personality.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||author=Baucum, Don |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=iCVSdYdA36EC&pg=PA182 |title=Psychology |publisher=Barron's |year=2006 |isbn=978-0-7641-3421-0 |edition=2nd |location=Hauppauge, NY |page=182}}</ref></noinclude> It does not involve split or multiple personalities—a split or multiple personality is [[dissociative identity disorder]].<noinclude><ref name="MultiplePersonalities">a. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Schizophrenia |url=http://www.nami.org/Template.cfm?Section=Schizophrenia9&Template=/ContentManagement/ContentDisplay.cfm&ContentID=118290 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120504155431/http://www.nami.org/Template.cfm?Section=Schizophrenia9&Template=%2FContentManagement%2FContentDisplay.cfm&ContentID=118290 |archive-date=May 4, 2012 |publisher=National Alliance on Mental Illness}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||title=10 Myths About Mental Illness |url=http://www.mha-oc.org/10myths.html |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110519221144/http://www.mha-oc.org/10myths.html |archive-date=May 19, 2011 |url-status=usurped |publisher=Mental Health Association}}</ref></noinclude>

==== Brain ====
* Broad generalizations are often made in popular psychology about certain brain functions being [[Lateralization of brain function|lateralized]], or more predominant in one hemisphere than the other. These claims are often inaccurate or overstated.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Goswami |first1=U |title=Neuroscience and education: from research to practice? |journal=Nature Reviews. Neuroscience |volume=7 |issue=5 |pages=406–11 |date=2006 |pmid=16607400 |doi=10.1038/nrn1907|s2cid=3113512 }}</ref></noinclude>
* The human brain, particularly the prefrontal cortex, does not reach [[Neuroplasticity|"full maturity" or "full development"]] at any particular age (e.g. 16, 18, 21, 25, 30). Changes in structure and myelination of gray matter are recorded to continue with relative consistency all throughout life including until death.  Different mental abilities peak earlier or later in life.<noinclude><ref>a. More modern research suggests that the prefrontal cortex region of the brain is changing in structure even well past the age of 30.{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Hartshorne |first1=Joshua K. |last2=Germine |first2=Laura T. |title=When Does Cognitive Functioning Peak? The Asynchronous Rise and Fall of Different Cognitive Abilities Across the Life Span |journal=Psychological Science |date=April 2015 |volume=26 |issue=4 |pages=433–443 |doi=10.1177/0956797614567339 |pmid=25770099 |pmc=4441622|quote="Some abilities peak and begin to decline around high school graduation; some abilities plateau in early adulthood, beginning to decline in subjects' 30s; and still others do not peak until subjects reach their 40s or later. These findings motivate a nuanced theory of maturation and age-related decline, in which multiple, dissociable factors differentially affect different domains of cognition." }}
b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.menshealth.com/health/a26868313/when-does-your-brain-fully-mature/ |title=People's Brains Don't Reach Adulthood Until Age 30, Study Finds|website=Men's Health|first=MEelissa|last=Matthews |date=March 19, 2019|quote="What we're really saying is that to have a definition of when you move from childhood to adulthood looks increasingly absurd... It's a much more nuanced transition that takes place over three decades."}}<br />
c. {{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.independent.co.uk/news/science/adult-brain-30s-teenagers-crime-mental-health-research-a8828681.html |title=Scientists think you are not a proper adult until you enter your 30s |website=[[Independent.co.uk]] |date=March 18, 2019}}<br />
d. {{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/health/news/8204782/Brain-only-fully-matures-in-middle-age-claims-neuroscientist.html#:~:text=You%20might%20think%20that%20you,developing%20until%20your%20late%2040s.&text=Scientists%20used%20to%20believe%20that,changing%20well%20into%20middle%20age | title=Brain only fully 'matures' in middle age, claims neuroscientist| date=December 16, 2010}}<br />
e. {{#invoke:cite journal||doi=10.1073/pnas.1105108108 |title=Extraordinary neoteny of synaptic spines in the human prefrontal cortex |year=2011 |last1=Petanjek |first1=Zdravko |last2=Judaš |first2=Miloš |last3=Šimić |first3=Goran |last4=Rašin |first4=Mladen Roko |last5=Uylings |first5=Harry B. M. |last6=Rakic |first6=Pasko |last7=Kostović |first7=Ivica |journal=Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences |volume=108 |issue=32 |pages=13281–13286 |pmid=21788513 |pmc=3156171 |bibcode=2011PNAS..10813281P |doi-access=free}}<br />
f. {{cite magazine |url=https://slate.com/human-interest/2020/01/age-of-consent-25-advice.html |title=My Progressive Friends Say It's Immoral to Have Sex With Anyone Under 25 |first=Stoya |last=Juzwiak |author2=Rich Juzwiak |date=January 9, 2020 |magazine=Slate}}<br />
g. {{#invoke:cite news||url=https://www.nytimes.com/2010/08/22/magazine/22Adulthood-t.html|title=What Is It About 20-Somethings?|newspaper=The New York Times|first=Robin Marantz |last=Henig|date=August 18, 2010}}<br />
h. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Cockcroft |first1=Kate |title=The role of working memory in childhood education: Five questions and answers |journal=South African Journal of Childhood Education |date=4 September 2015 |volume=5 |issue=1 |page=18 |id={{ProQuest|1898641293}} |doi=10.4102/sajce.v5i1.347 |doi-access=free }} <br />
i. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Hartshorne |first1=Joshua K. |last2=Germine |first2=Laura T. |title=When Does Cognitive Functioning Peak? The Asynchronous Rise and Fall of Different Cognitive Abilities Across the Life Span |journal=Psychological Science |date=April 2015 |volume=26 |issue=4 |pages=433–443 |doi=10.1177/0956797614567339 |pmid=25770099 |pmc=4441622 }}<br />
j. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Hu |first=Jane C. |date=2022-11-28 |title=A Powerful Idea About Our Brains Stormed Pop Culture and Captured Minds. It's Mostly Bunk. |url=https://slate.com/technology/2022/11/brain-development-25-year-old-mature-myth.html |access-date=2022-11-28 |website=Slate Magazine |language=en}}</ref></noinclude> The myth is believed to have originated from Jay Giedd's work on the adolescent brain funded by the National Campaign to Prevent Teen Pregnancy,<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| url=https://www.iflscience.com/does-the-brain-really-mature-at-the-age-of-25-68979 | title=Does the Brain Really Mature at the Age of 25? | date=May 19, 2023 }}</ref></noinclude> though it has also been popularized by [[Laurence Steinberg]] in his work with adolescent criminal reform who has considered ages 10–25 to constitute cognitive adolescence, despite denying any connection to the notion of the brain maturing at "25".<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news|| url=https://www.theglobeandmail.com/life/parenting/redefining-teenagers-and-the-adolescent-mind/article20789085/ | title=Why we need to treat adolescents in a radical new way | newspaper=The Globe and Mail | date=September 25, 2014 }}</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Gyrus Dentatus 40x.jpg|thumb|Some neurons can reform in the human brain.]]
* Humans do not generate all of the brain cells they will ever have by the age of two years. Although this belief was held by medical experts until 1998, it is now understood that new [[neuron]]s can be created [[postnatal|after infancy]] in some parts of the brain into late adulthood.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal|| author = Gross C. G. | date = 2000 | title = Neurogenesis in the adult brain: death of a dogma | journal = Nat Rev Neurosci | volume = 1 | issue = 1| pages = 67–73 | doi = 10.1038/35036235| pmid = 11252770 | s2cid = 2347812 }}</ref></noinclude>
* People do not use [[Ten percent of the brain myth|only 10% of their brains]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.snopes.com/science/stats/10percent.asp|title=Snopes on brains|website=[[Snopes]]|date=February 8, 2000 |access-date=August 29, 2009 }}</ref><ref name="Radford 1999">{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Radford|first=Benjamin|date=March–April 1999|title=The Ten-Percent Myth|journal=Skeptical Inquirer|issn=0194-6730|url=http://www.csicop.org/si/show/the_ten-percent_myth/|access-date=April 15, 2009|quote=It's the old myth heard time and again about how people use only ten percent of their brains | archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20131030034901/http://www.csicop.org/si/show/the_ten-percent_myth/ | archive-date = October 30, 2013| url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> While it is true that a small minority of [[neuron]]s in the brain are actively firing at any one time, a healthy human will normally use most of their brain over the course of a day, and the inactive neurons are important as well. The idea that activating 100% of the brain would allow someone to achieve their maximum potential and/or gain various psychic abilities is common in [[Ten percent of the brain myth#In popular culture|folklore and fiction]],<noinclude><ref name="Radford 1999" /><ref name="beyersteinbrain" /><ref name="msnbc news ref">{{#invoke:cite web||last=Bahn |first=Christopher |title='Limitless' brainpower plot isn't all that crazy |work=TODAY.com |date=7 March 2011 |url=http://today.msnbc.msn.com/id/41953704/ns/today-entertainment/ |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110313105800/http://today.msnbc.msn.com/id/41953704/ns/today-entertainment/ |archive-date=March 13, 2011 |access-date=March 31, 2011}}</ref></noinclude> but doing so in real life would likely result in a fatal [[seizure]].<noinclude><ref name="Fish2014">{{#invoke:cite journal||vauthors=Fisher RS, Acevedo C, Arzimanoglou A, Bogacz A, Cross JH, Elger CE, Engel J, Forsgren L, French JA, Glynn M, Hesdorffer DC, Lee BI, Mathern GW, Moshé SL, Perucca E, Scheffer IE, Tomson T, Watanabe M, Wiebe S |date=April 2014 |title=ILAE official report: a practical clinical definition of epilepsy |journal=Epilepsia |volume=55 |issue=4 |pages=475–482 |doi=10.1111/epi.12550 |pmid=24730690 |doi-access=free |s2cid=35958237}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||date=February 13, 2018 |title=The Life and Times of the 10% Neuromyth – Knowing Neurons |language=en-GB |work=Knowing Neurons |url=https://knowingneurons.com/2018/02/13/10-neuromyth/ |url-status=live |access-date=February 24, 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180315133927/https://knowingneurons.com/2018/02/13/10-neuromyth/ |archive-date=March 15, 2018}}</ref></noinclude> This misconception was attributed to late 19th century leading thinker [[William James]], who apparently used the expression only metaphorically.<noinclude><ref name="beyersteinbrain">{{#invoke:cite book||last=Beyerstein|first=Barry L.|title=Mind Myths: Exploring Popular Assumptions About the Mind and Brain|editor=Sergio Della Sala |publisher=Wiley |date=1999 |pages=3–24 |chapter=Whence Cometh the Myth that We Only Use 10% of our Brains? |isbn=978-0-471-98303-3}}</ref></noinclude>
* Although [[Phineas Gage]]'s brain injuries, caused by a several-foot-long tamping rod driven completely through his skull, caused him to become temporarily disabled, many fanciful descriptions of his aberrant behavior in later life are without factual basis or contradicted by known facts.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Barker |first=F. G. II |year=1995 |url=http://mysois.uwm.edu/omeka/archive/files/b7cf786e3d97d4cf63ac3b7d14e1f88e.pdf |title=Phineas among the phrenologists: the American crowbar case and nineteenth-century theories of cerebral localization |journal=Journal of Neurosurgery |volume=82 |issue=4 |pages=672–82 |pmid=7897537 |doi=10.3171/jns.1995.82.4.0672 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141006074853/http://mysois.uwm.edu/omeka/archive/files/b7cf786e3d97d4cf63ac3b7d14e1f88e.pdf |archive-date=October 6, 2014 }}</ref></noinclude>

==== Senses ====
[[File:Taste buds.svg|thumb|upright|An '''incorrect''' [[tongue map|map of the tongue]] showing taste zones. In fact, all zones can sense all tastes.]]
* Humans have more than the commonly cited five senses. The number of senses in various categorizations ranges from 5 to more than 20. In addition to sight, smell, taste, touch, and hearing, which were the senses identified by [[Aristotle]], humans can sense [[Balance (ability)|balance]] and acceleration ([[equilibrioception]]), pain ([[nociception]]), body and limb position ([[proprioception]] or kinesthetic sense), and relative temperature ([[thermoception]]).<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||author=Cerretani, Jessica |date=Spring 2010 |website=Harvard Medicine |publisher=Harvard College |url=http://harvardmedicine.hms.harvard.edu/fascinoma/fivesenses/beyond/extra.php |title=Extra Sensory Perceptions |access-date=January 13, 2011| archive-url= https://web.archive.org/web/20120211190433/http://harvardmedicine.hms.harvard.edu/fascinoma/fivesenses/beyond/extra.php |archive-date=February 11, 2012}}</ref></noinclude> Other senses sometimes identified are the sense of time, [[Human echolocation|echolocation]], itching, pressure, hunger, thirst, fullness of the stomach, need to urinate, need to defecate, blood carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) levels,<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://health.howstuffworks.com/mental-health/human-nature/perception/question242.htm |date=April 2000 |title=How many senses does a human being have? |website=Discovery Health |publisher=[[Discovery Communications Inc.]] |access-date=January 13, 2011 |archive-date=November 6, 2011 |archive-url= https://web.archive.org/web/20111106034223/http://health.howstuffworks.com/mental-health/human-nature/perception/question242.htm |url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> and electric field sensation.<noinclude><ref name="Shimizu">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Odagiri-Shimizu |first1=H |last2=Shimizu |first2=K |title=Experimental analysis of the human perception threshold of a DC electric field |journal=Medical * Biological Engineering & Computing |date=Nov 1999 |volume=37 |issue=6 |pages=727–732 |doi=10.1007/BF02513374 |pmid=10723879}}</ref></noinclude>
* All different tastes can be detected on all parts of the tongue by taste buds,<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||title=The cells and logic for mammalian sour taste detection|journal=Nature|volume=442|issue=7105 |pages=934–938 |date=August 2006|pmid=16929298|pmc=1571047 |doi=10.1038/nature05084|bibcode=2006Natur.442..934H |vauthors=Huang AL, Chen X, Hoon MA, Chandrashekar J, Guo W, Tränkner D, Ryba NJ, Zuker CS}}</ref></noinclude> with slightly increased sensitivities in different locations depending on the person; the [[tongue map]] showing the contrary is fallacious.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=October 22, 2002|title=Beyond the Tongue Map|publisher=Asha.org |url=http://www.asha.org/Publications/leader/2002/021022/f021022a.htm|access-date=August 29, 2009|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131214115137/http://www.asha.org/Publications/leader/2002/021022/f021022a.htm|archive-date=December 14, 2013}}</ref></noinclude>
* There are not four [[primary tastes]], but five: in addition to bitter, sour, salty, and sweet, humans have taste receptors for [[umami]], which is a "savory" or "meaty" taste.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||title=Food Science and Technology |last=Campbell-Platt |first=Geoffrey |date=2009 |publisher=Wiley |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=E7GXHploJasC&pg=PA31 |isbn=978-0-632-06421-2 |page=31}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.rtc.edu/programs/generaleducation/biology/biology220/files/Senses_notes.pdf |title=Senses Notes |access-date=January 13, 2011|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110720040515/http://www.rtc.edu/programs/generaleducation/biology/biology220/files/Senses_notes.pdf |archive-date=July 20, 2011}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||author=Krulwich, Robert |date=November 5, 2007 |title=Sweet, Sour, Salty, Bitter&nbsp;... and Umami |url=https://www.npr.org/templates/story/story.php?storyId=15819485 |website=Krulwich Wonders, an NPR Science Blog |publisher=NPR |access-date=January 13, 2011 }}
<br />d. {{Cite web |title=Probing Question: What is umami? {{!}} Penn State University |url=https://www.psu.edu/news/research/story/probing-question-what-umami/ |access-date=2024-07-23 |website=www.psu.edu |language=en}}</ref></noinclude> Fat does interact with specific [[Receptor (biochemistry)|receptors]] in [[taste bud]] cells, but whether it is a sixth primary taste remains inconclusive.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Besnard|first=P |date=December 2015|title=Taste of Fat: A Sixth Taste Modality?|journal=Physiological Reviews|volume=96|issue=1 |pages=151–176|doi=10.1152/physrev.00002.2015|pmid=26631596 |doi-access=free}}</ref></noinclude>
* The human [[sense of smell]] is not weak or underdeveloped. Humans have similar senses of smell to other mammals, and are more sensitive to some odors than rodents and dogs.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=McGann |first=John P. |date=2017-05-12 |title=Poor human olfaction is a 19th-century myth |journal=Science |language=en |volume=356 |issue=6338 |pages=eaam7263 |doi=10.1126/science.aam7263 |issn=0036-8075 |pmc=5512720 |pmid=28495701}}</ref></noinclude>

=== Toxicology ===
* [[Smokeless tobacco]] is not a "safe" alternative to conventional tobacco; smokeless tobacco products contain [[nicotine]] and are therefore highly addictive.<noinclude><ref name="VidyasagaranSiddiqi2016">{{#invoke:cite journal ||last1=Vidyasagaran |first1=A. L. |last2=Siddiqi |first2=K. |last3=Kanaan |first3=M. |year=2016 |title=Use of smokeless tobacco and risk of cardiovascular disease: A systematic review and meta-analysis |url=http://eprints.whiterose.ac.uk/101100/1/EJPC_D_16_00085_R1_2016.pdf |journal=European Journal of Preventive Cardiology |volume=23 |issue=18 |pages=1970–1981 |doi=10.1177/2047487316654026 |issn=2047-4873 |pmid=27256827 |s2cid=206820997}}</ref></noinclude> They also can cause various harmful effects such as dental disease, [[oral cancer]], [[Esophageal cancer|oesophagus cancer]], and [[Pancreatic cancer|pancreas cancer]], [[Coronary artery disease|coronary heart disease]], as well as negative reproductive effects including [[stillbirth]], [[premature birth]] and [[low birth weight]].<noinclude><ref name="VidyasagaranSiddiqi2016" /><ref name="Gupta 2019">{{#invoke:cite journal ||last1=Gupta |first1=Ruchika |last2=Gupta |first2=Sanjay |last3=Sharma |first3=Shashi |last4=Sinha |first4=Dhirendra N |last5=Mehrotra |first5=Ravi |date=2019-01-01 |title=Risk of Coronary Heart Disease Among Smokeless Tobacco Users: Results of Systematic Review and Meta-Analysis of Global Data |url=https://academic.oup.com/ntr/article/21/1/25/4793346 |journal=Nicotine & Tobacco Research |language=en |volume=21 |issue=1 |pages=25–31 |doi=10.1093/ntr/nty002 |issn=1469-994X |pmc=6941711 |pmid=29325111}}</ref></noinclude>
* Swallowing gasoline does not generally require special emergency treatment, as long as it goes into the stomach and not the lungs; inducing vomiting can make it worse.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||url=https://healthcare.utah.edu/the-scope/shows.php?shows=0_g9tzppx4 |publisher=[[University of Utah]]|title= Dos and Don'ts in Case of Gasoline Poisoning |author=University of Utah Poison Control Center|date=June 24, 2014}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url= https://www.atsdr.cdc.gov/MMG/MMG.asp?id=465&tid=83 |title= Medical Management Guidelines for Gasoline (Mixture) CAS# 86290-81-5 and 8006-61-9 |publisher=[[Centers for Disease Control and Prevention]] |date= October 21, 2014 |author= Agency for Toxic Substances and Disease Registry |author-link= Agency for Toxic Substances and Disease Registry }}</ref></noinclude>
* A [[chloroform]]-soaked rag cannot instantly incapacitate a person.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Payne|first=J. P.|s2cid=1718276|date=July 1998|title=The criminal use of chloroform|journal=[[Anaesthesia (journal)|Anaesthesia]]|volume=53|issue=7|pages=685–690|doi=10.1046/j.1365-2044.1998.528-az0572.x|pmid=9771177|doi-access=free}}</ref></noinclude> It takes at least five minutes of inhaling an item soaked in chloroform to render a person unconscious. Most criminal cases involving chloroform also involve another drug being co-administered, such as alcohol or [[diazepam]], or the victim being found to have been complicit in its administration. The misconception that chloroform can be used as an [[incapacitating agent]]<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||title=Medical Annotation: Chloroform amongst Thieves |journal=[[The Lancet]] |date=1865 |volume=2 |issue=2200 |pages=490–491|doi=10.1016/s0140-6736(02)58434-8}}</ref></noinclude> has been popularized by crime fiction authors.
* Although bananas contain naturally occurring [[radioactive isotope]]s, particularly [[potassium-40]] (<sup>40</sup>K), which emit [[ionizing radiation]] when undergoing [[radioactive decay]], the levels of such radiation are far too low to induce [[radiation poisoning]], and bananas are not a radiation hazard. It would not be physically possible to eat enough bananas to cause radiation poisoning, as [[Cumulative dose|the radiation dose from bananas is non-cumulative]].<noinclude><ref name="AdventHealth 2018">{{#invoke:cite web||title=The Most Common Medical Radiation Myths Dispelled |url=https://www.ahu.edu/blog/the-most-common-medical-radiation-myths-dispelled |access-date=2022-11-05 |website=AdventHealth University |date=May 21, 2018 |language=en}}</ref><ref name="Loughran 2022">{{#invoke:cite web||last=Loughran |first=Sarah |title=Are bananas really 'radioactive'? An expert clears up common misunderstandings about radiation |url=http://theconversation.com/are-bananas-really-radioactive-an-expert-clears-up-common-misunderstandings-about-radiation-193211 |access-date=2022-11-06 |website=The Conversation |date=November 3, 2022 |language=en}}</ref><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||last1=Eisenbud |first1=Merril |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=67Pn4ydLOVAC |title=Environmental radioactivity: from natural, industrial, and military sources |last2=Gesell |first2=Thomas F. |publisher=Academic Press |year=1997 |isbn=978-0-12-235154-9 |pages=[https://books.google.com/books?id=67Pn4ydLOVAC&pg=PA171 171–172] |quote=It is important to recognize that the potassium content of the body is under strict homeostatic control and is not influenced by variations in environmental levels. For this reason, the dose from {{sup|40}}K in the body is constant.}}
<br />b. U. S. Environmental Protection Agency (1999), [https://www.epa.gov/sites/default/files/2015-05/documents/402-r-99-001.pdf Federal Guidance Report 13], page 16: "For example, the ingestion coefficient risk for 40K would not be appropriate for an application to ingestion of <sup>40</sup>K in conjunction with an elevated intake of natural potassium. This is because the biokinetic model for potassium used in this document represents the relatively slow removal of potassium (biological half-time 30 days) that is estimated to occur for typical intakes of potassium, whereas an elevated intake of potassium would result in excretion of a nearly equal mass of natural potassium, and hence of <sup>40</sup>K, over a short period."
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||author=Maggie Koerth-Baker |author-link=Maggie Koerth-Baker |date=Aug 27, 2010 |title=Bananas are radioactive—But they aren't a good way to explain radiation exposure |url=http://boingboing.net/2010/08/27/bananas-are-radioact.html |access-date=25 May 2011}}. Attributes the title statement to Geoff Meggitt, former UK Atomic Energy Authority.</ref></noinclude> (See also: [[Banana equivalent dose]])
* Ingesting [[Visine]], a brand of eye drops, does not cause diarrhea. It is [[Neurotoxicity|neurotoxic]], with consumption causing several serious side-effects. Pranks spiking people with Visine rose after the misconception was popularized by the film ''[[Wedding Crashers]]''.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||date=July 24, 2013 |title=Nothing funny about so-called 'Visine prank,' says pharmacist |url=https://www.therecord.com/news/waterloo-region/nothing-funny-about-so-called-visine-prank-says-pharmacist/article_c955e29f-ffab-5c9b-a82f-671ff7ea9dc3.html |work=[[Waterloo Region Record]]}}</ref><ref>{{Cite magazine |last=Blum |first=Deborah |date=March 25, 2013 |title=Just an (Eye) Drop of Poison |url=https://www.wired.com/2013/03/just-an-eye-drop-of-poison/ |magazine=[[Wired (magazine)|Wired]]}}</ref></noinclude>

=== Transportation ===

[[File:Bermuda Triangle.png|thumb| One version of the Bermuda Triangle area]]
* The [[Bermuda Triangle]] does not have any more shipwrecks or mysterious disappearances than most other waterways.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite news||url =https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/science-environment-22806362|title =Study finds shipwrecks threaten precious seas|date =June 7, 2013|access-date = June 7, 2013|publisher =BBC News/science}}

<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal|| url=http://www.csmonitor.com/Science/2013/0610/Bermuda-Triangle-doesn-t-make-the-cut-on-list-of-world-s-most-dangerous-oceans | title=Bermuda Triangle doesn't make the cut on list of world's most dangerous oceans | journal=The Christian Science Monitor | access-date=March 22, 2016| date=June 10, 2013 }}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite book||title=The Bermuda Triangle Mystery Solved| year=1975|first=Lawrence David|last= Kusche|isbn = 978-0-87975-971-1|publisher=Prometheus Books|location = Buffalo, NY|ref=Kusche, 1975}}</ref></noinclude>
* Toilet waste is never intentionally jettisoned from an aircraft. All waste is collected in tanks and emptied into [[Ground support equipment#Lavatory service vehicles|toilet waste vehicles]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://blogs.wsj.com/middleseat/2008/11/19/on-world-toilet-day-let-us-praise-the-airline-lav/|last=Philips|first=Matt|title=On World Toilet Day, Let Us Praise the Airline Lav|work=The Middle Seat Terminal (Wall Street Journal)|access-date=April 2, 2009|date=November 19, 2008 | archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20131214105710/https://blogs.wsj.com/middleseat/2008/11/19/on-world-toilet-day-let-us-praise-the-airline-lav/ | archive-date = December 14, 2013| url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> [[Blue ice (aviation)|Blue ice]] is caused by accidental leakage from the waste tank. [[Passenger train toilet]]s, on the other hand, have indeed historically flushed onto the tracks; modern trains in most developed countries usually have retention tanks on board and therefore do not dispose of waste in such a manner.
* [[automotive battery|Automotive batteries]] stored on a concrete floor do not discharge any faster than they would on other surfaces, in spite of a worry that concrete harms batteries.<noinclude><ref name="Snopes">{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.snopes.com/autos/techno/battery.asp|title=Battery Parked|work=[[Snopes]]|date=February 8, 2011|access-date=June 2, 2013}}</ref></noinclude> Early batteries with porous, leaky cases may have been susceptible to moisture from floors, but for many years [[Lead–acid battery|lead–acid car batteries]] have had impermeable [[polypropylene]] cases.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://news.google.com/newspapers?id=k8BIAAAAIBAJ&pg=2232,1623674|title= No End to Battery Storage Debate|newspaper=The Vindicator |date= November 4, 1999|first1=Tom|last1=Magliozzi|author1-link= Tom Magliozzi|first2= Ray|last2= Magliozzi|author2-link=Ray Magliozzi|page=37}}</ref></noinclude> While most modern automotive batteries are [[VRLA battery|sealed]], and do not leak battery acid when properly stored and maintained,<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||title=Ask Click and Clack: Answers from Car Talk|first1=Tom|last1= Magliozzi|author1-link=Tom Magliozzi|first2=Ray|last2=Magliozzi|author2-link=Ray Magliozzi|url= https://books.google.com/books?id=Z1nntWoIBp4C&pg=PA68|pages=68–69|publisher=Chronicle Books|date=2008|isbn= 978-0-8118-6477-0 |access-date= June 2, 2013 }}
<br />b. {{cite magazine |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=MxXlKb9wIe0C&pg=PA136 |title=Car Care Auto Clinic |magazine=[[Popular Mechanics]] |date=November 2000 |volume=177 |issue=11 |issn=0032-4558 |page=136 |access-date=June 2, 2013}}</ref></noinclude> the sulfuric acid in them can leak out and stain, etch, or corrode concrete floors if their cases crack or tip over or their vent-holes are breached by floods.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite news||title=Use care in cleaning battery-acid stain|url=https://www.seattletimes.com/life/lifestyle/use-care-in-cleaning-battery-acid-stain/|first=Karen|last=Youso|website=The Seattle Times|access-date=May 9, 2018|date=October 4, 2008}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Managing Used Lead-Acid Batteries|url=http://www.gecap.org/pdf/lead-acid_batteries.pdf|website=Georgia Environmental Compliance Assistance Program|publisher=Georgia Tech Research Institute|access-date=May 9, 2018|date=July 2002|archive-date=October 28, 2020|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20201028080456/https://www.gecap.org/pdf/lead-acid_batteries.pdf}}</ref></noinclude>

<noinclude>{{Short description|none}}
Each entry on this list of common misconceptions is worded as a correction; the misconceptions themselves are implied rather than stated. These entries are concise summaries; the main subject articles can be consulted for more detail.

{{Main|List of common misconceptions}}</noinclude>

=== Ancient history ===  <!-- NOTE: Headers are done this way so that the transclusion works. Please do not "fix" them. -->
* The [[Egyptian pyramids|Pyramids of Egypt]] were not constructed with [[Slavery in ancient Egypt|slave labor]]. Archaeological evidence shows that the laborers were a combination of skilled workers and poor farmers working in the off-season with the participants paid in high-quality food and tax exemptions.<noinclude><ref>a. {{Cite magazine |last=Shaw |first=Johnathan |title=Who Built the Pyramids? |url=https://www.harvardmagazine.com/2003/07/who-built-the-pyramids-html |date=July–August 2003 |magazine=[[Harvard Magazine]] |access-date=August 14, 2022 |archive-date=March 19, 2023 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230319160551/https://www.harvardmagazine.com/2003/07/who-built-the-pyramids-html |url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Egypt tombs suggest pyramids not built by slaves|url=https://www.reuters.com/article/us-egypt-antiquities-tombs/egypt-tombs-suggest-pyramids-not-built-by-slaves-idUSTRE6091E720100110|date=January 10, 2010|website=Reuters|access-date=August 14, 2022|archive-date=December 20, 2022|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20221220233133/https://www.reuters.com/article/us-egypt-antiquities-tombs/egypt-tombs-suggest-pyramids-not-built-by-slaves-idUSTRE6091E720100110|url-status=live}}
<br />c. {{Cite magazine |last=Weiss |first=Daniel |title=Journeys of the Pyramid Builders |date=July–August 2022 |url=https://www.archaeology.org/issues/473-2207/features/10601-egypt-wadi-el-jarf-port-papyri#art_page6 |magazine=[[Archaeology (magazine)|Archaeology]] |publisher=[[Archaeological Institute of America]] |access-date=August 14, 2022 |quote=Based on the contents of the papyri, Tallet believes that at least some workers in the time of Khufu were highly skilled and well rewarded for their labor, contradicting the popular notion that the Great Pyramid was built by masses of oppressed slaves. |archive-date=March 14, 2023 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230314143516/https://www.archaeology.org/issues/473-2207/features/10601-egypt-wadi-el-jarf-port-papyri#art_page6 |url-status=live}}</ref><ref name="Watterson">{{#invoke:cite book||title=The Egyptians|last=Watterson |first=Barbara|year=1997|publisher=Blackwell|chapter=The Era of Pyramid-builders|page=63|url-access=registration |url=https://archive.org/details/egyptianspeoples00barb |quote=Herodotus claimed that the Great Pyramid at Giza was built with the labour of 100,000 slaves working in three-monthly shifts, a charge that cannot be substantiated. Much of the non-skilled labour on the pyramids was undertaken by peasants working during the Inundation season when they could not farm their lands. In return for their services they were given rations of food, a welcome addition to the family diet.}}</ref></noinclude> The idea that slaves were used originated with [[Herodotus]], and the idea that they were Israelites arose centuries after the pyramids were constructed.<noinclude><ref name="USPyramids">{{#invoke:cite web||last=Kratovac |first=Katarina |date=January 12, 2010 |title=Egypt: New Find Shows Slaves Didn't Build Pyramids |url=https://www.usnews.com/science/articles/2010/01/12/egypt-new-find-shows-slaves-didnt-build-pyramids |access-date=August 14, 2022 |website=U.S. News |archive-date=May 12, 2019 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190512025618/https://www.usnews.com/science/articles/2010/01/12/egypt-new-find-shows-slaves-didnt-build-pyramids |url-status=live }}</ref><ref name="Watterson" /></noinclude>
* [[Galley]]s in ancient times were not commonly operated by chained slaves or prisoners, as depicted in films such as ''[[Ben-Hur (1959 film)|Ben Hur]]'', but by paid laborers or soldiers, with slaves used only in times of crisis, in some cases even gaining freedom after the crisis was averted. [[Ptolemaic Egypt]] was a possible exception.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Casson |first=Lionel |date=1966 |title=Galley Slaves |journal=Transactions and Proceedings of the American Philological Association |volume=97 |pages=35–36 |doi=10.2307/2936000 |jstor=2936000 }}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Sargent |first=Rachel L |date=July 1927 |title=The Use of Slaves by the Athenians in Warfare II. In Warfare by Sea |url=https://warwick.ac.uk/fac/arts/classics/intranets/students/modules/warfare/essays/the_use_of_slaves_by_the_athenians_in_warfare_ii.pdf |journal=Classical Philology |volume=22 |issue=3 |pages=264–279 |doi=10.1086/360910 |jstor=262754 |via=[[JSTOR]]}}</ref></noinclude> Other types of vessels, such as Roman merchant vessels, were manned by slaves, sometimes even with slaves as ship's master.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last=Unger |first=Richard |title=The ship in the medieval economy, 600-1600 |publisher=Croom Helm |year=1980 |isbn=0-85664-949-X |location=London |page=37}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Tutankhamun]]'s tomb is not inscribed with a [[Curse of the pharaohs|curse on those who disturb it]]. This was a media invention of 20th-century tabloid journalists.<noinclude><ref>a. [[James Hamilton-Paterson]], Carol Andrews, ''Mummies: Death and Life in Ancient Egypt'', p.&nbsp;191, Collins for [[British Museum Publications]], 1978, {{ISBN|978-0-00-195532-5}}
<br />b. [[Charlotte Booth]], ''The Boy Behind the Mask'', p.&nbsp;xvi, Oneword, 2007, {{isbn|978-1-85168-544-8}}
<br />c. Richard Cavendish, "Tutankhamun's Curse?", ''History Today'' '''64''':3 ([https://www.historytoday.com/archive/months-past/tutankhamuns-curse 3 March 2014] {{Webarchive|url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230403225344/https://www.historytoday.com/archive/months-past/tutankhamuns-curse|date=April 3, 2023}})</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Minoan civilization]] was not destroyed by the [[Minoan eruption|eruption of Thera]] and was not the inspiration for [[Plato]]'s parable of [[Atlantis]].<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Neer|first=Richard|year=2012|title=Art and Archaeology of the Greek World|publisher=Thames and Hudson|page=37|isbn=978-0-500-05166-5|quote="...popular associations of the eruption with a legend of Atlantis should be dismissed...nor is there good evidence to suggest that the eruption...brought about the collapse of Minoan Crete}}
<br />b. {{cite encyclopedia |year=2012 |title=Eruption of Thera/Santorini |encyclopedia=The Oxford Handbook of the Bronze Age Aegean |publisher=Oxford University Press |last=Manning |first=Stuart |editor-last=Cline |editor-first=Eric |pages=457–454 |doi=10.1093/oxfordhb/9780199873609.013.0034 |isbn=978-0-19-987360-9 |quote="Marinatos (1939) famously suggested that the eruption might even have caused the destruction of Minoan Crete (also Page 1970). Although this simple hypothesis has been negated by the findings of excavation and other research since the late 1960s... which demonstrate that the eruption occurred late in the Late Minoan IA ceramic period, whereas the destructions of the Cretan palaces and so on are some time subsequent (late in the following Late Minoan IB ceramic period)"}}</ref></noinclude>[[File:Jacques-Louis David - Oath of the Horatii - Google Art Project.jpg|thumb|The ancient Romans did not use the Roman salute depicted in ''[[Oath of the Horatii|The Oath of the Horatii]]'' (1784).]]
* The ancient Greeks did not use the word "[[Idiot (Athenian democracy)|idiot]]" ({{langx|grc|ἰδιώτης|idiṓtēs}}) to disparage people who did not take part in civic life. An {{lang|grc|ἰδιώτης}} was simply a private citizen as opposed to a government official. The word also meant any sort of non-expert or layman,<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||author=Sparkes A.W.|year=1988|title=Idiots, Ancient and Modern|journal=Australian Journal of Political Science|volume=23|pages=101–102|doi=10.1080/00323268808402051}}</ref></noinclude> then later someone uneducated or ignorant, and much later to mean stupid or mentally deficient.<noinclude><ref name="oed_idiot">''[[Oxford English Dictionary]]'', [http://www.oed.com/view/Entry/91049 ''s.v.''] {{Webarchive|url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240808134649/https://www.oed.com/dictionary/idiot_n |date=August 8, 2024 }}</ref></noinclude>

==== Ancient Rome ====
* The so-called [[Roman salute]], in which the arm is fully extended forwards or diagonally with palm down and fingers touching, was not used in ancient Rome. The gesture was first associated with ancient Rome in the 1784 painting ''[[Oath of the Horatii|The Oath of the Horatii]]'' by the French artist [[Jacques-Louis David]], which inspired later salutes, most notably the [[Nazi salute]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last=Winkler |first=Martin M. |title=The Roman Salute: Cinema, History, Ideology |publisher=Ohio State University Press |location=Columbus |year=2009 |isbn=978-0-8142-0864-9}} p. 55</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Amphitheatre-Toulouse 2.JPG|thumb|upright|A ''[[vomitorium]]'' in a Roman amphitheater]]
* Wealthy Ancient Romans did not use rooms called ''vomitoria'' to purge food during meals so they could continue eating<noinclude><ref name="McKeown 2010">{{#invoke:cite book||last=McKeown|first=J.C.|title=A Cabinet of Roman Curiosities: Strange Tales and Surprising Facts from the World's Greatest Empire|date=2010|publisher=Oxford University Press|isbn=978-0-19-539375-0|pages=153–54|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=YGYwlMZ3ursC&pg=PA153}}</ref></noinclude> and vomiting was not a regular part of [[Ancient Roman cuisine#Table culture|Roman dining customs]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last=Fass|first=Patrick|title=Around the Roman Table|date=1994|publisher=[[University of Chicago Press]]|isbn=978-0-226-23347-5|pages=66–67|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=YXGlAr17oekC|access-date=September 21, 2016|archive-date=January 21, 2023|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230121034111/https://books.google.com/books?id=YXGlAr17oekC|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> A ''[[vomitorium]]'' of an amphitheatre or stadium was a passageway allowing quick exit at the end of an event.<noinclude><ref name="McKeown 2010" /></noinclude>
* [[Scipio Aemilianus]] did not [[Salting the earth|sow salt]]<!-- this is the topic article--> over the city of [[Carthage]] after defeating it in the [[Third Punic War]].<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Ridley|first1=R.T.|title=To Be Taken with a Pinch of Salt: The Destruction of Carthage |journal=Classical Philology|volume=81|issue=2|year=1986 |pages=140–146|doi=10.1086/366973|s2cid=161696751 |jstor=269786}}: "a tradition in Roman history well known to most students"
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Stevens|first1=Susan T. |year=1988|title=A Legend of the Destruction of Carthage |journal=Classical Philology |volume=83 |issue=1 |pages=39–41 |jstor=269635 |doi=10.1086/367078 |s2cid=161764925}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Visona |first1=Paolo |year=1988 |title=Passing the Salt: On the Destruction of Carthage Again |journal=Classical Philology |volume=83 |issue=1 |pages=41–42 |jstor=269636 |doi=10.1086/367079 |s2cid=162289604}}: "this story... had already gained widespread currency"
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Warmington |first1=B.H. |title=The Destruction of Carthage: A Retractatio |journal=Classical Philology |volume=83 |issue=4 |pages=308–10|year=1988| jstor=269510 |doi=10.1086/367123 |s2cid=162850949}}: "the frequently repeated story"</ref></noinclude>
* [[Julius Caesar]] was not born via [[caesarean section]]. Such a procedure would have been fatal to the mother at the time, and Caesar's mother was still alive when he was 45 years old.<noinclude><ref>"[A mother in the 1st century AD] could not survive the trauma of a Caesarean" ''Oxford Classical Dictionary, Third Edition'', "Childbirth"</ref><ref name="Wanjek 2003 p5">{{#invoke:cite book||last1=Wanjek |first1=Christopher |title=Bad Medicine: Misconceptions and Misuses Revealed, from Distance Healing to Vitamin O |date=April 7, 2003 |publisher=John Wiley & Sons |isbn=978-0-471-46315-3 |page=5 |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=oIJ5TKh7mPgC&dq=caesar&pg=PA5 |language=en}}</ref></noinclude>

=== Middle Ages ===
{{See also|List of common misconceptions about the Middle Ages}}

==== Europe ====
* The [[Middle Ages]] were not "a time of ignorance, barbarism and superstition"; the Church did not place religious authority over personal experience and rational activity; and the term "[[Dark Ages (historiography)|Dark Ages]]" is rejected by modern historians.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||first=David C.|last=Lindberg|chapter=The Medieval Church Encounters the Classical Tradition: Saint Augustine, Roger Bacon, and the Handmaiden Metaphor|editor1-first=David C.|editor1-last=Lindberg|editor2-first=Ronald L.|editor2-last=Numbers|title=When Science & Christianity Meet|location=Chicago|publisher=[[University of Chicago Press]]|year=2003|page=8}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||first=Edward|last=Grant|title=God and Reason in the Middle Ages|publisher=Cambridge|year=2001|page=9}}
<br />c. {{cite encyclopedia |first=Ted |last=Peters |chapter=Science and Religion |editor-first=Lindsay |editor-last=Jones |encyclopedia=Encyclopedia of Religion |edition=2nd |publisher=Thomson Gale |year=2005 |page=8182}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Snyder|first=Christopher A.|author-link=Christopher Snyder (historian)|year=1998|title=An Age of Tyrants: Britain and the Britons A.D. 400–600|publisher=Pennsylvania State University Press|publication-date=1998|location=University Park|pages=xiii–xiv|isbn=978-0-271-01780-8}}</ref></noinclude>
* While modern [[Life expectancy|life expectancies]] are much higher than those in the Middle Ages and earlier,<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book|| vauthors = Bitel LM |title=Women in Early Medieval Europe, 400-1100 |date=2002-10-24 |publisher=Cambridge University Press |isbn=978-0-521-59773-9 |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=XRudk_NcA7cC&pg=PA25}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| publisher = U.N. Department of Economic and Social Affairs | work = Population Division |title=World Population Prospects 2019 |url=https://population.un.org/wpp/Publications/Files/WPP2019_Highlights.pdf}}</ref></noinclude> adults in the Middle Ages did not die in their 30s on average. That was the life expectancy ''at birth'', which was skewed by high infant and adolescent mortality. The life expectancy among adults was much higher;<noinclude><ref name="Wanjek 2003" /></noinclude> a 21-year-old man in medieval England, for example, could expect to live to the age of 64.<noinclude><ref name="apps.business.ualberta.ca">{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://apps.business.ualberta.ca/rfield/lifeexpectancy.htm |title="Expectations of Life" by H.O. Lancaster as per | archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20120904043610/http://apps.business.ualberta.ca/rfield/lifeexpectancy.htm | archive-date = September 4, 2012| url-status=live}}</ref><ref name="Wanjek 2003">{{#invoke:cite book||url= https://books.google.com/books?id=oIJ5TKh7mPgC&q=%22this+is+one+of+the+biggest+misconceptions+about+old+age%22&pg=PA70 |author-link=Christopher Wanjek |last=Wanjek |first=Christopher |title=Bad Medicine: Misconceptions and Misuses Revealed, from Distance Healing to Vitamin O |publisher=Wiley |year=2002 |pages=70–71 |isbn=978-0-471-43499-3}}</ref></noinclude> However, in various places and eras, life expectancy was noticeably lower. For example, monks often died in their 20s or 30s.<noinclude><ref name="Scott 2011">{{#invoke:cite book||last=Scott|first=Robert A.|title=Miracle Cures: Saints, Pilgrimage, and the Healing Powers of Belief|date=October 4, 2011|publisher=[[University of California Press]]|isbn=978-0-520-27134-0|edition=1st|location=Berkeley|pages=10–11}}</ref></noinclude>
* In the tale of [[King Canute and the tide]], the king did not command the tide to reverse in a fit of delusional arrogance. According to the story, his intent was to prove a point that no man is all-powerful, and that all people must bend to forces beyond their control, such as the tides.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/magazine-13524677|title=Is King Canute misunderstood?|publisher=BBC|date=May 26, 2011|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140420145809/http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/magazine-13524677|archive-date=April 20, 2014|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* There is no evidence that [[Iron maiden (torture)|iron maidens]] were used for torture, or even yet invented, in the Middle Ages. Instead they were pieced together in the 18th century from several artifacts found in museums, arsenals and the like to create spectacular objects intended for commercial exhibition.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||first=Wolfgang|last=Schild|date=2000|title=Die eiserne Jungfrau. Dichtung und Wahrheit (Schriftenreihe des Mittelalterlichen Kriminalmuseums Rothenburg o. d. Tauber Nr. 3)|location=Rothenburg ob der Tauber|publisher=Mittelalterl. Kriminalmuseum}}</ref></noinclude>
* Spiral staircases in castles were not designed in a clockwise direction to hinder right-handed attackers.<noinclude><ref name="Guy">{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Guy|first=Neil|title=The Rise of the Anticlockwise Newel Stair|journal=The Castle Studies Group Journal|volume=25|year=2011–2012|pages=114, 163|url=http://www.castlestudiesgroup.org.uk/CSGJournal2011-12X5stairs.pdf|access-date=May 12, 2020|archive-date=August 7, 2020|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20200807073740/http://www.castlestudiesgroup.org.uk/CSGJournal2011-12X5stairs.pdf|url-status=live}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||url=https://historytheinterestingbits.com/2019/10/09/guest-post-busting-mediaeval-building-myths-part-one/|title=Guest Post: Busting Mediaeval Building Myths: Part One|publisher=History... the interesting bits!|first=James|last=Wright|date=October 9, 2019|access-date=February 24, 2020|archive-date=January 8, 2020|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20200108190529/https://historytheinterestingbits.com/2019/10/09/guest-post-busting-mediaeval-building-myths-part-one/|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> While clockwise spiral staircases are more common in castles than anti-clockwise, they were even more common in medieval structures without a military role, such as religious buildings.<noinclude><ref name="Ryder">{{cite thesis |last=Ryder |first=Charles |title=The spiral stair or vice: its origins, role and meaning in medieval stone castles |access-date=May 12, 2020 |type=PhD |publisher=University of Liverpool |url=https://chesterrep.openrepository.com/handle/10034/239772 |page=294 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20200223182558/https://chesterrep.openrepository.com/handle/10034/239772 |archive-date=February 23, 2020 |year=2011 |url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[plate armor]] of European soldiers did not stop soldiers from moving around or necessitate a crane to get them into a saddle. They needed to be able to fight on foot in case they could not ride their horse and could mount and dismount without help.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Breiding|first=Dirk|title=Department of Arms and Armor, The Metropolitan Museum of Art|url=http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/hd/aams/hd_aams.htm#weight_b|publisher=metmuseum.org|access-date=February 23, 2012|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140426172847/http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/hd/aams/hd_aams.htm|archive-date=April 26, 2014|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> However, armor used in tournaments in the late Middle Ages was significantly heavier than that used in warfare.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://madeofwynn.net/2012/10/08/medieval-anachronisms-part-3-cranes-hoisting-armored-knights/|title=Cranes hoisting armored knights|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131029210602/http://madeofwynn.net/2012/10/08/medieval-anachronisms-part-3-cranes-hoisting-armored-knights/|archive-date=October 29, 2013|access-date=October 23, 2013}}</ref></noinclude>
* Whether [[chastity belt]]s, devices designed to prevent women from having sexual intercourse, were invented in medieval times is disputed by modern historians. Most existing chastity belts are now thought to be deliberate fakes from the 19th century.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||title=Misconceptions About the Middle Ages|last=Keyser|first=Linda Migl|date=2008|publisher=Routledge|editor-last=Harris|editor-first=Stephen J.|chapter=The Medieval Chastity Belt Unbuckled|ref=have|editor2-last=Grigsby|editor2-first=Bryon L.}}</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Gossuin de Metz - L'image du monde - BNF Fr. 574 fo42 - miniature.jpg|thumb|upright|Medieval depiction of a [[spherical Earth]]]]
* Medieval European scholars [[Myth of the flat Earth|did not believe the Earth was flat]]. Scholars have known the Earth is spherical since at least the sixth century BCE.<noinclude><ref name="washingtonpost.com">{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/answer-sheet/post/busting-a-myth-about-columbus-and-a-flat-earth/2011/10/10/gIQAXszQaL_blog.html|title=Busting a myth about Columbus and a flat Earth|newspaper=Washington Post|language=en|access-date=September 29, 2018|archive-date=February 20, 2022|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220220023642/https://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/answer-sheet/post/busting-a-myth-about-columbus-and-a-flat-earth/2011/10/10/gIQAXszQaL_blog.html|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* Medieval cartographers did not regularly write "[[here be dragons]]" on their maps. The only maps from this era that have the phrase inscribed on them are the [[Hunt-Lenox Globe]] and the [[Ostrich Egg Globe]], next to a coast in Southeast Asia for both of them. Maps in this period did occasionally have illustrations of mythical or real animals.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Meyer|first=Robinson|url=https://www.theatlantic.com/technology/archive/2013/12/no-old-maps-actually-say-here-be-dragons/282267/|title=No Old Maps Actually Say 'Here Be Dragons'|website=The Atlantic|date=December 12, 2013|access-date=June 24, 2022|archive-date=February 1, 2023|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230201040526/https://www.theatlantic.com/technology/archive/2013/12/no-old-maps-actually-say-here-be-dragons/282267/|url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal||last=Van Duzer|first=Chet|date=June 4, 2014|title=Bring on the Monsters and Marvels: Non-Ptolemaic Legends on Manuscript Maps of Ptolemy's Geography|journal=Viator|volume=45|issue=2|pages=303–334|doi=10.1484/J.VIATOR.1.103923|issn=0083-5897}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite news||last=Kim|first=Meeri|url=https://www.washingtonpost.com/national/health-science/oldest-globe-to-depict-the-new-world-may-have-been-discovered/2013/08/19/503b2b4a-06b4-11e3-a07f-49ddc7417125_story.html|title=Oldest globe to depict the New World may have been discovered|newspaper=The Washington Post|date=August 19, 2013|access-date=July 2, 2022|quote=The only other map or globe on which this specific phrase appears is what can arguably be called the egg's twin: the copper Hunt-Lenox Globe, dated around 1510 and housed by the Rare Book Division of the New York Public Library.|archive-date=September 30, 2021|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20210930080237/https://www.washingtonpost.com/national/health-science/oldest-globe-to-depict-the-new-world-may-have-been-discovered/2013/08/19/503b2b4a-06b4-11e3-a07f-49ddc7417125_story.html|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Christopher Columbus]]' efforts to obtain support for [[Voyages of Christopher Columbus|his voyages]] were not hampered by belief in a flat Earth, but by valid worries that the East Indies were farther than he realized.<noinclude><ref>{{cite encyclopedia |title=The Myth of the Flat Earth |encyclopedia=Misconceptions about the Middle Ages |publisher=Routledge |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=hdCTAgAAQBAJ&pg=PT149 |access-date=January 26, 2014 |date=2010 |editor=Stephen Harris |isbn=978-1-135-98666-7 |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240808134641/https://books.google.com/books?id=hdCTAgAAQBAJ&pg=PT149#v=onepage&q&f=false |archive-date=August 8, 2024 |author=Louise M. Bishop |editor2=Bryon L. Grigsby}}</ref></noinclude> In fact, Columbus grossly underestimated the Earth's circumference because of two calculation errors.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://spectrum.ieee.org/tech-talk/at-work/test-and-measurement/columbuss-geographical-miscalculations|title=Columbus's Geographical Miscalculations|website=IEEE Spectrum: Technology, Engineering, and Science News|language=en|access-date=October 3, 2018|date=October 9, 2012|archive-date=October 3, 2018|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20181003063329/https://spectrum.ieee.org/tech-talk/at-work/test-and-measurement/columbuss-geographical-miscalculations}}</ref></noinclude> The myth that Columbus proved the Earth was round was propagated by authors like [[Washington Irving]] in ''[[A History of the Life and Voyages of Christopher Columbus]]''.<noinclude><ref name="washingtonpost.com" /></noinclude>
* Columbus was not the first European to visit the Americas:<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||url=https://books.google.com/books?id=YkLCiKN0x4UC&pg=PA90|title=Terra cognita: the mental discovery of America|author1=Eviatar Zerubavel|date=2003|publisher=Transaction Publishers|isbn=978-0-7658-0987-2|pages=90–91|access-date=September 21, 2016|archive-date=January 21, 2023|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230121034111/https://books.google.com/books?id=YkLCiKN0x4UC&pg=PA90|url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Sale|first=Kirkpatrick|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=AUd0QgAACAAJ|title=The Conquest of Paradise: Christopher Columbus and the Columbian Legacy|year=1991|pages=204–09|publisher=Plume|isbn=978-1-84511-154-0|via=Google Books}}</ref></noinclude> [[Leif Erikson]], and possibly other Vikings before him, explored [[Vinland]], an area of coastal North America. Ruins at [[L'Anse aux Meadows]] prove that at least one Norse settlement was built in Newfoundland, confirming a story in the ''[[Saga of Erik the Red]]''.

==== Vikings ====
* There is no evidence that Viking warriors wore [[Horned helmet|horns on their helmets]]; this would have been impractical in battle.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||title=World History: Societies of the Past|last=Kahn|first=Charles|date=2005|publisher=Portage & Main Press|isbn=978-1-55379-045-7|page=9|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=gEXCIH4tek8C}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Viking helmets|publisher=National Museum of Denmark|quote= In a battle situation, horns on a helmet would get in the way. |url=https://en.natmus.dk/historical-knowledge/denmark/prehistoric-period-until-1050-ad/the-viking-age/weapons/helmets/}}</ref></noinclude>
* Vikings did not [[Vikings#Use of skulls as drinking vessels|drink out of the skulls of vanquished enemies]]. This was based on a mistranslation of the [[skald]]ic poetic use of ''ór bjúgviðum hausa'' (branches of skulls) to refer to [[drinking horn]]s.<noinclude><ref>E. W. Gordon, ''Introduction to Old Norse'' (2nd edition, Oxford 1962) pp. lxix–lxx.</ref></noinclude>
* Vikings did not [[Names of Iceland|name Iceland]] "Iceland" as a ploy to discourage oversettlement. According to the [[Sagas of Icelanders]], [[Hrafna-Flóki Vilgerðarson]] saw icebergs on the island when he traveled there, and named the island after them.<noinclude><ref>{{cite magazine |last=Evans |first=Andrew |url=https://news.nationalgeographic.com/2016/06/iceland-greenland-name-swap/ |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20160630143403/http://news.nationalgeographic.com/2016/06/iceland-greenland-name-swap/ |archive-date=June 30, 2016 |title=Is Iceland Really Green and Greenland Really Icy? |magazine=[[National Geographic]] |date=June 2016}}</ref></noinclude> Popular legend holds that Greenland was named in the hopes of attracting settlers.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||url=http://www.gutenberg.org/files/17946/17946-h/17946-h.htm |title=Eirik the Red's Saga |publisher=Gutenberg.org |date=March 8, 2006 |access-date=September 6, 2010 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110511061916/http://www.gutenberg.org/files/17946/17946-h/17946-h.htm |archive-date=May 11, 2011 |url-status=live }}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://ancientstandard.com/2010/12/17/how-greenland-got-its-name|title=How Greenland Got Its Name|work=The Ancient Standard|date=December 17, 2010|access-date=January 24, 2020|archive-date=March 19, 2012|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120319050912/http://ancientstandard.com/2010/12/17/how-greenland-got-its-name/}}.
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite journal||last=Grove|first=Jonathan|year=2009|url=https://cambridge.academia.edu/JonathanGrove/Papers/397439/The_Place_of_Greenland_In_Medieval_Icelandic_Saga_Narrative|title=The place of Greenland in medieval Icelandic saga narrative|journal=Journal of the North Atlantic|volume=2|pages=30–51|doi=10.3721/037.002.s206|s2cid=163032041|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120411212816/http://cambridge.academia.edu/JonathanGrove/Papers/397439/The_Place_of_Greenland_In_Medieval_Icelandic_Saga_Narrative|archive-date=April 11, 2012}}</ref></noinclude>

=== Early modern ===
{{further|Black legend}}
