* The [[Mexica]] people of the [[Aztec Empire]] did not mistake [[Hernán Cortés]] and his landing party for gods during Cortés' [[Spanish conquest of the Aztec Empire|conquest of the empire]]. This notion came from [[Francisco López de Gómara]], who never went to Mexico and concocted the myth while working for the retired Cortés in Spain years after the conquest.<noinclude><ref>Wills, Matthew (January 17, 2020). [https://daily.jstor.org/the-mexica-didnt-believe-the-conquistadors-were-gods/ The Mexica Didn't Believe the Conquistadors Were Gods] {{Webarchive|url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230403223246/https://daily.jstor.org/the-mexica-didnt-believe-the-conquistadors-were-gods/ |date=April 3, 2023 }}. ''JSTOR''. Retrieved January 19, 2020.</ref></noinclude>
* The elite of the [[Dutch Golden Age]] wore black clothes primarily as a status symbol rather than out of [[Puritans|Puritan]] self-restraint. The clothes attracted status from the difficulty of the dyeing process and the cost of elaborate embellishments.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Pound |first=Cath |date=March 14, 2018 |title=When the Old Masters Were the P.R. Agents of the Rich and Powerful |url=https://www.nytimes.com/2018/03/14/arts/high-society-rijksmuseum.html |url-status=live |archive-url=https://archive.today/20180314203736/https://www.nytimes.com/2018/03/14/arts/high-society-rijksmuseum.html |archive-date=March 14, 2018 |access-date=June 26, 2024 |work=[[The New York Times]]}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Higgins |first=Charlotte |date=June 22, 2007 |title=The old black |url=https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2007/jun/22/art.fashion |access-date=June 26, 2024 |work=[[The Guardian]]}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Shah Jahan]], the Indian [[Mughal emperors|Mughal Emperor]] who commissioned the [[Taj Mahal]], did not cut off the hands of the rumored 40,000 workers or lead designers so as to not allow the construction of another monument more beautiful than the Taj Mahal. This is an urban myth that goes back to the 1960s.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Mehta|first=Archit|date=2021-12-24|title=Fact-check: Did Shah Jahan chop off the hands of Taj Mahal workers?|url=https://www.altnews.in/fact-check-did-shah-jahan-chop-off-the-hands-of-taj-mahal-workers/|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20220812075912/https://www.altnews.in/fact-check-did-shah-jahan-chop-off-the-hands-of-taj-mahal-workers/|archive-date=12 August 2022|access-date=2024-01-24|website=Alt News}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Beg|first=M. Saleem|date=2022-03-08|title=Debunking an urban myth about Taj Mahal|url=https://www.thehindu.com/opinion/op-ed/debunking-an-urban-myth-about-taj-mahal/article65205195.ece|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230709230812/https://www.thehindu.com/opinion/op-ed/debunking-an-urban-myth-about-taj-mahal/article65205195.ece|archive-date=9 July 2023|access-date=2024-01-24|work=The Hindu|language=en-IN|issn=0971-751X}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=S. Sharma|first=Manimugdha|date=2017-10-22|title=Busting the Taj fake news|url=https://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/home/sunday-times/busting-the-taj-fake-news/articleshow/61166015.cms|url-status=live|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20231004054041/https://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/home/sunday-times/busting-the-taj-fake-news/articleshow/61166015.cms|archive-date=4 October 2023|access-date=2024-01-24|work=The Times of India|issn=0971-8257}}</ref></noinclude>
* The story that [[Isaac Newton]] was inspired to research the nature of gravity when an [[Isaac Newton#Apple incident|apple fell on his head]] is almost certainly apocryphal. All Newton himself ever said was that the idea came to him as he sat "in a contemplative mood" and "was occasioned by the fall of an apple".<noinclude><ref name="Newton's apple: The real story">{{#invoke:cite journal||title=Newton's apple: The real story|date=January 18, 2010 |journal=New Scientist |url=https://www.newscientist.com/blogs/culturelab/2010/01/newtons-apple-the-real-story.php|archive-url=https://archive.today/20100121073908/http://www.newscientist.com/blogs/culturelab/2010/01/newtons-apple-the-real-story.php|archive-date=January 21, 2010|access-date=May 10, 2010}}</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Marie-Antoinette, 1775 - Musée Antoine Lécuyer.jpg|thumb|upright|alt=Portrait of Marie Antoinette|The phrase "[[let them eat cake]]" is misattributed to [[Marie Antoinette]].]]
* [[Marie Antoinette]] did not say "[[let them eat cake]]" when she heard that the French peasantry were starving due to a shortage of bread. The phrase was first published in [[Jean-Jacques Rousseau|Rousseau]]'s ''[[Confessions (Rousseau)|Confessions]]'', written when Marie Antoinette was only nine years old and not attributed to her, just to "a great princess". It was first attributed to her in 1843.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://history.howstuffworks.com/european-history/top-5-marie-antoinette-scandals1.htm|title=Top 5 Marie Antoinette Scandals|publisher=[[HowStuffWorks|history.howstuffworks.com]]|access-date=January 13, 2023|archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20120904040526/http://history.howstuffworks.com/european-history/top-5-marie-antoinette-scandals1.htm|archive-date=September 4, 2012|url-status=live|date=September 2, 2008}}</ref></noinclude>

==== North America ====
* The early settlers (commonly known as [[Pilgrim Fathers|Pilgrims]]) of the [[Plymouth Colony]] in North America usually did not wear all black, and their [[capotain]]s (hats) did not include buckles. Instead, their fashion was based on that of the late Elizabethan era.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://web.ccsd.k12.wy.us/techcurr/social%20studies/05/0101pilcloth.html|title=Plymouth Colony Clothing|publisher=Web.ccsd.k12.wy.us|access-date=February 9, 2012|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131022032249/http://web.ccsd.k12.wy.us/techcurr/social%20studies/05/0101pilcloth.html|archive-date=October 22, 2013}}</ref></noinclude> The traditional image was formed in the 19th century when buckles were a kind of emblem of quaintness.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Schenone|first=Laura|author-link=Laura Schenone|title=A Thousand Years Over A Hot Stove: A History Of American Women Told Through Food, Recipes, And Remembrances|location=New York|publisher=W. W. Norton & Company|year=2004|page=118|isbn=978-0-393-32627-7}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Wilson|first=Susan|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=VwlpQwAACAAJ|title=Literary Trail of Greater Boston|location=Boston|publisher=Houghton Mifflin Company|year=2000|page=23|isbn=978-0-618-05013-0|via=Google Books|access-date=April 18, 2021|archive-date=May 23, 2024|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240523022412/https://books.google.com.et/books?id=VwlpQwAACAAJ&redir_esc=y|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> (The [[Puritan]]s, who settled in the adjacent [[Massachusetts Bay Colony]] shortly after the Pilgrims arrived in Plymouth, ''did'' frequently wear all black.)<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last1=Brooks|first1=Rebecca Beatrice|title=What Did the Pilgrims Wear?|url=https://historyofmassachusetts.org/what-did-pilgrims-wear/|website=History of Massachusetts Blog|publisher=Rebecca Beatrice Brooks|access-date=November 10, 2019|date=July 22, 2018|archive-date=November 10, 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20191110011232/https://historyofmassachusetts.org/what-did-pilgrims-wear/|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* People accused of witchcraft were not burned at the stake during the [[Salem witch trials]]. Of the accused, nineteen people convicted of witchcraft were executed by hanging, at least five died in prison, and [[Giles Corey|one man]] was pressed to death by stones while trying to extract a confession from him.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Rosenthal|first=Bernard|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=YNYyMV_huGAC&pg=PA209|title=Salem Story: Reading the Witch Trials of 1692|publisher=[[Cambridge University Press]]|year=1995|isbn=978-0-521-55820-4|page=209|access-date=September 21, 2016|archive-date=January 21, 2023|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230121034111/https://books.google.com/books?id=YNYyMV_huGAC&pg=PA209|url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Adams|first=Gretchen|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=wMIF3EFgnRsC&pg=PR22|title=The Specter of Salem: Remembering the Witch Trials in Nineteenth-Century America|publisher=ReadHowYouWant.com|year=2010|isbn=978-1-4596-0582-4|page=xxii|via=[[Google Books]]|access-date=September 21, 2016|archive-date=January 21, 2023|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230121034111/https://books.google.com/books?id=wMIF3EFgnRsC&pg=PR22|url-status=live}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Kruse|first=Colton|date=March 22, 2018|title=Salem Never Burned Any Witches At The Stake|url=https://www.ripleys.com/weird-news/or-not-salem-witch-trials/|access-date=July 18, 2022|website=Ripley's Believe It or Not!|language=en-US|archive-date=December 3, 2022|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20221203085349/https://www.ripleys.com/weird-news/or-not-salem-witch-trials/|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[George Washington]] did not have [[George Washington's teeth|wooden teeth]]. His dentures were made of lead, gold, hippopotamus ivory, the teeth of various animals, including horse and donkey teeth,<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.nbcnews.com/id/6875436 |title=Washington's False Teeth Not Wooden |work=NBC News |date=January 27, 2005 |access-date=August 29, 2009 |archive-date=February 11, 2013 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130211015649/http://www.nbcnews.com/id/6875436 |url-status=live }}</ref><ref name="TeethMyth">{{#invoke:cite web||last=Etter |first=William M. |title=George Washington's Teeth Myth |url=https://www.mountvernon.org/library/digitalhistory/digital-encyclopedia/article/wooden-teeth-myth/ |access-date=24 March 2019 |website=www.mountvernon.org |publisher=Mount Vernon Ladies' Association |archive-date=January 10, 2024 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240110063639/https://www.mountvernon.org/library/digitalhistory/digital-encyclopedia/article/wooden-teeth-myth/ |url-status=live }}</ref></noinclude> and human teeth, possibly bought from slaves or poor people.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last1=Thompson|first1=Mary V.|title=The Private Life of George Washington's Slaves|website=[[PBS]]|url=https://www.pbs.org/wgbh/pages/frontline/shows/jefferson/video/lives.html|access-date=June 16, 2014|archive-date=June 25, 2014|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140625025145/http://www.pbs.org/wgbh/pages/frontline/shows/jefferson/video/lives.html|url-status=live}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Teeth |url=https://www.mountvernon.org/george-washington/health/teeth/ |access-date=2022-12-02 |website=George Washington's Mount Vernon |language=en |archive-date=December 5, 2023 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20231205224433/https://www.mountvernon.org/george-washington/health/teeth/ |url-status=live }}</ref></noinclude> Because ivory teeth quickly became stained, they may have had the appearance of wood to observers.<noinclude><ref name="TeethMyth" /></noinclude>
[[File:Washington Teeth.jpg|thumb|right|[[George Washington's teeth|George Washington's dentures]]]]
* The signing of the [[United States Declaration of Independence]] did not occur on July 4, 1776. After the [[Second Continental Congress]] voted to declare independence on July 2, the final language of the document was approved on July 4, and it was printed and distributed on July 4–5.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Declaration of Independence – A History |url=https://www.archives.gov/exhibits/charters/declaration_history.html |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20100126014257/http://www.archives.gov/exhibits/charters/declaration_history.html |archive-date=January 26, 2010 |access-date=April 4, 2011 |work=archives.gov |publisher=U.S. National Archives and Records Administration}}</ref></noinclude> However, the actual signing occurred on August 2, 1776.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.gallup.com/poll/3742/new-poll-gauges-americans-general-knowledge-levels.aspx|date=July 6, 1999|title=New Poll Gauges Americans' General Knowledge Levels|first=Steve|last=Crabtree|publisher=Gallup News Service|access-date=January 13, 2011|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140327130003/http://www.gallup.com/poll/3742/New-Poll-Gauges-Americans-General-Knowledge-Levels.aspx|archive-date=March 27, 2014|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Benjamin Franklin]] did not propose that the [[wild turkey#Benjamin Franklin and the myth of U.S. national bird suggestion|wild turkey]] be used as the symbol for the United States instead of the bald eagle. While he did serve on a commission that tried to design a seal after the Declaration of Independence, his proposal was an image of Moses. His objections to the eagle as a national symbol and preference for the turkey were stated in a 1784 letter to his daughter in response to the [[Society of the Cincinnati]]'s use of the former; he never expressed that sentiment publicly.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Lund|first=Nicholas|title=Did Benjamin Franklin ''Really'' Say the National Symbol Should Be the Turkey?|url=http://www.slate.com/articles/health_and_science/holidays/2013/11/benjamin_franklin_turkey_symbol_why_he_hated_the_bald_eagle_for_the_great.html|work=[[Slate (website)|Slate]]|date=November 21, 2013|access-date=November 22, 2013|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140427083733/http://www.slate.com/articles/health_and_science/holidays/2013/11/benjamin_franklin_turkey_symbol_why_he_hated_the_bald_eagle_for_the_great.html|archive-date = April 27, 2014|url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||last=McMillan|first=Joseph|url=http://americanheraldry.org/pages/index.php?n=MMM.Turkey|title=The Arms of the United States: Benjamin Franklin and the Turkey|publisher=American Heraldry Society|date=May 18, 2007|access-date=November 24, 2013|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140427082418/http://www.americanheraldry.org/pages/index.php?n=MMM.Turkey|archive-date=April 27, 2014}}</ref></noinclude>
* There was never a bill to make German the [[official language of the United States]] that was defeated by one vote in the House of Representatives, nor has one been proposed at the state level. In 1794, a petition from a group of German immigrants was put aside on a procedural vote of 42 to 41, that would have had the government publish some laws in German. This was the basis of the [[Muhlenberg legend]], named after the Speaker of the House at the time, [[Frederick Muhlenberg]], who was of German descent and abstained from this vote.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||title=Der Dativ ist dem Genetiv sein Tod|author-link=Bastian Sick|first=Bastian|last=Sick|publisher=[[Kiepenheuer & Witsch]]|date=2004|isbn=978-3-462-03448-6|url=https://archive.org/details/derdativistdemge00sick|language=german|pages=131–135|via=[[Internet Archive]]|url-access=registration}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.ulib.iupui.edu/kade/adams/chap7.html|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20100624133002/http://www.ulib.iupui.edu/kade/adams/chap7.html|title=Willi Paul Adams: ''The German Americans.'' Chapter 7: ''German or English''|archive-date=June 24, 2010}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.snopes.com/language/apocryph/german.asp|date=July 9, 2007|title=The German Vote|website=[[Snopes]]|access-date=August 31, 2013|archive-date=December 16, 2008|archive-url=https://wayback.archive-it.org/all/20081216214909/http://www.snopes.com/language/apocryph/german.asp|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>

=== Modern ===
[[File:Eastlake - Napoleon on the Bellerophon.jpg|thumb|right|upright|Napoleon was not especially short.]]
* [[Napoleon]] Bonaparte was not especially short for a Frenchman of his time. He was the height of an average French male in 1800, but short for an aristocrat or officer.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||author=Owen Connelly|title=Blundering to Glory: Napoleon's Military Campaigns|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=4Pzu7_QhfU8C&pg=PA7|year=2006|publisher=Rowman & Littlefield|page=7|isbn=978-0-7425-5318-7}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||title= Sorry, Wrong Answer: Trivia Questions That Even Know-It-Alls Get Wrong |author-link= Rod L. Evans |first= Rod L. |last= Evans |publisher= Penguin Books |date= 2010 |isbn= 978-0-399-53586-4 |access-date= December 31, 2011 |url= https://books.google.com/books?id=Q7CkHF7xTuYC&pg=PT116 |archive-date= January 21, 2023 |archive-url= https://web.archive.org/web/20230121034113/https://books.google.com/books?id=Q7CkHF7xTuYC&pg=PT116 |url-status= live }}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite news||date= February 11, 2009 |title= Forget Napoleon – Height Rules |work= CBS News |access-date= December 31, 2011 |url= http://www.cbsnews.com/stories/2003/10/17/health/main578654.shtml |archive-url= https://web.archive.org/web/20130702045955/http://www.cbsnews.com/stories/2003/10/17/health/main578654.shtml |archive-date= July 2, 2013 |url-status= live }}</ref></noinclude> After his death in 1821, the French emperor's height was recorded as 5&nbsp;feet 2&nbsp;inches in [[Mesures usuelles|''French'' feet]], which in English measurements is {{Convert|1.70|m|order=flip}}.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.napoleon.org/en/essential_napoleon/faq/index.asp#ancre54 |title=Fondation Napoléon |publisher=Napoleon.org|access-date=August 29, 2009|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140417162738/http://www.napoleon.org/en/essential_napoleon/faq/index.asp|archive-date = April 17, 2014| url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||title=La taille de Napoléon |url=http://www.napoleon.org/fr/salle_lecture/articles/files/Taillenapo_RIN_89_oct1963_2006.asp |language=fr|access-date=July 22, 2010|archive-url=https://www.webcitation.org/5jk3XhZKJ?url=http://www.napoleon.org/fr/salle_lecture/articles/files/Taillenapo_RIN_89_oct1963_2006.asp |archive-date=September 12, 2009 |url-status=live}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Napoleon's Imperial Guard |url=http://napoleonistyka.atspace.com/IMPERIAL_GUARD_infantry_1.htm |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140427082423/http://napoleonistyka.atspace.com/IMPERIAL_GUARD_infantry_1.htm |archive-date=April 27, 2014}}</ref></noinclude>
* The nose of the [[Great Sphinx of Giza]] was not shot off by Napoleon's troops during the [[French campaign in Egypt and Syria|French campaign in Egypt]] (1798–1801); it has been missing since at least the 10th century.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||title=The nose of the Great Sphinx |url=https://www.britannica.com/video/179518/investigation-Great-Sphinx-Egypt-Giza |access-date=2022-11-27 |website=[[britannica.com]] |language=en |archive-date=February 27, 2023 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230227192151/https://www.britannica.com/video/179518/investigation-Great-Sphinx-Egypt-Giza |url-status=live }}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Feder |first=Kenneth L. |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=RlRz2symkAsC&dq=nose+of+the+sphinx+shot+myth&pg=PA129 |title=Encyclopedia of Dubious Archaeology: From Atlantis to the Walam Olum: From Atlantis to the Walam Olum |date=2010-10-11 |publisher=ABC-CLIO |isbn=978-0-313-37919-2 |language=en |access-date=December 18, 2022 |archive-date=April 3, 2023 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230403224957/https://books.google.com/books?id=RlRz2symkAsC&dq=nose+of+the+sphinx+shot+myth&pg=PA129 |url-status=live }}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Zivie-Coche|first=Christiane|title=Sphinx: History of a Monument|publisher=[[Cornell University Press]]|year=2002|isbn=978-0-8014-3962-9|page=16}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Cinco de Mayo]] is not [[Grito de Dolores|Mexico's Independence Day]], but the celebration of the Mexican Army's victory over the French in the [[Battle of Puebla]] on May 5, 1862. Mexico's Declaration of Independence from Spain in 1810 is celebrated on September 16.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://news.nationalgeographic.com/news/2006/05/0505_060505_cinco_de_mayo.html |work=National Geographic News |title=Cinco de Mayo, From Mexican Fiesta to Popular U.S. Holiday |first=Stefan |last=Lovgren |date=May 5, 2006 |archive-url=https://www.webcitation.org/5QBwlWfYP?url=http://news.nationalgeographic.com/news/2006/05/0505_060505_cinco_de_mayo.html |archive-date=July 9, 2007 }}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://news.discovery.com/history/cinco-de-mayo-not-mexicos-independence-day.html|title=Cinco de Mayo: NOT Mexico's Independence Day |author=Lauren Effron |work=Discovery News |publisher=[[Discovery Channel]]|date=May 5, 2010|access-date=May 5, 2011|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120321055147/http://news.discovery.com/history/cinco-de-mayo-not-mexicos-independence-day.html |archive-date=March 21, 2012|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* Victorian-era doctors did not invent the [[Vibrator (sex toy)|vibrator]] to cure female "hysteria" by triggering orgasm.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Hysteria|date=August 12, 2015|publisher=Welcome Collection|url=https://wellcomecollection.org/articles/hysteria|access-date=September 25, 2018|archive-date=August 8, 2018|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180808171603/https://wellcomecollection.org/articles/hysteria|url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal||last=King|first=Helen|title=Galen and the widow. Towards a history of therapeutic masturbation in ancient gynaecology|journal=Eugesta, Journal of Gender Studies in Antiquity|year=2011|pages=227–31}}<br />c. {{cite magazine |title=Victorian-Era Orgasms and the Crisis of Peer Review |magazine=The Atlantic |url=https://www.theatlantic.com/health/archive/2018/09/victorian-vibrators-orgasms-doctors/569446/ |access-date=September 25, 2018 |date=September 6, 2018 |archive-date=September 20, 2018 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180920210318/https://www.theatlantic.com/health/archive/2018/09/victorian-vibrators-orgasms-doctors/569446/ |url-status=live}}
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Why the Movie "Hysteria" Gets Its Vibrator History Wrong|work=dildographer |url=https://dildographer.com/2012/05/04/why-the-movie-hysteria-gets-its-vibrator-history-wrong/|access-date=September 25, 2018|date=May 4, 2012|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190415173243/https://dildographer.com/2012/05/04/why-the-movie-hysteria-gets-its-vibrator-history-wrong/|archive-date=April 15, 2019}}
<br />e. {{#invoke:cite journal||last=King|first=Helen|title=Galen and the widow. Towards a history of therapeutic masturbation in ancient gynaecology|journal=Eugesta, Journal of Gender Studies in Antiquity|year= 2011|pages=206–08}}
<br />f. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Buzzkill: Vibrators and the Victorians (NSFW)|publisher=The Whores of Yore|url=https://www.thewhoresofyore.com/kates-journal/buzzkill-vibrators-and-the-victorians|access-date=September 25, 2018|archive-date=August 9, 2018|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20180809111703/https://www.thewhoresofyore.com/kates-journal/buzzkill-vibrators-and-the-victorians|url-status=live}}
<br />g. {{#invoke:cite news||title=No, no, no! Victorians didn't invent the vibrator|newspaper=The Guardian|url=https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2014/nov/10/victorians-invent-vibrator-orgasms-women-doctors-fantasy|access-date=September 25, 2018|date=November 10, 2014|last1=Riddell|first1=Fern|author-link1=Fern Riddell|archive-date=April 15, 2019|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20190415173536/https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2014/nov/10/victorians-invent-vibrator-orgasms-women-doctors-fantasy|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Albert Einstein as a child.jpg|thumb|[[Albert Einstein]], photographed at 14, did not fail mathematics at school.]]
* [[Albert Einstein]] did not fail mathematics classes in school. Einstein remarked, "I never failed in mathematics.... Before I was fifteen I had mastered differential and integral calculus."<noinclude><ref>a. {{cite magazine |author=Isaacson, Walter |date=April 5, 2007 |title=Making the Grade |url=https://content.time.com/time/specials/packages/article/0,28804,1936731_1936743_1936758,00.html |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140329164620/http://content.time.com/time/specials/packages/article/0%2C28804%2C1936731_1936743_1936758%2C00.html |archive-date=March 29, 2014 |access-date=December 31, 2013 |magazine=[[Time (magazine)|Time]]}}</ref></noinclude> Einstein did, however, fail his first entrance exam into the [[ETH Zurich|Swiss Federal Polytechnic School]] (ETH) in 1895, when he was two years younger than his fellow students, but scored exceedingly well in the mathematics and science sections, and then passed on his second attempt.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Kruszelnicki |first=Karl |title=Einstein Failed School |website=[[Australian Broadcasting Corporation]] |url=http://www.abc.net.au/science/articles/2004/06/23/1115185.htm?site=science/greatmomentsinscience |access-date=July 12, 2012 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140427082641/http://www.abc.net.au/science/articles/2004/06/23/1115185.htm?site=science%2Fgreatmomentsinscience |archive-date=April 27, 2014 |url-status=live |date=June 22, 2004 }}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Alfred Nobel]] did not omit mathematics in the [[Nobel Prize]] due to a rivalry with mathematician [[Gösta Mittag-Leffler]], as there is little evidence the two ever met, nor was it because Nobel's spouse had an affair with a mathematician, as Nobel was never married. The more likely explanation is that Nobel believed mathematics was too theoretical to benefit humankind, as well as his personal lack of interest in the field.<noinclude><ref name="López-Ortiz 1998">a. {{#invoke:cite web||last=López-Ortiz |first=Alex |date=February 20, 1998 |title=Why is there no Nobel in mathematics? |website=[[University of Waterloo]] |url=https://cs.uwaterloo.ca/~alopez-o/math-faq/mathtext/node21.html |access-date=June 28, 2022 |archive-date=February 17, 2023 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230217135338/https://cs.uwaterloo.ca/~alopez-o/math-faq/mathtext/node21.html |url-status=live }}<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Mikkelson |first=David |date=October 4, 2013 |title=No Nobel Prize for Math |website=[[Snopes]] |url=https://www.snopes.com/fact-check/the-prizes-rite/ |access-date=June 28, 2022 |archive-date=November 12, 2021 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20211112002131/https://www.snopes.com/fact-check/the-prizes-rite/ |url-status=live }}<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Firaque |first=Kabir |date=October 16, 2019 |title=Explained: Why is there no mathematics Nobel? The theories, the facts, the myths |url=https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/why-is-there-no-mathematics-nobel-the-theories-the-facts-the-myths-6071015/ |access-date=June 28, 2022 |website=[[The Indian Express]] |archive-date=March 24, 2023 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230324134705/https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/why-is-there-no-mathematics-nobel-the-theories-the-facts-the-myths-6071015/ |url-status=live }}</ref></noinclude> (See also: [[Nobel Prize controversies]])
* [[Grigori Rasputin]] was not assassinated by being fed cyanide-laced cakes and [[Madeira wine|wine]], shot multiple times, and then thrown into the [[Little Nevka]] river when he survived the former two. A contemporary autopsy reported that he was just killed with gunshots. A sensationalized account from the memoirs of co-conspirator [[House of Yusupov|Prince]] [[Felix Yusupov]] is the only source of this story.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||last=Harris |first=Carolyn |date=December 27, 2016 |title=The Murder of Rasputin, 100 Years Later |website=Smithsonian Magazine |url=https://www.smithsonianmag.com/history/murder-rasputin-100-years-later-180961572/ |access-date=October 16, 2023 |archive-date=October 15, 2023 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20231015210652/https://www.smithsonianmag.com/history/murder-rasputin-100-years-later-180961572/ |url-status=live }}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||date=December 31, 2016 |title=How was Russian mystic Rasputin murdered? |website=BBC |url=https://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-38469903 |access-date=October 16, 2023 |archive-date=October 31, 2023 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20231031131904/https://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-38469903 |url-status=live }}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||first=Douglas|last=Smith|chapter=A Cowardly Crime|publisher=Farrar, Straus and Giroux |title=Rasputin: Faith, Power, and the Twilight of the Romanovs |year=2016|isbn=978-0-374-71123-8|pages=590–592}}</ref></noinclude>
* The Italian dictator [[Benito Mussolini]] did not "make the trains run on time". Much of the repair work had been performed before he and the [[National Fascist Party|Fascist Party]] came to power in 1922. Moreover, the Italian railways' supposed adherence to timetables was more propaganda than reality.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://www.independent.co.uk/opinion/rear-window-making-italy-work-did-mussolini-really-get-the-trains-running-on-time-1367688.html|title=Rear Window: Making Italy work: Did Mussolini really get the trains running on time|access-date=September 3, 2010|work=The Independent|location=London|first=Brian |last=Cathcart|date=April 3, 1994|url-status=live|archive-date=January 24, 2012|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120124072305/http://www.independent.co.uk/opinion/rear-window-making-italy-work-did-mussolini-really-get-the-trains-running-on-time-1367688.html}}</ref></noinclude>
* There is no evidence of [[Polish cavalry]] mounting a brave but futile charge against German tanks using lances and sabers during the German [[invasion of Poland]] in 1939. This story may have originated from German propaganda efforts following the [[charge at Krojanty]].<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||last=Ankerstjerne|first=Christian|title=The myth of Polish cavalry charges |publisher=Panzerworld |url=http://www.panzerworld.net/fallweiss.html#polish-cavalry-charges-against-tanks |access-date=April 5, 2011|url-status=live|archive-date=August 4, 2012|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20120804070934/http://www.panzerworld.net/fallweiss.html}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.polamjournal.com/Library/APHistory/Cavalry_Myth/cavalry_myth.html |title=The Mythical Polish Cavalry Charge |work=Polish American Journal|publisher=Polamjournal.com |date=July 2008|access-date=February 9, 2012|archive-url=https://www.webcitation.org/6AvQeRgCM?url=http://www.polamjournal.com/Library/APHistory/Cavalry_Myth/cavalry_myth.html |archive-date=September 24, 2012 |url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Nazi Germany|Nazis]] did not use the term "Nazi" to refer to themselves. The full name of the [[Nazi Party]] was ''Nationalsozialistische Deutsche Arbeiterpartei'' (National Socialist German Workers' Party), and members referred to themselves as ''Nationalsozialisten'' (National Socialists) or ''Parteigenossen'' (party comrades). The term "Nazi" was in use prior to the rise of the Nazis as a colloquial and derogatory word for a backwards farmer or peasant. Opponents of the National Socialists abbreviated their name as "Nazi" for derogatory effect and the term was popularized by German exiles outside of Germany.<noinclude><ref>''Nazi.'' In: Friedrich Kluge, [[Elmar Seebold]]: ''Etymologisches Wörterbuch der deutschen Sprache.'' 24. Auflage, Walter de Gruyter, Berlin/New York 2002, {{ISBN|3-11-017473-1}} ([http://www.etymonline.com/index.php?term=Nazi Online Etymology Dictionary: ''Nazi''] {{Webarchive|url=https://web.archive.org/web/20141006134952/http://www.etymonline.com/index.php?term=Nazi |date=October 6, 2014 }}).</ref></noinclude>
* During the [[occupation of Denmark]] by the Nazis during World War II, King [[Christian X of Denmark]] did not thwart Nazi attempts to identify Jews by wearing a [[yellow badge|yellow star]] himself. Jews in Denmark were never forced to wear the [[Star of David]]. The [[Danish resistance movement|Danish resistance]] did [[Rescue of the Danish Jews|help most Jews flee the country]] before the end of the war.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.diis.dk/graphics/CVer/Personlige_CVer/Holocaust_and_Genocide/Publikationer/holocaust_DK_kap_5.pdf|title=The King and the Star – Myths created during the Occupation of Denmark|access-date=April 5, 2011 |author=Vilhjálmur Örn Vilhjálmsson|publisher=Danish institute for international studies|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110716022617/http://www.diis.dk/graphics/CVer/Personlige_CVer/Holocaust_and_Genocide/Publikationer/holocaust_DK_kap_5.pdf|archive-date=July 16, 2011}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||title=Some Essential Definitions & Myths Associated with the Holocaust |url=http://www.chgs.umn.edu/histories/myths.html|access-date=April 5, 2011|publisher=Center for Holocaust and Genocide Studies – University of Minnesota|archive-date=May 15, 2011|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110515002212/http://www.chgs.umn.edu/histories/myths.html}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite web||title=King Christian and the Star of David|url=http://www.natmus.dk/sw81068.asp|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110928011038/http://www.natmus.dk/sw81068.asp|archive-date=September 28, 2011|access-date=February 21, 2017|publisher=The National Museum of Denmark}}</ref></noinclude>
* Not all [[skinhead]]s are [[White power skinhead|white supremacists]]; many skinheads [[Redskin (subculture)|identify as left-wing]] or apolitical, and many oppose racism, such as the [[Skinheads Against Racial Prejudice]]. Originating from the 1960s British working class, many of its initial adherents were [[Black British people|black]] and [[British African-Caribbean people|West Indian]]; it became associated with white supremacy in the 1970s as a result of far-right groups like the [[National Front (UK)|National Front]] recruiting from the subculture for grassroot support.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Craig |first1=Laura |last2=Young |first2=Kevin |date=2008 |title=Beyond White Pride: Identity, Meaning and Contradiction in the Canadian Skinhead Subculture* |url=https://www.researchgate.net/publication/229688891 |journal=Canadian Review of Sociology/Revue Canadienne de Sociologie|pages=175–206 |volume=34 |number=2 |doi=10.1111/j.1755-618x.1997.tb00206.x |access-date=July 2, 2022}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Borgeson |first1=Kevin |last2=Valeri |first2=Robin |date=Fall 2005 |title=Examining Differences in Skinhead Ideology and Culture Through an Analysis of Skinhead Websites |journal=Michigan Sociological Review|pages=45–62 |volume=19 |jstor=40969104}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite news||last=Lambert |first=Chris |date=November 12, 2017 |title='Black Skinhead': The politics of New Kanye |website=Daily Dot |url=https://www.dailydot.com/upstream/kanye-west-black-skinhead/ |access-date=July 2, 2022 |quote="Skinhead" was a term originally used to describe a 1960s British working-class subculture that revolved around fashion and music and that would heavily inspire the punk rock scene. While it has harmless roots, the skinhead movement fell into polemic politics. Nowadays, it's popularly associated with neo-Nazis, despite having split demographics of far-right, far-left, and apolitical.}}</ref><ref name="sub-pop-pol">{{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Brown|first1=Timothy S.|title=Subcultures, Pop Music and Politics: Skinheads and "Nazi Rock" in England and Germany|journal=Journal of Social History|date=January 1, 2004|volume=38|issue=1|pages=157–178|jstor=3790031|doi=10.1353/jsh.2004.0079|s2cid=42029805}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Cotter|first=John M.|date=1999|title=Sounds of hate: White power rock and roll and the neo-nazi skinhead subculture|journal=Terrorism and Political Violence|volume=11|issue=2|pages=111–140|doi=10.1080/09546559908427509|issn=0954-6553}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Shaffer|first=Ryan|date=2013|title=The soundtrack of neo-fascism: youth and music in the National Front|journal=Patterns of Prejudice|volume=47|issue=4–5|pages=458–482|doi=10.1080/0031322X.2013.842289|s2cid=144461518|issn=0031-322X}}</ref></noinclude>

==== United States ====

[[File:Betsy Ross flag.svg|thumb|The flag that Betsy Ross purportedly designed]]
* [[Betsy Ross]] did not design or make the first official U.S. flag, despite it being widely known as the [[Betsy Ross flag]]. The claim was first made by her grandson a century later.<noinclude><ref>Marc Leepson, "[https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/five-myths-about-the-american-flag/2011/06/08/AG3ZSkOH_story.html Five myths about the American flag]" {{webarchive|url=https://web.archive.org/web/20170715155019/https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/five-myths-about-the-american-flag/2011/06/08/AG3ZSkOH_story.html |date=2017-07-15 }}, ''[[The Washington Post]]'', June 12, 2011, p. B2.</ref></noinclude>
[[File:Emancipation Proclamation.PNG|thumb|300px|[[Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution|The Thirteenth Amendment]] [[Abolitionism|abolished]] chattel slavery in the United States nationwide, not the Emancipation Proclamation (red areas only).]]
* [[Abraham Lincoln]] did not write his [[Gettysburg Address]] speech on the back of an envelope on his train ride to Gettysburg. The speech was substantially complete before Lincoln left Washington for Gettysburg.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://rmc.library.cornell.edu/lincoln/exhibition/gettysburg/|title=The Lincoln Presidency: Last Full Measure of Devotion|website=rmc.library.cornell.edu}}</ref><ref name="Green2005">{{#invoke:cite book||last=Green|first=Joey|title=Contrary to Popular Belief: More than 250 False Facts Revealed|publisher=Broadway|date=2005|isbn=978-0-7679-1992-0}}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Emancipation Proclamation]] did not free all slaves in the United States; the Proclamation applied in the ten states that were still in [[American Civil War|rebellion]] in 1863, and thus did not cover the nearly five hundred thousand slaves in the slaveholding [[Border states (American Civil War)|border states]]  that had not seceded.<noinclude><ref name="Stewart 2013" /><ref name="Berlin 1985" /><ref>{{#invoke:cite book|| last=Foner | first=Eric |author-link=Eric Foner | title=The fiery trial: Abraham Lincoln and American slavery |location=New York |publisher=W. W. Norton | date=2010 | isbn=978-0-393-06618-0 | oclc=601096674 | pages=241–242}}</ref></noinclude> (See also: [[Abolition of slavery timeline]])
* Likewise, the [[General Order No. 3|June 19, 1865 order]] celebrated annually as "[[Juneteenth]]" only applied ''in Texas'', not the United States at large. [[Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution|The Thirteenth Amendment]], ratified and proclaimed in December 1865, was the article that banned slavery nationwide except as punishment for a crime.<noinclude><ref name="Stewart 2013">{{cite news |last1=Stewart |first1=Alicia W. |title=150 years later, myths persist about the Emancipation Proclamation |url=https://www.cnn.com/2013/01/01/us/150-years-later-myths-persist-about-the-emancipation-proclamation/index.html |access-date=29 July 2024 |publisher=CNN |date=1 January 2013}}</ref><ref name="Berlin 1985">{{cite book |editor1-last=Berlin |editor1-first=Ira |editor2-last=Fields |editor2-first=Barbara J. |editor3-last=Glymph |editor3-first=Thavolia |editor4-last=Reidy |editor4-first=Joseph P. |editor5-last=Rowland |editor5-first=Leslie S. |title=Freedom: A Documentary History of Emancipation, 1861-1867: Series 1, Volume 1: The Destruction of Slavery |date=1985 |publisher=Cambridge University Press |isbn=978-0-521-22979-1 |page=69 |url=https://archive.org/details/destructionofsla0001unse/page/68/mode/1up?view=theater |url-access=registration}}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Alaska Purchase]] was generally viewed as positive or neutral in the United States, both among the public and the press. The opponents of the purchase who characterized it as "[[Seward's Folly]]", alluding to [[William H. Seward]], the Secretary of State who negotiated it, represented a minority opinion at the time.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite journal||last1=Haycox|first1=Stephen|title=Haycox, Stephen. "Truth and Expectation: Myth in Alaska History|journal=Northern Review|date=1990|volume=6|url=http://journals.sfu.ca/nr/index.php/nr/article/viewFile/397/390|access-date=August 31, 2015}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite journal||first=Richard E. Jr. |last=Welch |title=American Public Opinion and the Purchase of Russian America |journal=[[American Slavic and East European Review]] |year=1958 |volume=17 |issue=4 |pages=481–94 |jstor=3001132 |doi=10.2307/3001132 }}
<br />c. Howard I. Kushner, {{"'}}Seward's Folly'?: American Commerce in Russian America and the Alaska Purchase". ''California Historical Quarterly'' (1975): 4–26. {{JSTOR|25157541}}.
<br />d. {{#invoke:cite news||title=Biographer calls Seward's Folly a myth|url=http://www.thesewardphoenixlog.com/story/2014/04/03/features/biographer-calls-sewards-folly-a-myth/2492.html|access-date=August 31, 2015|agency=The Seward Phoenix LOG|date=April 3, 2014|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20170622084130/http://www.thesewardphoenixlog.com/story/2014/04/03/features/biographer-calls-sewards-folly-a-myth/2492.html|archive-date=June 22, 2017}}
<br />e. {{Cite video |url=https://www.c-span.org/video/?326990-1/founding-anchorage-alaska |title=Founding of Anchorage, Alaska |date=July 9, 2015 |type=Adobe Flash |access-date=December 22, 2017 |people=Professor Preston Jones (Featured Speaker) |publisher=[[C-SPAN]]}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Cook |first=Mary Alice |date=Spring 2011 |title=Manifest Opportunity: The Alaska Purchase as a Bridge Between United States Expansion and Imperialism |url=http://alaskahistoricalsociety.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/12/Article-on-purchase.pdf |journal=Alaska History |volume=26 |issue=1 |pages=1–10}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Cowboy hat]]s were not initially popular in the [[American frontier|Western American frontier]], with [[bowler hat|derby or bowler hats]] being the typical headgear of choice.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=The Hat That Won the West |url=https://news.google.com/newspapers?nid=336&dat=19571026&id=xQQpAAAAIBAJ&pg=7036,5636283 |access-date=February 10, 2010}}</ref></noinclude> Heavy marketing of the [[John B. Stetson Company|Stetson]] "[[Boss of the Plains]]" model in the years following the [[American Civil War]] was the primary driving force behind the cowboy hat's popularity, with its characteristic dented top not becoming standard until near the end of the 19th century.<noinclude><ref>Snyder, Jeffrey B. (1997) ''Stetson Hats and the John B. Stetson Company 1865–1970''. p. 50 {{ISBN|978-0-7643-0211-4}}</ref></noinclude>
* The [[Great Chicago Fire]] of 1871 was not caused by [[Catherine O'Leary|Mrs. O'Leary]]'s cow kicking over a lantern. A newspaper reporter later admitted to having invented the story to make colorful copy.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web|| title = The O'Leary Legend | website= Chicago History Museum | url = http://www.chicagohistory.org/fire/oleary/essay-2.html | access-date = March 18, 2007 | archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110110110402/http://www.chicagohistory.org/fire/oleary/essay-2.html | archive-date=January 10, 2011}}</ref></noinclude>
* There is no evidence that [[Frederic Remington]], on assignment to Cuba in 1897, telegraphed [[William Randolph Hearst]]: "There will be no war. I wish to return," nor that Hearst responded: "Please remain. You furnish the pictures, and I'll furnish the war". The anecdote was originally included in a book by [[James Creelman]] and probably never happened.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||last1=Campbell |first1=W. Joseph|title=Getting it Wrong: Ten of the Greatest Misreported Stories in American Journalism|url=https://archive.org/details/gettingitwrongte0000camp|url-access=registration |date=2010|publisher=[[University of California Press]]|location=Berkeley|isbn=978-0-520-26209-6|pages=[https://archive.org/details/gettingitwrongte0000camp/page/9 9]–25|via=[[Internet Archive]]}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Campbell|first=W. Joseph|year=2003|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=TflDlgzRtSYC&pg=PA72|title=Yellow Journalism: Puncturing the Myths, Defining the Legacies|publisher= [[Praeger Publishers|Praeger]]|page=72|isbn=978-0-275-98113-6}}</ref></noinclude>
* The electrocution of [[Topsy the Elephant]] was not an anti-alternating current demonstration organized by [[Thomas A. Edison]] during the [[war of the currents]]. Edison was never at [[Luna Park]], and the electrocution of Topsy took place ten years after the war of currents.<noinclude><ref name="edison.rutgers.edu">{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://edison.rutgers.edu/topsy.htm|title=Did Edison really electrocute Topsy the Elephant|website=The Edison Papers|via=[[Rutgers University]]|date=October 28, 2016|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20131113102308/http://edison.rutgers.edu/topsy.htm |archive-date=November 13, 2013 }}</ref></noinclude> This myth may stem from the fact that [[Electrocuting an Elephant|the recording of the event]] was produced by the [[Edison Studios|Edison film company]].
* [[Mary Mallon]], known as "[[wikt:Typhoid Mary|Typhoid Mary]]", testified at her 1909 trial that she did not believe she was contagious while an [[asymptomatic carrier]] of the [[bacteria]] ''[[Salmonella typhi]]''.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Foss |first=Katherine |date=April 24, 2020 |title=#TyphoidMary – now a hashtag – was a maligned immigrant who got a bum rap |url=https://theconversation.com/typhoidmary-now-a-hashtag-was-a-maligned-immigrant-who-got-a-bum-rap-136571 |work=[[The Conversation (website)|The Conversation]]}}</ref></noinclude> She later infected many others, while using fake names and evading health authorities.
* Immigrants' last names were not Americanized (voluntarily, mistakenly, or otherwise) upon arrival at [[Ellis Island]]. Officials there kept no records other than checking ship manifests created at the point of origin, and there was simply no paperwork that would have let them recast surnames, let alone any law. At the time in New York, anyone could change the spelling of their name simply by using that new spelling.<noinclude><ref name="Ellis Island">{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.nypl.org/blog/2013/07/02/name-changes-ellis-island |title=Why Your Family Name Was Not Changed at Ellis Island (and One That Was) |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20151208072225/http://www.nypl.org/blog/2013/07/02/name-changes-ellis-island |archive-date=December 8, 2015 |url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> These names are often referred to as an "[[Ellis Island Special]]".
* [[Prohibition in the United States|Prohibition]] did not make drinking alcohol illegal in the United States. The [[Eighteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution|Eighteenth Amendment]] and the subsequent [[Volstead Act]] prohibited the production, sale, and transport of "intoxicating liquors" within the United States, but their possession and consumption were never outlawed.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Prohibition {{!}} Definition, History, Eighteenth Amendment, & Repeal|url=https://www.britannica.com/event/Prohibition-United-States-history-1920-1933|access-date=2022-10-24|website=[[britannica.com]]|language=en}}</ref></noinclude>
* Distraught stockbrokers did not [[Suicide by jumping from height|jump to their deaths]] in large numbers after the [[Wall Street Crash of 1929]]. Although extensively reported by the news media, the phenomenon was limited in number and the overall suicide rate following the 1929 crash did not increase.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Market Crash Eexacts a Toll in Suicides|date=January 12, 2009 |url=https://macleans.ca/economy/business/market-crash-exacts-a-toll-in-suicides/|access-date=2024-08-24|language=en}}</ref></noinclude>
* There was no widespread outbreak of panic across the United States in response to [[Orson Welles]]' 1938 [[The War of the Worlds (1938 radio drama)|radio adaptation]] of [[H.G. Wells]]' ''[[The War of the Worlds (novel)|The War of the Worlds]]''. Only a very small share of the radio audience was listening to it, but newspapers, being [[The Press-Radio War|eager to discredit radio as a competitor for advertising]], played up isolated reports of incidents and increased emergency calls. Both Welles and CBS, which had initially reacted apologetically, later came to realize that the myth benefited them and actively embraced it in later years.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite news||last1=Pooley|first1=Jefferson|author2-link=Michael J. Socolow|last2=Socolow|first2=Michael|title=The Myth of the War of the Worlds Panic|url=http://www.slate.com/articles/arts/history/2013/10/orson_welles_war_of_the_worlds_panic_myth_the_infamous_radio_broadcast_did.html|newspaper=[[Slate (website)|Slate]]|date=October 28, 2013|access-date=November 24, 2013|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140509160518/http://www.slate.com/articles/arts/history/2013/10/orson_welles_war_of_the_worlds_panic_myth_the_infamous_radio_broadcast_did.html| archive-date=May 9, 2014| url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite book||last1 = Campbell|first1 = W. Joseph|title=Getting it wrong: ten of the greatest misreported stories in American Journalism|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=SodvJ4EuTggC|date=2010|publisher=[[University of California Press]]|location= Berkeley|isbn=978-0-520-26209-6| pages=26–44|via=Google Books}}</ref></noinclude>
* American pilot [[Kenneth Arnold]] did not coin the term ''[[flying saucer]]''; he did not use that phrase when describing his [[Kenneth Arnold UFO sighting|1947 UFO sighting]] at [[Mount Rainier]], [[Washington (state)|Washington]]. ''[[The East Oregonian]]'', the first newspaper to report on the incident, merely quoted him as saying the objects "flew like a saucer" and were "flat like a pie pan".<noinclude><ref name="UFOAtlantic">{{#invoke:cite news||last=Garber |first=Megan |date=June 15, 2014 |title=The Man Who Introduced the World to Flying Saucers |work=The Atlantic|url=https://www.theatlantic.com/technology/archive/2014/06/the-man-who-introduced-the-world-to-flying-saucers/372732/ |access-date=June 24, 2022}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Lacitis |first=Eric |date=June 24, 2017 |title='Flying saucers' became a thing 70 years ago Saturday with sighting near Mount Rainier |work=The Seattle Times|url=https://www.seattletimes.com/seattle-news/northwest/flying-saucers-became-a-thing-70-years-ago-saturday-with-sighting-near-mount-rainier/ |access-date=June 24, 2022}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite interview ||title=12:15 news |last=Arnold |first=Kenneth |date=June 26, 1947 |interviewer-last=Smith |interviewer-first=Ted |publisher=[[KTIX|KWRC]] |location=Pendleton, Oregon |type=Radio}}</ref><ref name="Meyer-2011">{{#invoke:cite news ||last1=Meyer |first1=Dave |title=64th anniversary of flying saucers at Mt. Rainier |url=https://www.knkx.org/other-news/2011-06-24/64th-anniversary-of-flying-saucers-at-mt-rainier |access-date=18 July 2024 |work=KNKX Public Radio |date=24 June 2011 |language=en |quote=Arnold described the shiny objects as 'something like a pie plate that was cut in half with a sort of a convex triangle in the rear' and that they flew 'like a saucer if you skipped it across the water.' The term 'flying saucer' made it into a newspaper headline and the rest, as they say, is history.}}</ref></noinclude>
* U.S. Senator [[George Smathers]] never gave a [[George Smathers#In popular culture|speech to a less-educated audience]] describing his opponent, [[Claude Pepper]], as an "extrovert" whose sister was a "thespian", in the apparent hope they would confuse them with similar-sounding words like "pervert" and "lesbian". Smathers offered US$10,000 to anyone who could prove he had made the speech; it was never claimed.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite news||url=http://www.time.com/time/magazine/article/0,9171,805369,00.html |magazine=Time |title=Florida: Anything Goes |date=April 17, 1950 |access-date=May 3, 2010 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130624133711/http://www.time.com/time/magazine/article/0%2C9171%2C805369%2C00.html |archive-date=June 24, 2013 }}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite news||last=Nohlgren|first=Stephen|title=A born winner, if not a native Floridian|url=http://www.sptimes.com/2003/11/29/State/A_born_winner__if_not.shtml|newspaper=[[St. Petersburg Times]]|date=November 29, 2003|access-date=October 8, 2011 | archive-url = https://web.archive.org/web/20121005062919/http://www.sptimes.com/2003/11/29/State/A_born_winner__if_not.shtml | archive-date = October 5, 2012| url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Dwight D. Eisenhower]] did not order the construction of the [[Interstate Highway System]] for the sole purpose of evacuating cities in the event of nuclear warfare. While military motivations were present, the primary motivations were civilian.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||title=Interstate Highway System - The Myths |url=https://highways.dot.gov/highway-history/interstate-system/50th-anniversary/interstate-highway-system-myths#question2 |url-status=live |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240429041854/https://highways.dot.gov/highway-history/interstate-system/50th-anniversary/interstate-highway-system-myths#question2 |archive-date=April 29, 2024 |access-date=June 24, 2024 |website=[[Federal Highway Administration]]}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Laskow |first=Sarah |date=August 24, 2015 |title=Eisenhower and History's Worst Cross-Country Road Trip |url=https://slate.com/human-interest/2015/08/in-1919-eisenhower-took-a-disastrous-road-trip-that-led-to-his-support-of-the-modern-paved-highway.html |access-date=June 24, 2024 |work=[[Slate (magazine)|Slate]]}}</ref></noinclude>
* [[Rosa Parks]] was not sitting in the front ("white") section of the bus during the event that made her famous and incited the [[Montgomery bus boycott]]. Rather, she was sitting in the front of the back ("colored") section of the bus, where African Americans were expected to sit, and rejected an order from the driver to vacate her seat in favor of a white passenger when the "white" section of the bus had become full.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://www.archives.gov/education/lessons/rosa-parks |title=An Act of Courage, The Arrest Records of Rosa Parks |date=August 15, 2015 |access-date=December 1, 2020 |website=National Archives}}</ref></noinclude>
* The African-American intellectual and activist [[W. E. B. Du Bois]] did not renounce his U.S. citizenship while living in Ghana shortly before [[W.E.B. Du Bois#Death in Africa|his death]].<noinclude><ref name="Dubois controversy book">{{#invoke:cite book||last1=Bass|first1=Amy|title=Those about Him Remained Silent: The Battle Over W.E.B. Du Bois|date=2009|publisher=[[University of Minnesota Press]]|isbn=978-0-8166-4495-7|page=155|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=wn7B_04lNJkC&pg=PA155}}</ref><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite news||title=Renouncing citizenship is usually all about the Benjamins, say experts|url=http://www.foxnews.com/politics/2012/05/11/renouncing-citizenship-is-usually-all-about-benjamins-say-experts/|newspaper=Fox News|date=May 11, 2012|access-date=May 18, 2015}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite news||title=Celebrities Who Renounced Their Citizenship|url=https://www.huffingtonpost.com/2013/02/01/celebrities-who-renounced-citizenship_n_2602099.html|newspaper=Huffington Post|date=February 1, 2012|access-date=May 18, 2015}}
<br />c. {{#invoke:cite book||last1=Aberjhani|first1=Sandra L. West|title=Encyclopedia of the Harlem Renaissance|date=2003|publisher=Infobase Publishing|isbn=978-1-4381-3017-0|page=89|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=XP48QWTmjyUC&pg=PA89}}</ref></noinclude> In early 1963, his membership in the [[Communist Party of the United States|Communist Party]] and support for the Soviet Union led the [[U.S. State Department]] to refuse to renew his [[United States passport|passport]] while he was already in Ghana. After leaving the embassy, he stated his intention to renounce his citizenship in protest, but while he took Ghanaian citizenship, he never actually renounced his American citizenship.<noinclude><ref name="Lewis Du Bois bio">{{#invoke:cite book||last1=Lewis|first1=David|title=W.E.B. Du Bois: A Biography|date=2009|publisher=MacMillan|isbn=978-0-8050-8805-2|page=841|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=BU4vH95YySgC&pg=PA841}}</ref><ref name="Dubois controversy book" /></noinclude>
* US President [[John F. Kennedy]]'s words "{{lang|de|[[Ich bin ein Berliner]]}}" are standard German for "I am a Berliner (citizen of Berlin)."<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Daum|first=Andreas W.|title=Kennedy in Berlin|publisher=Cambridge University Press|pages=148–49|date=2007|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=IrK1TG34vw8C&pg=PT148|isbn=978-3-506-71991-1|access-date=May 13, 2018|archive-date=January 21, 2023|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20230121034115/https://books.google.com/books?id=IrK1TG34vw8C&pg=PT148|url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.canoo.net/services/OnlineGrammar/Wort/Artikel/Gebrauch/ArtIndef.html|title=Gebrauch des unbestimmten Artikels (German, "Use of the indefinite article")|publisher=Canoo Engineering AG|access-date=July 5, 2010|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140328165614/http://canoo.net/services/OnlineGrammar/Wort/Artikel/Gebrauch/ArtIndef.html|archive-date=March 28, 2014|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude> It is not true that by using the indefinite article ''ein'', he changed the meaning of the sentence from the intended "I am a citizen of Berlin" to "I am a [[Berliner (doughnut)|Berliner]]", a Berliner being a type of German pastry, similar to a [[jelly doughnut]], amusing Germans.<noinclude><ref>a. {{#invoke:cite book||last=Ryan|first=Halford Ross|title=U.S. presidents as orators: a bio-critical sourcebook|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=Efqq9cYYqq0C&pg=PA220|year=1995|publisher=Greenwood|isbn=978-0-313-29059-6|pages=219–20|access-date=May 23, 2024|archive-date=May 23, 2024|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20240523022410/https://books.google.com/books?id=Efqq9cYYqq0C&pg=PA220|url-status=live}}
<br />b. {{#invoke:cite web||url= http://www1.stadtkind.com/item/135/ich-bin-ein-berliner-john-f-kennedy-west-berlin.htm|language= de|trans-title= I am a jelly doughnut. Or a Berliner?|title= Ich bin ein Pfannkuchen. Oder ein Berliner?|date= August 22, 2005|publisher= Stadtkind|access-date= June 26, 2013|archive-date= June 19, 2008|archive-url= https://web.archive.org/web/20080619060413/http://www1.stadtkind.com/item/135/ich-bin-ein-berliner-john-f-kennedy-west-berlin.htm}}</ref></noinclude> Furthermore, the pastry, which [[Berliner (doughnut)#Names|is known by many names]] in Germany, was not then — nor is it now — commonly called "Berliner" in the Berlin area.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://m.faz.net/aktuell/feuilleton/berliner-krapfen-pfannkuchen-wie-heisst-der-kreppel-wirklich-16068952.html|title=Essen und Sprechen Geben Sie mir ein Semmelbrötchen!|newspaper=Faz.net|date=March 5, 2019|last1=Rüther|first1=Tobias|access-date=May 23, 2024|archive-date=October 20, 2021|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20211020101500/https://m.faz.net/aktuell/feuilleton/berliner-krapfen-pfannkuchen-wie-heisst-der-kreppel-wirklich-16068952.html|url-status=live}}</ref></noinclude>
* When [[Murder of Kitty Genovese|Kitty Genovese was murdered]] outside her apartment in 1964, there were not 38 neighbors standing idly by and watching who failed to call the police until after she was dead, as was initially reported<noinclude><ref name="NYT Kitty Genovese story">{{#invoke:cite news||last=Gansberg |first=Martin |url=http://www.37themovie.com/images/about/NYTimes_KittyGenoveseArticle.pdf |title=37 Who Saw Murder Didn't Call the Police |newspaper=The New York Times |date=March 27, 1964 |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150807055841/http://www.37themovie.com/images/about/NYTimes_KittyGenoveseArticle.pdf |archive-date=August 7, 2015 }}</ref></noinclude> to widespread public outrage that persisted for years and even [[Bystander effect|became the basis of a theory in social psychology]]. In fact, witnesses only heard brief portions of the attack and did not realize what was occurring, and only six or seven actually saw anything. One witness, who had called the police, said when interviewed by officers at the scene, "I didn't want to get involved",<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||last=Bregman |first=Rutger |chapter-url=https://books.google.com/books?id=R7rEDwAAQBAJ&q=humankind+%2522Karl+Ross%2522&pg=PT179 |title=Humankind: A Hopeful History |publisher=Bloomsbury Publishing |year=2020 |isbn=978-1-4088-9896-3 |language=en |chapter=9}}</ref></noinclude> an attitude later attributed to all the neighbors.<noinclude><ref name="American Heritage Genovese article">{{#invoke:cite news||author= Rasenberger, Jim| url= http://www.americanheritage.com/content/nightmare-austin-street|title= Nightmare on Austin Street| work= [[American Heritage (magazine)|American Heritage]]| date= October 2006|access-date=May 18, 2015}}</ref></noinclude>

[[File:Pruitt-igoeUSGS02.jpg|right|thumb|The Wendell O. Pruitt Homes and William Igoe Apartments complex]]
* While it was praised by one architectural magazine before it was built as "the best high apartment of the year", the [[Pruitt–Igoe]] [[housing project]] in St. Louis, Missouri never won any awards for its design.<noinclude><ref name="AIA Pruitt Igoe page">{{#invoke:cite web||last=Cendón|first=Sara Fernández|title=Pruitt-Igoe 40 Years Later |url=http://www.aia.org/practicing/AIAB092656|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20140314062237/http://www.aia.org/practicing/AIAB092656|archive-date=March 14, 2014|publisher=[[American Institute of Architects]]|date=February 3, 2012|access-date=December 31, 2014|quote=For example, Pruitt-Igoe is often cited as an AIA-award recipient, but the project never won any architectural awards. }}</ref></noinclude> The architectural firm that designed the buildings did win an award for an earlier St. Louis project, which may have been confused with Pruitt–Igoe.<noinclude><ref name="Pruitt Igoe Myth">{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Bristol|first=Katharine|title=The Pruitt–Igoe Myth|journal=[[Journal of Architectural Education]]|date=May 1991|volume=44|issue=3|page=168|url=http://www.pruitt-igoe.com/temp/1991-bristol-pruitt-igoemyth.pdf|issn=1531-314X|doi=10.1111/j.1531-314X.2010.01093.x|s2cid=219542179|quote=Though it is commonly accorded the epithet 'award-winning,"' Pruitt-Igoe never won any kind of architectural prize. An earlier St. Louis housing project by the same team of architects, the John Cochran Garden Apartments, did win two architectural awards. At some point this prize seems to have been incorrectly attributed to Pruitt-Igoe|access-date=December 31, 2014|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20150924082652/http://www.pruitt-igoe.com/temp/1991-bristol-pruitt-igoemyth.pdf|archive-date=September 24, 2015}}</ref></noinclude>
* There is little contemporary documentary evidence for the notion that US Vietnam veterans were [[The Spitting Image|spat upon by anti-war protesters]] upon return to the United States. This belief was detailed in some biographical accounts and was later popularized by films such as ''[[Rambo (franchise)|Rambo]]''.<noinclude><ref>Jerry Lembcke, ''The Spitting Image: Myth, Memory and the Legacy of Vietnam'', 1998, {{isbn|978-0-8147-5147-3}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite book||first=Bob|last=Greene|author-link=Bob Greene|title=Homecoming: When the Soldier Returned from Vietnam|publisher=G. P. Putnam's Sons|year=1989|isbn=978-0-399-13386-2}}</ref><ref name="Were They Spat On?">{{#invoke:cite journal||last=Vlieg|first=Heather|title=Were They Spat On? Understanding The Homecoming Experience of Vietnam Veterans|journal=The Grand Valley Journal of History|date=September 2019|volume=7|issue=1 |url=https://scholarworks.gvsu.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1187&context=gvjh}}</ref></noinclude>
* Women [[Miss America protest|did not burn their bras]] outside the Miss America contest in 1969 as a protest in support of women's liberation. They did symbolically throw bras in a trash can, along with other articles seen as emblematic of women's position in American society such as mops, make-up, and high-heeled shoes. The myth of bra burning came when a journalist hypothetically suggested that women may do so in the future, as men of the era [[Draft-card burning|burned their draft cards]].<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||url=https://www.bbc.com/news/world-45303069|title=100 Women: The truth behind the 'bra-burning' feminists|work=BBC News|date=September 6, 2018}}</ref></noinclude>
* The American space program in the 1960s never had a wide base of [[Criticism of space exploration#Public opinion|public support]] and did not unify America. Belief that the [[Apollo program]] was worth the time and money invested peaked at 51% for a few months after the [[Apollo 11|1969 Apollo 11 moon landing]], and otherwise had fluctuated between 35–45% support.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Novak |first=Matt |date=May 15, 2012 |title=How Space-Age Nostalgia Hobbles Our Future |url=https://slate.com/technology/2012/05/space-program-s-future-and-landing-on-the-moon-how-nostalgia-for-the-apollo-program-doesn-t-help.html |access-date=June 26, 2024 |work=[[Slate (magazine)|Slate]]}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Saripalli |first=Srikanth |date=September 19, 2013 |title=To Boldly Go Nowhere, for Now |url=https://slate.com/technology/2013/09/manned-space-exploration-cyborgs-and-finding-the-next-earth.html |access-date=June 26, 2024 |work=[[Slate (magazine)|Slate]]}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Strauss |first=Mark |date=April 14, 2011 |title=Ten Enduring Myths About the U.S. Space Program |url=https://www.smithsonianmag.com/science-nature/ten-enduring-myths-about-the-us-space-program-1969206/ |access-date=June 26, 2024 |work=[[Smithsonian (magazine)|Smithsonian]]}}</ref></noinclude>
* Despite popularizing the phrase "[[drinking the Kool-Aid]]",<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=https://jonestown.sdsu.edu/?page_id=93983|title=Kool Aid/Flavor Aid: Inaccuracies vs. Facts Part 7|website=Alternative Considerations of Jonestown & Peoples Temple|access-date=June 20, 2022|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20200713202524/https://jonestown.sdsu.edu/?page_id=93983|archive-date=July 13, 2020}}</ref></noinclude> Kool-Aid was not used for the [[potassium cyanide]]-fruit punch mix ingested as part of the [[Jonestown massacre]].<noinclude><ref name="Atlantickoolaid">{{#invoke:cite news||last=Higgins|first=Chris|title=Stop Saying 'Drink the Kool-Aid'|url=https://www.theatlantic.com/health/archive/2012/11/stop-saying-drink-the-kool-aid/264957/|website=The Atlantic|date=November 8, 2012|access-date=June 20, 2022}}</ref></noinclude> A similar product, [[Flavor-Aid]], was used.<noinclude><ref>{{#invoke:cite news||last=Krause|first=Charles A.|title=Jonestown Is an Eerie Ghost Town Now|url=https://www.washingtonpost.com/archive/politics/1978/12/17/jonestown-is-an-eerie-ghost-town-now/3baa4355-58ea-41c0-ae04-a962b3e34651/|newspaper=Washington Post|date=December 17, 1978|access-date=June 20, 2022|quote=A pair of woman's eyelasses, a towel, a pair of shorts, packets of unopened Flavor-Aid lie scattered about waiting for the final cleanup that may one day return Jonestown to the tidy, if overcrowded, little community it once was.}}</ref><ref>{{#invoke:cite web||url=http://www.fastcompany.com/magazine/92/debunk.html |title=Don't Drink the Grape-Flavored Sugar Water... |access-date=June 20, 2022 |author=Kihn, Martin|date=March 2005 |publisher=Fast Company|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20050407051630/http://www.fastcompany.com/magazine/92/debunk.html|archive-date=April 7, 2005}}</ref></noinclude>
